<a id="paper-2605.15785"></a>
### ⭐ [Dynamical aspects of steady-state subradiance: Detailed balance and its breakdown](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.15785v1)

**Highlighted author(s):** Simon B. Jäger, Murray J. Holland  
**Authors:** Athreya Shankar, Simon B. Jäger, Jarrod T. Reilly, Raphael Kaubruegger, Murray J. Holland, Walter Hahn  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** statistical mechanics · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.15785v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** 🔥 `driven-dissipative phase transition` **4/5** · 🔥 `superradiant laser` **4/5** · `Dicke superradiance` **3/5** · `Tavis-Cummings & cavity-many-emitter` **3/5** · `correlated / nonlocal dissipation` **3/5** · `methods for driven-dissipative` **3/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **3/5** · `non-equilibrium universality` **3/5** · `correlated cavity matter` **2/5** · `Keldysh / 2PI / non-Gaussian methods` **1/5** · `analog quantum simulation` **1/5** · `entanglement & information structure` **1/5**

<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 4.</b> FIG. 4. Internal entropy production rate (normalized to sys- tem size) as a function of repump for different system sizes. Here, w = γ = 0.1Γc is the critical point indicated by the vertical line.</figcaption>

<img src="2605.15785_figures/2605.15785_fig5.jpg" alt="Figure 5">

</figure>
</div>

**Summary.** This work analyzes the dynamics of a bad-cavity laser, showing that a phase transition corresponds precisely to the breakdown of detailed balance in the system's underlying Markov chain. When detailed balance breaks down, the system exhibits non-zero internal entropy production and observable self-pulsing in the cavity light output.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides a concrete, physical realization (bad-cavity laser) where fundamental concepts from non-equilibrium statistical mechanics, such as detailed balance and entropy production, can be directly observed and quantified through quantum dynamics.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper investigates the connection between a dissipative phase transition in the subradiant regime of a bad-cavity laser and the breakdown of detailed balance in the underlying Markov chain.

**Main result.** The system exhibits a transition where the dynamics switch from time-reversible (satisfying detailed balance) to time-irreversible (breaking detailed balance) as a parameter is varied.

**Method.** The quantum master equation is mapped onto a classical Markov chain description, allowing the analysis of steady-state probability currents and entropy production rates.

**Model / system.** The system is a bad-cavity laser involving N incoherently pumped atomic dipoles coupled to a heavily damped cavity mode. The dynamics are analyzed using collective angular momentum states and reduced to a rate equation for probabilities.

**Key observables.** Internal entropy production rate ($\dot{S}_i$), net probability currents, and the intensity correlation function $g_2(	au)$ (detecting self-pulsing).

**Important parameters / regimes.** The ratio of the repump rate ($w$) to the free-space decay rate ($\gamma$) defines the critical point ($w = \gamma$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis relies on the bad-cavity limit ($\kappa$ large) and mapping the quantum dynamics onto a classical Markov chain description.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the population flow and current loops associated with time-reversal symmetry breaking, and plot the entropy production rate ($\dot{s}$) showing a sharp change at the critical point $w = \gamma$.

**Paper structure.** The paper first establishes the Markov chain mapping and then analyzes two distinct regimes based on the ratio $w/\gamma$: one satisfying detailed balance and the other exhibiting time-asymmetric currents and entropy production.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

The dynamics of dissipative many-body quantum systems sometimes admit an emergent classical description in terms of a Markov chain even though the corresponding state space contains highly entangled states. In particular, a bad-cavity laser is a paradigm system whose dynamics can be formulated as a Markov chain in a two-dimensional state space spanned by collective angular momentum states. In this article, we investigate the connection between a dissipative phase transition that occurs in the subradiant regime of this system in the large atom number limit, and the properties of the underlying Markov chain. In one of the phases, the Markov chain approaches the detailed-balance condition with increasing atom number $N$ and hence becomes effectively time-reversible. This is caused by a collective atomic emission process that effectively reduces the Markov chain to one dimension. In the other phase, we find the emergence of time-asymmetric probability currents in the two-dimensional state space that signals a breakdown of detailed balance. This is accompanied by a macroscopic internal entropy production rate in the Markov chain that scales extensively with the atom number $N$. An observable consequence of the probability currents is a self-pulsing of the cavity light output in this phase, which can be detected as an anti-correlation dip in the intensity correlation function.

</details>

### ⭐ [Driven two-level systems as a minimal resource for remote entanglement stabilization](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.15989v1)

**Highlighted author(s):** Peter Rabl

**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `correlated / nonlocal dissipation` **3/5** · `entanglement & information structure` **3/5** · `methods for driven-dissipative` **3/5** · `Keldysh / 2PI / non-Gaussian methods` **2/5** · `Tavis-Cummings & cavity-many-emitter` **2/5** · `correlated cavity matter` **2/5** · `driven-dissipative phase transition` **2/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **2/5** · `analog quantum simulation` **1/5** · `interference shaping light` **1/5** · `non-equilibrium universality` **1/5**

(b) In the resolved-Purcell regime, ˜Ω≫κ ≫g, two cavities can be adiabatically eliminated to obtain an effective model for the TLS, with jump operators O1 = ˜τ −, O2 = ˜τ + and decay rates ˜κ1 and ˜κ2. (c) In the opposite regime, where Γ0 ≫g, κ, one can adiabatically eliminate the TLS, and model the re- maining dynamics of the two cavities by a quadratic TMS Lindbladian. (d) Sketch of the relevant frequencies and...</figcaption>
</figure>
</div>

**Summary.** This paper develops a theoretical framework to quantify how entanglement can be remotely stabilized between two qubits using a correlated photon source derived from a driven two-level system. The authors demonstrate that while the basic setup yields some entanglement, optimizing the system by adding filter cavities is crucial to approach the theoretical maximum. This has direct implications for building quantum networks using solid-state defects.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work provides a detailed theoretical roadmap for engineering entanglement distribution in solid-state quantum networks, showing how cavity engineering can overcome intrinsic limitations of simple sources.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper analyzes the autonomous stabilization of remote entanglement between two distant qubits using the correlated photon emission from a driven two-level system (TLS) source.

**Main result.** While a bare TLS shows some entanglement, achieving near-maximal entanglement requires coupling the TLS to additional filter cavities to enhance specific correlated emission events.

**Method.** The analysis develops a general framework using master equations and two-time correlation functions to quantify distributable entanglement, comparing different physical regimes (e.g., Purcell vs. Qubit-mediated squeezing).

**Model / system.** The system involves two target qubits coupled to a correlated photon source, which is modeled as a driven TLS interacting with optical cavities. The dynamics are governed by a master equation incorporating source, qubit, and interaction terms.

**Key observables.** Distributable entanglement (quantified by concurrence, $C_d$), effective two-mode squeezing parameters ($	ext{reff}, \mu_{	ext{eff}}$), and the steady-state density matrix.

**Important parameters / regimes.** TLS drive strength ($\Omega_0$), cavity decay rate ($\kappa$), qubit detunings ($\Delta_q$), and the ratio of decay rates ($\gamma/\kappa$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis relies on the Rotating-Wave Approximation (RWA) and various adiabatic elimination techniques depending on the coupling regime (e.g., Purcell or QMS).

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate schematics of the quantum network, plot the achievable concurrence ($C_d$) versus driving parameters ($\Omega_0, \Delta_0$), and show the dependence of entanglement on the ratio of decay rates ($\gamma/\kappa$).

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses from a general framework for entanglement quantification to specific applications using a bare TLS, then systematically explores enhancements by coupling the TLS to structured cavities, analyzing different physical regimes (Purcell, QMS) to maximize entanglement.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

We analyze the autonomous stabilization of remote entanglement by driving two distant qubits with the output of a correlated photon source. By treating the qubits as idealized entanglement detectors, we develop a general framework to quantify the maximum amount of entanglement that can be remotely stabilized in this way with a given photon source. We then apply this approach to evaluate the suitability of a single driven two-level system as a minimal resource for autonomous entanglement distribution schemes. While our analysis confirms the presence of distributable entanglement in the Mollow sidebands of a bare two-level system, we show that stabilizing close to maximally entangled states requires additional filter cavities that enhance the relevant correlated emission events compared to other processes. We identify optimized driving and cavity parameters and explain the achievable amount of entanglement in different regimes in terms of an effective two-mode squeezing model. These insights are particularly relevant for quantum networks based on photons or phonons in solid-state systems, where isolated spins, impurity centers, or other two-level defects are readily available, while alternative sources of correlated photons are difficult to realize.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

### [Extensive mixed-state entanglement in kinetically constrained superradiance](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.16131v1)

**Summary.** The paper demonstrates that imposing local kinetic constraints on an ensemble of quantum emitters undergoing superradiance generates robust, extensive mixed-state entanglement. This effect is fast, retaining the characteristic superradiant scaling, and can be realized in neutral-atom cavity QED setups. It offers a general method for the dissipative engineering of highly entangled quantum states.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work provides a concrete, experimentally viable framework for engineering quantum resources (entangled dark states) using collective quantum phenomena (superradiance) in solid-state or neutral-atom platforms, which is highly relevant to quantum simulation and quantum networking.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** To investigate how adding local kinetic constraints to Dicke superradiance affects entanglement generation, particularly in the mixed state of emitters.

**Main result.** Local kinetic constraints convert superradiance into a fast dissipative route that generates extensive mixed-state entanglement, while retaining the characteristic superradiant scaling ($\propto N^2$ intensity, $\propto (\log N)/N$ time).

**Method.** The analysis uses a combination of analytical derivations (e.g., lower bounds for emission rates) and numerical simulations of the Lindblad master equation, employing techniques like adiabatic elimination and quantum trajectory methods.

**Model / system.** The system is an ensemble of quantum emitters (modeled as Rydberg atoms) coupled to a single optical cavity mode, governed by a generalized Dicke model incorporating local Boolean kinetic constraints (AND, EAST, OR).

**Key observables.** Mixed-state entanglement (logarithmic negativity $E_N$), emission rate $\langle F^\dagger F angle$, and the adjacent pair count $\langle N_{	ext{adj}} angle$ used as an entanglement witness.

**Important parameters / regimes.** The analysis explores the bad-cavity regime, the ratio of coupling strengths to decay rates ($g/\omega_c$), and the impact of various noise sources like single-site and common-mode dephasing.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis often relies on the bad-cavity limit and the adiabatic elimination of intermediate states, though robustness checks against realistic Rydberg tails and dephasing are performed.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the superradiance dynamics, showing the characteristic scaling of the emission rate and entanglement. They also compare the entanglement measures ($E_N$, $\langle N_{	ext{adj}} angle$) under different constraints and noise conditions.

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses from establishing the failure of entanglement generation in standard Dicke superradiance to introducing local constraints. It then analyzes the resulting dynamics, proving the existence of entanglement via specific correlators, and finally testing the robustness of this effect against realistic imperfections like decay and noise.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Dicke superradiance by an ensemble of quantum emitters produces a collective burst of radiation, but no entanglement in the mixed state of the emitters. We show that adding a local kinetic constraint between the emitters generates extensive mixed-state entanglement, while otherwise preserving all key features of Dicke superradiance. Specifically, for any local Boolean constraint, we analytically derive a lower bound for the emission rate which implies a peak intensity $\propto N^2$ and a peak time $\propto (\log N)/N$ with number of spins $N$. This effect enables the superradiantly accelerated preparation of entangled dark states. Hereby, Hilbert-space fragmentation of the Dicke ladder leads to an exponentially branching decay tree that generates a hierarchy of dark states. Importantly, these disconnected manifolds include exponentially many long-range entangled singlet dark states. The explored kinetic constraints and superradiant dynamics can be realized in neutral-atom arrays coupled to an optical cavity, and we suggest a simple and accessible witness to detect the predicted mixed-state entanglement in such experiments. Moreover, we show that entanglement generation is robust against atomic decay and collective dephasing, and should be observable under recently reported experimental conditions. Our results, thereby, offer a general framework and experimentally viable approach for the dissipative engineering of entangled dark states enhanced by superradiance.

</details>

**Summary.** This theoretical work proposes a method to generate large, high-fidelity collective spin cat states in an atomic ensemble by leveraging the physics near the superradiant phase transition. The key technique involves performing a photon-number measurement on the cavity field, which acts as a quantum filter to herald the desired non-classical atomic state. This demonstrates a powerful synergy between cavity QED criticality and quantum state engineering.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work provides a concrete, measurement-based pathway to generate complex, non-classical many-body states (SCSs) using controllable quantum optical phenomena (criticality), which is highly relevant for quantum simulation and quantum memory.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper addresses the challenge of generating high-fidelity collective spin cat states (SCSs) within a cavity-coupled atomic ensemble.

**Main result.** They demonstrate that performing a photon-number measurement on the cavity field near the superradiant critical point heralds SCSs with enhanced size and fidelity.

**Method.** The protocol relies on exploiting strong light-matter entanglement and anti-squeezing inherent to the critical regime, mathematically linking the process to generalized photon subtraction.

**Model / system.** The system is modeled by the Dicke model, describing N two-level atoms coupled to a single cavity mode. The analysis utilizes the Hamiltonian and examines the system's behavior near the superradiant phase transition.

**Key observables.** SCS size ($\ell_{	ext{opt}}$), Fidelity ($F(	heta)$), Wigner function negativity, and the probability distribution of measured photons ($P(n)$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** The critical coupling strength ($g_c$), the atom number ($N$), and the photon number ($n$) measured.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis often focuses on the resonant case ($\omega_{	ext{cav}} = \omega_{	ext{atom}}$) but confirms the mechanism's applicability off-resonance. It assumes the ground state of the equilibrium Dicke model.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the protocol schematic (Fig. 1a), the Wigner function showing SCS non-classicality (Fig. 1b), and quantitative dependencies of $\ell_{	ext{opt}}$ and $P(n)$ on the coupling strength $g$ and the photon number $n$ (Figs. 2 and 6).

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the Dicke model and the concept of SCSs. It then details the measurement-based protocol, showing how photon detection projects the atomic state into a heralded SCS. Finally, it analyzes the scaling laws and the physical mechanism connecting this to generalized photon subtraction.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

We propose a method for generating collective spin cat states in a cavity-coupled atomic ensemble by exploiting strong light-matter entanglement and anti-squeezing associated with the superradiant phase transition. We numerically and analytically demonstrate that the cat states can be heralded by photon-number measurement on the ground state of the Dicke model. The near-critical regime enhances both the cat-state size and the probability of obtaining larger photon-number outcomes, and outcomes with larger photon numbers yield even larger cat states. We also show that a thermodynamic-limit analysis clarifies the generation mechanism and connects it to a natural light-matter analogue of generalized photon subtraction for optical cat-state generation. These results suggest that exploiting criticality in strongly coupled light-matter systems could open new directions for matter-based many-body quantum technologies.

</details>

**Summary.** This survey reviews the theory of polariton condensates, which are hybrid light-matter quasi-particles. It details the theoretical tools needed to describe their many-body physics, contrasting the quantum description with simpler classical analogues. The work highlights that understanding the system requires careful consideration of both linear and non-interacting effects alongside strong correlations.

**Why it may be interesting.** It bridges the gap between standard BEC theory (quantum gases) and quantum optics, providing a rich platform to study non-equilibrium, driven-dissipative many-body physics.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper surveys the theoretical concepts governing the formation, coherence, and dynamics of polariton condensates, emphasizing their unique nature compared to atomic BECs.

**Main result.** The dynamics can be understood by mapping the system to a coherent state evolution, which mathematically mirrors classical coupled harmonic oscillators, suggesting linear effects are often undervalued.

**Method.** The analysis employs a combination of one-particle quantum superposition descriptions (Bloch sphere) and many-body quantum-optical density matrix formalisms.

**Model / system.** The system is composed of exciton-polaritons, which are quasi-particles formed by the strong coupling of excitons (matter) and cavity photons (light) in a microcavity. The Hamiltonian describes the coupled light-matter system.

**Key observables.** Two-polariton reduced density matrix ($ho^{(2)}$), two-photon correlation function ($g^{(2)}(0)$), and coherence measures.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Detuning, effective masses ($m_{ex}, m_C$), and the degree of interaction strength.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The text argues that while the quantum picture is fundamental, much phenomenology can be explained using semi-classical or non-interacting approximations.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the unfolding of light-matter interaction, the two-particle polariton picture, and the two-photon correlation function $g^{(2)}(0)$ showing bunching, antibunching, and no correlation.

**Paper structure.** The structure moves from defining the polariton quasi-particle (one-particle picture) to describing the many-body quantum state using density matrices, and finally comparing the quantum vs. classical descriptions of the dynamics.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Polaritons are a superposition of light and matter, that combine Strong Interferences (of light) with Weak Interactions (of excitons), making them WISI (Weakly-Interacting, Strongly-Interfering) particles. Their condensation is the main highlight of a field which occupies a unique position at the intersection of quantum optics, condensed matter physics and nonlinear dynamics of driven, dissipative systems. This chapter surveys selected theoretical concepts of polariton condensates' formation, coherence and dynamics, with an emphasis on their distinctions from their atomic counterparts and on points of ongoing controversy. We argue that linear and non-interacting effects are undervalued in polariton physics, and that a significant part of the phenomenology -- including bosonic correlations and coherence buildup -- can often be understood without invoking strong interactions or genuine quantum effects.

</details>

<a id="paper-2605.15882"></a>
### [Propagation of conditional nonclassical reservoir states during quantum decoherence](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.15882v1)

**Authors:** Jargalsaikhan Artag, Koki Awaya, Takumi Kanezashi, Haruya Nagata, Daisuke Tsukayama, Moe Shimada, Jun-ichi Shirakashi

**Summary.** This theoretical paper explores how quantum coherence lost from a qubit during decoherence is imprinted onto the surrounding environment, or reservoir. By analyzing the reservoir state conditioned on a measurement outcome, the authors demonstrate that the environment can retain nonclassical quantum features, such as Wigner negativity, which are otherwise lost in the averaged, unconditional description.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work provides a detailed, phase-space picture of decoherence, showing that the environment acts as a memory that retains measurement-dependent quantum information, which is crucial for understanding quantum measurement processes.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper investigates the information content carried by the environment (reservoir) during quantum decoherence, specifically contrasting the state when the system is measured (conditional) versus when it is simply traced out (unconditional).

**Main result.** The reservoir state, when conditioned on a specific qubit readout, develops measurable nonclassical features like Wigner negativity and interference fringes that are absent in the unconditional reservoir state.

**Method.** The authors employ tensor-network dynamics to evolve the joint qubit-reservoir state, mapping the continuous bosonic bath to a discrete 1D chain representation for analysis.

**Model / system.** The model is based on spin-boson decoherence, coupling a two-level system (qubit) to a structured bosonic reservoir. The dynamics are analyzed by mapping the bath to a 1D chain Hamiltonian.

**Key observables.** Wigner negativity ($	ext{Vnc}$), qubit coherence ($\langle\sigma_xangle$), reservoir occupation ($\langle c^\dagger_k c_k angle$), and the conditional/unconditional reservoir state.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Spectral exponent ($s$), temperature ($\Theta/\Delta$), and coupling strengths ($\alpha$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis relies on mapping the continuous bosonic bath to a finite 1D chain, which is an approximation for the full reservoir dynamics.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the mapping from the spin-boson geometry to the 1D chain, parameter landscapes showing peak conditional nonclassicality, and time evolution plots comparing conditional vs. unconditional reservoir properties.

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses by first establishing the model and mapping, then calculating and comparing the conditional and unconditional reservoir states using Wigner functions, and finally analyzing how temperature and spectral properties control the visibility of this conditional nonclassical imprint.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Decoherence is usually described as the loss of local quantum coherence after tracing over environmental degrees of freedom. This reduced description, however, hides the reservoir state that carries the lost coherence. Here we show that spin-boson decoherence can write a postselectable nonclassical imprint into a structured bosonic reservoir. We map zero- and finite-temperature reservoirs to one-dimensional chains, evolve the joint qubit-reservoir state with tensor-network dynamics, and reconstruct the Wigner function of a time-adaptive leading collective reservoir coordinate after transverse qubit readout. The conditioned mapped-reservoir coordinate develops Wigner negativity and interference fringes that are strongly suppressed in the unconditional reservoir state. A parameter sweep shows that the spectral exponent and temperature control the visibility of this conditional nonclassicality, the mapped-chain excitation transport, and the degree to which a single collective coordinate captures the imprint. These results provide a branch-resolved phase-space picture of decoherence: the reservoir is not only a sink for qubit coherence, but can carry a measurement-conditioned nonclassical state in a collective mapped coordinate.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

**Summary.** This work develops a statistical framework to study Dynamical Quantum Phase Transitions (DQPTs) by analyzing the ensemble of states resulting from quantum measurements on an Ising chain. By using moment averaging of the dynamical free energy, the authors show that while single trajectories exhibit sharp nonanalyticities, the statistical average smooths these out. This provides a crucial theoretical tool for interpreting experimental measurements in quantum devices.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides a rigorous statistical framework to study non-equilibrium quantum dynamics, showing how measurement fundamentally alters the observable signatures of quantum phase transitions, which is crucial for interpreting experimental quantum measurements.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper investigates Dynamical Quantum Phase Transitions (DQPTs) by analyzing the statistical ensemble of post-measurement quantum states, moving beyond single-trajectory analysis.

**Main result.** The statistical average (Born rule) of the ensemble washes out the nonanalytic signatures of DQPTs seen in single trajectories, but the framework successfully characterizes statistical DQPTs using the average dynamical free energy.

**Method.** The authors characterize the ensemble using the $n$-th moment average dynamical free energy ($f_n$), mapping the distribution to a statistical model, and employing analytic continuation into the complex time plane.

**Model / system.** The primary model is the one-dimensional transverse-field Ising chain, subjected to a quantum quench followed by measurement, which induces a mixed state described by the Born probability distribution.

**Key observables.** Average dynamical free energy ($f_n$), multifractal dimensions ($D_q$), and the vanishing/emergence of Yang-Lee-Fisher zeros in the complex time plane.

**Important parameters / regimes.** The moment index $n$ acts as an effective inverse temperature, allowing crossover from high-temperature ($n=0$) to zero-temperature ($n	o\infty$) limits.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis assumes the measurement-induced mixed state is distinct from standard finite-temperature mixed states, and that the system can be analyzed via moment averaging.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the evolution of the dynamical free energy spectrum, showing level condensation near critical times, and compare the complex time evolution of post-selected vs. statistically averaged quantities, demonstrating the disappearance of zeros in the latter.

**Paper structure.** The paper first introduces the statistical characterization via $f_n$ for the Ising model. It then analyzes the crossover from multifractality to delocalization at the critical time. Finally, it uses analytic continuation to show how the Born-rule average affects the detection of DQPT signatures compared to single-trajectory methods.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Dynamical quantum phase transitions (DQPTs) occur at times when a quantum state exhibits a nonanalytic change in its return probability. This can be viewed as the probability of collapsing the evolved state to the initial state by quantum measurement. However, the initial wave function usually has exponentially small amplitude in the late time evolved state. Here we perform statistical characterization for all the possible post-measurement states distributed according to the Born's rule, by sampling a one-dimensional quantum Ising chain after a quantum quench dynamics. The statistical ensemble can also be viewed as a mixed state when the time evolved state is subjected to maximally dephasing noise in a certain basis. We map the distribution to a statistical model and characterize its effective "energy" spectrum, and introduce the average dynamical free energy, establishing a framework for the statistical DQPTs. We show the recovering of DQPT under high-moment average and a delocalized level distribution following critical times. Through analytic continuation into the complex time plane, we demonstrate the vanishing of Yang-Lee-Fisher zeros and the emergent level crossing near critical times. Finally, we propose a measurement-based quantum computation protocol to simulate the unitary evolution via single-qubit measurements on a two-dimensional cluster state. Our results provide a way for experimentally investigating statistical DQPTs in quantum devices, shedding light on the structured circuit sampling with insights from DQPT and generalizing the understanding of mixed state due to decoherence beyond equilibrium.

</details>

### [Energy-space quantum walks: Thermalization without state convergence](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.15339v1)

**Authors:** Alana Spak dos Santos, Renato Moreira Angelo

**Summary.** This paper introduces energy-space quantum walks to study thermalization, showing that energy populations can equilibrate to a Gibbs state even when the full quantum state fails to converge to that state. By quantifying the deviation using the thermal distance, the authors demonstrate that coherence acts as a persistent, controllable source of non-thermal behavior, separating classical equilibration from quantum corrections.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work provides a rigorous, quantitative framework to distinguish between classical relaxation processes and genuine quantum non-equilibrium effects in open quantum systems, which is crucial for understanding thermalization in realistic quantum simulators.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper investigates the fundamental difference between energy equilibration (population relaxation) and full quantum state thermalization in quantum walks.

**Main result.** It establishes a minimal scenario of 'thermalization without state convergence,' where energy populations reach a classical Gibbs distribution, but the full quantum state retains a persistent, coherence-induced deviation from the thermal manifold.

**Method.** The analysis uses an effective-dynamics approach, mapping the system onto an energy ladder and comparing the time evolution of the full density operator against its diagonal (population) components.

**Model / system.** The model is an energy-space quantum walk, where the configuration space is mapped onto a ladder of energy eigenlevels. The dynamics are governed by a unitary, collision-assisted quantum channel $\Phi_\mu$ parameterized by a coherence strength $\mu$.

**Key observables.** Thermal distance ($d_{th}(t)$) and the difference between the full thermal distance and the dephased distance, which quantifies the coherence contribution.

**Important parameters / regimes.** The coherence parameter $\mu$, which controls the quantum corrections, and the bias $b = p_-/p_+$ in the classical limit.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis assumes the underlying classical dynamics is ergodic and that the system can be effectively described by a minimal collision-assisted quantum walk structure.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the time evolution of the thermal distance, showing saturation at a finite, non-zero value when coherence ($\mu>0$) is present, contrasting this with the zero saturation seen in purely thermalizing cases.

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses by first establishing the classical limit (birth-death-lazy dynamics) leading to Gibbs equilibrium. It then embeds this into a unitary, coherent model, systematically decomposing the evolution into classical (population) and quantum (coherence) parts to quantify the separation.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

We introduce energy-space quantum walks as a minimal framework to investigate equilibration, thermalization, and irreversibility from an effective-dynamics perspective. By mapping the configuration space of a walk onto a ladder of energy eigenlevels, we reinterpret thermalization as transport in energy space, independently of microscopic system--bath details. At the classical level, the resulting birth--death--lazy dynamics leads to equilibration of the energy distribution and, under suitable conditions, to a Gibbs stationary state. We then embed this dynamics into a unitary, collision-assisted model in which coherence is controlled by a single parameter. A central result is a structural decoupling between population dynamics and coherence generation: while the populations evolve according to the classical process and relax to the Gibbs distribution, the full quantum state exhibits a persistent coherence-induced deviation from the thermal manifold. This establishes a minimal scenario of thermalization without state convergence, where equilibration occurs at the level of populations but not at the level of the full density operator. We quantify this effect using the thermal distance to the Gibbs state and derive perturbative bounds that relate the long-time deviation to classical transport properties. Our results show that coherence acts as a controllable and quantitatively bounded source of nonthermal behavior, providing a clear separation between classical equilibration and genuinely quantum corrections.

</details>

### [Beyond Unitary Quantum Simulation: Open-System Approaches to Quantum Chemistry toward Quantum Advantage](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.15277v1)

**Authors:** Michael Marthaler, Elias Zapusek, Florentin Reiter  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.15277v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** 🔥 `methods for driven-dissipative` **4/5** · `Keldysh / 2PI / non-Gaussian methods` **3/5** · `correlated / nonlocal dissipation` **3/5** · `driven-dissipative phase transition` **3/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **2/5** · `non-equilibrium universality` **2/5** · `Kondo & dissipative impurity` **1/5** · `analog quantum simulation` **1/5**

<div class="figure-strip-hint">↔ Scroll figures horizontally</div>
<div class="paper-figures-scroll">
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.15277_figures/2605.15277_fig1.jpg" alt="Figure 1">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 1.</b> Figure 1: Molecule (FeMoco cluster) coupled to a ther- mal environment, illustrating the open-system setting that pervades chemistry, condensed-matter physics, and materials science. Standard quantum algorithms largely abstract away this system–bath interaction, treating the electronic problem as closed and unitary. The dissipa- tive simulation framework discussed in this review in- stead takes the environmental coupling as a computa- tional resource rather than a nuisance.</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.15277_figures/2605.15277_fig2.jpg" alt="Figure 2">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 2.</b> Figure 2: Conceptual comparison between standard Born–Oppenheimer quantum chemistry and beyond-/post-Born– Oppenheimer approaches. In the Born–Oppenheimer picture, one first solves an electronic structure problem for fixed nuclear coordinates and then derives nuclear dynamics from the resulting potential-energy surface. In the beyond- Born–Oppenheimer picture, electrons and nuclei are treated on a common quantum footing, allowing nonadiabatic effects, vibronic structure, tunneling, and, in principle, dissipation acting on the coupled electron–nuclear system.</figcaption>
</figure>
</div>

**Summary.** This review argues that simulating quantum chemistry must embrace open-system dynamics, moving beyond idealized unitary models to account for environmental coupling. It analyzes the theoretical challenges and computational scaling associated with incorporating dissipation into quantum algorithms. The paper concludes by proposing a practical, incremental research strategy, moving from solid-state models to molecular dynamics.

**Why it may be interesting.** It directly bridges the gap between foundational quantum computation theory (open systems, dissipation) and a core area of physical chemistry, providing a roadmap for future quantum simulation efforts.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper addresses the need to move quantum chemistry simulations beyond idealized, closed-system unitary dynamics to incorporate the reality of open-system physics, such as relaxation and decoherence.

**Main result.** It argues that incorporating dissipation is crucial for realistic quantum chemistry but requires a fundamental overhaul of existing quantum simulation machinery, suggesting an incremental, hierarchical approach for practical implementation.

**Method.** The review synthesizes theoretical proposals, comparing scaling estimates for various mapping techniques (second-quantized vs. first-quantized) and outlining practical pathways from solid-state physics to molecular dynamics.

**Model / system.** The primary system is quantum chemistry, modeled by Hamiltonians that can be either closed (Born-Oppenheimer) or open (coupled to a thermal environment via Lindbladians). The scope ranges from periodic solids to small molecules.

**Key observables.** Stationary thermal state, electronic ground-state energy, reaction probabilities, and transition amplitudes.

**Important parameters / regimes.** System size (A), time/precision requirements, and the nature of environmental coupling (dissipation strength).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The ideal, fully general quantum simulation incorporating all physics is deemed too demanding; progress must be achieved through restricted, physically motivated settings.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 contrasts closed-system vs. open-system views of molecular dynamics. Figure 2 compares scaling approaches from standard Born-Oppenheimer to beyond-B-O treatments.

**Paper structure.** The review progresses by first critiquing the unitary assumption, then detailing the theoretical integration of dissipation, followed by a discussion of scaling limits, and concluding with a practical, hierarchical roadmap for future research.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Quantum simulation is widely regarded as one of the most promising routes to genuine quantum advantage, yet most existing approaches to quantum chemistry are formulated in terms of closed-system, unitary dynamics and ground-state preparation within the Born--Oppenheimer approximation. In this review, we discuss a broader perspective motivated by the observation that naturally occurring quantum systems are rarely isolated and often reach physically relevant states only through relaxation, decoherence, and thermalization. We first examine what is and is not known about exponential quantum advantage in chemistry, emphasizing that coherent Hamiltonian simulation provides the clearest formal case for speed-up, while many open questions remain for realistic problems. We then discuss how dissipation might ideally be integrated into quantum chemistry on a fault-tolerant quantum computer, using recent proposals for chemically motivated dynamical simulation as a guiding vision. More generally, we highlight the practical appeal of this approach to enhancing the robustness of quantum algorithms.

</details>

**Summary.** This work develops and applies a biorthogonal framework to study Dynamical Quantum Phase Transitions in non-Hermitian topological superconductors, using the Kitaev chain as a model. The authors demonstrate that this biorthogonal approach provides a theoretically consistent and physically robust method for identifying critical transition times, correcting discrepancies found using standard self-normal methods.

**Why it may be interesting.** The rigorous development of a consistent diagnostic tool (biorthogonality) for non-equilibrium dynamics in non-Hermitian systems is highly relevant for understanding open quantum systems and driven many-body physics.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper investigates Dynamical Quantum Phase Transitions (DQPTs) in non-Hermitian systems, specifically addressing the theoretical shortcomings of conventional self-normal approaches.

**Main result.** The authors establish that a biorthogonal framework is essential for consistently diagnosing DQPTs, yielding critical times that differ systematically from conventional methods.

**Method.** The study employs an associated-state formalism and reformulates key diagnostics like the Loschmidt rate function and the Dynamical Topological Order Parameter (DTOP) using biorthogonal inner products.

**Model / system.** The primary model is the non-Hermitian Kitaev chain, used as a prototypical system for non-Hermitian topological superconductors. The analysis is performed in momentum space using a specific Hamiltonian structure.

**Key observables.** Biorthogonal Loschmidt echo/rate, Dynamical Topological Order Parameter (DTOP), and Dynamical Fisher zeros ($Z_n(k)$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Critical times ($t_c$) associated with the transition, and the momentum $k$ resolved subsystems.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The core assumption is that the conventional self-normal approach lacks a proper probabilistic interpretation in non-Hermitian dynamics, necessitating the biorthogonal formalism.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the nonanalytic behavior of the biorthogonal Loschmidt rate and DTOP at critical times, and compare the results of the biorthogonal versus self-normal schemes across multiple quench processes.

**Paper structure.** The paper develops the biorthogonal framework, applies it to the Kitaev chain, compares its diagnostics (Loschmidt echo, DTOP) against conventional methods, and concludes by establishing the framework's robustness and necessity for non-Hermitian dynamics.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Dynamical quantum phase transitions in non-Hermitian systems pose fundamental challenges due to the intrinsic biorthogonality of their eigenstates. In this work, we extend a biorthogonal framework to investigate dynamical quantum phase transitions in non-Hermitian topological superconductors. Taking the non-Hermitian Kitaev chain as a prototypical model, we construct an associated-state formalism and reformulate the Loschmidt rate function, dynamical topological order parameter, and dynamical Fisher zeros. Within this framework, we find that the critical times at which dynamical quantum phase transitions occur differ from those based on the conventional self-normal approaches. We further analyze momentum-resolved subsystems at critical momenta and demonstrate the robustness of the biorthogonal framework. Our work highlights the essential role of biorthogonality in nonequilibrium dynamics and establishes a consistent theoretical framework for dynamical quantum phase transitions in non-Hermitian topological superconductors.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

### [An agitated oscillator chain](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.16209v1)

**Summary.** This work analyzes an oscillator chain coupled to active run-and-tumble particles, deriving a reduced Langevin equation under time-scale separation. The key finding is that the active bath induces negative linear friction, leading to an instability that is ultimately arrested by nonlinear effects. This results in the chain adopting a self-sustained, non-equilibrium steady state with characteristic Rayleigh-like pulsations.

**Why it may be interesting.** The emergence of self-sustained, non-equilibrium dynamics (like Rayleigh oscillators) from coupling to active matter is a key topic in non-equilibrium statistical mechanics, highly relevant to understanding active matter systems.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study investigates how the stationary dynamics of an oscillator chain are modified when coupled to a bath of active run-and-tumble particles.

**Main result.** The coupling can transform a passive chain into a self-sustained fluctuating medium exhibiting Rayleigh-like dynamics, characterized by non-equilibrium steady states and persistent currents.

**Method.** The analysis uses time-scale separation to derive a reduced Langevin equation for the chain dynamics, followed by Fourier space analysis of the resulting coupled modes.

**Model / system.** The system consists of a chain of coupled harmonic oscillators interacting with a bath of run-and-tumble particles (RTPs) moving on a ring. The dynamics are modeled via a reduced Langevin equation incorporating mean forces, induced friction, and noise.

**Key observables.** Mean Squared Velocity (MSV), Mean Squared Displacement (MSD), and the antisymmetric displacement-velocity cross-correlation $C_{qv}(\ell)$.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Activity parameter ($\alpha$), coupling strength ($\zeta$), and the ratio of time scales ($\epsilon = \omega_{chain}L/v_0$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The primary assumption is the time-scale separation between the slow oscillator chain dynamics and the fast bath dynamics, allowing for projection operator techniques.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate mode amplitude growth in the negative-friction regime, phase-space trajectories showing limit cycles (Rayleigh-like), and the non-equilibrium nature of spatial correlations.

**Paper structure.** The paper proceeds by deriving the reduced Langevin dynamics using time-scale separation, analyzing the resulting friction and noise terms in Fourier space, and finally characterizing the non-equilibrium stationary state through correlation functions and stability analysis.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

We study how the stationary dynamics of an oscillator chain is modified when coupled to a bath of run-and-tumble particles. First, assuming time-scale separation, we derive the induced Langevin chain dynamics with explicit expressions for the streaming term, friction coefficient, and noise amplitude. At high persistence of the run-and-tumble particle bath, the linear friction turns negative, creating an instability. Second, we find that this anti-damping is arrested at long times due to nonlinear effects, reminiscent of a Rayleigh oscillator. We conclude that a passive harmonic chain can be transformed by its coupling to active matter into a self-sustained fluctuating medium with many-body Rayleigh-like dynamics. That transfer of activity results in pulsations of the displacements, spatial oscillations, and the emergence of persistence in velocities along the chain.

</details>

### [Thermodynamic Networks: Harnessing Non-Equilibrium Steady States for Computation](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.15985v1)

**Summary.** This work introduces thermodynamic networks as a general framework for computation based on physical relaxation to non-equilibrium steady states. It rigorously proves that the ability to exhibit Negative Differential Conductance (NDC) is necessary and sufficient for these networks to achieve universal function approximation. The approach is demonstrated using quantum dot and enzymatic models, establishing a deep link between non-equilibrium physics and computational power.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides a direct physical realization of machine learning concepts (like universal approximation) using fundamental principles of non-equilibrium thermodynamics and transport physics, linking computation to dissipation.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Developing a general framework for autonomous, physics-based computation using non-equilibrium steady states (NESS).

**Main result.** The presence of Negative Differential Conductance (NDC) is the critical physical property that enables universal function approximation in these thermodynamic networks.

**Method.** The computation is modeled by finding the steady-state solution ($\dot{\mu}=0$) of the network dynamics, and training is achieved using implicit differentiation techniques analogous to Deep Equilibrium Models.

**Model / system.** The system is a 'thermodynamic network' modeled as a graph of mesoscopic reservoirs exchanging conserved quantities. Computation occurs when the system relaxes to a NESS, which maps an input state to a steady-state output.

**Key observables.** Differential Conductance, Conductance Matrix ($G$), Steady-State Output ($\mu^\star$), and the ability to compute non-monotone functions like XOR.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Negative Differential Conductance (NDC), Cooperativity, Input potentials ($x$), and system parameters ($	heta$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The process is autonomous, governed only by dissipation, and the adiabatic regime ($	au_{	ext{internal}} \ll 	au_{	ext{charging}}$) is assumed for the steady-state calculation.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the network structure, showing how cooperative channels yield monotone responses, while NDC channels enable non-monotone maps, and demonstrate successful classification (e.g., MNIST) using NDC.

**Paper structure.** The paper establishes the NESS framework, identifies NDC as the key ingredient for non-monotonicity, proves universal approximation theorems, and illustrates this concept using quantum dot and enzymatic reaction network platforms.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

We introduce thermodynamic networks, a general framework for autonomous, physics-based computation using non-equilibrium steady states. These networks are modeled as a collection of finite-size reservoirs that exchange conserved quantities--such as electric charge or molecular number--while relaxing to a non-equilibrium steady state, which encodes the solution of a computational problem. We identify Negative Differential Conductance (NDC) as the critical physical property governing the computational expressivity of the thermodynamic network. While networks lacking NDC are restricted to computing monotonic functions, the presence of NDC enables universal function approximation. For the training of the network, we use protocols that take advantage of the natural tendency of the system to equilibrate. We illustrate the versatility of our approach via two different platforms: quantum dot networks and enzymatic reaction networks. Both systems can be engineered to have NDC, enabling high performance in standard benchmarks, including sine function approximation and MNIST digit classification. Overall, our work establishes a rigorous link between non-equilibrium steady states and computational expressivity.

</details>

### [Short-time critical dynamics in the classical cubic dimer model](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.15730v1)

**Summary.** This work characterizes the non-equilibrium short-time critical dynamics of the 3D classical dimer model. Using Monte Carlo simulations, the authors determine key critical exponents, notably finding an unusual negative critical initial slip exponent. This negative exponent is linked theoretically to the combined effects of emergent SO(5) symmetry and a local U(1) gauge constraint.

**Why it may be interesting.** The study provides a detailed look at non-equilibrium critical dynamics, which shares conceptual links with how quantum systems evolve away from equilibrium, particularly in understanding universal scaling laws beyond simple mean-field descriptions.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Investigating the nonequilibrium critical dynamics, particularly in the short-time regime, of the classical cubic dimer model.

**Main result.** The authors determined the critical temperature ($T_c = 0.672(1)$), dynamic exponent ($z = 1.92(1)$), and found an anomalous negative critical initial slip exponent ($	heta = -1.052(5)$).

**Method.** Large-scale Monte Carlo simulations were used to analyze the short-time scaling behavior of the order parameter and its time correlation function.

**Model / system.** The system is the classical dimer model on a cubic lattice, which exhibits a continuous phase transition beyond the conventional Landau-Ginzburg-Wilson paradigm.

**Key observables.** Order parameter squared ($\langle N^2 angle$), time-correlation function ($Q(t)$), critical exponents ($eta/
u$, $z$, $	heta$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** The study focuses on the short-time regime, quenching from both ordered and disordered initial states.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis relies on theoretical scaling relations for short-time dynamics, and the negative $	heta$ is attributed to emergent SO(5) symmetry and a local U(1) gauge constraint.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the dependence of the order parameter on time and system size, showing data collapse when rescaled using the derived exponents.

**Paper structure.** The paper details the model and methods, reviews the theory of short-time scaling, presents the numerical results for the exponents, and concludes by interpreting the physical meaning of the anomalous negative exponent $	heta$.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

The classical dimer model on the cubic lattice hosts a columnar ordered phase and a disordered Coulomb phase, separated by a continuous phase transition that lies beyond the conventional Landau-Ginzburg-Wilson paradigm. While its equilibrium critical properties have been extensively studied, the nonequilibrium critical dynamics of this model--particularly in the short-time regime--remains largely unexplored. In this work, we investigate the short-time critical dynamics near the transition using large-scale Monte Carlo simulations. By quenching the system from both ordered and disordered initial states with vanishing initial correlation length, we analyze the scaling behaviors of the order parameter and its time correlation function in the short-time stage. From these scaling behaviors, we accurately determine the critical temperature $T_c = 0.672(1)$ and the static critical exponent $β/ν= 0.581(5)$ according to the scaling theory of the short-time dynamics. These results are in excellent agreement with previous equilibrium studies. Moreover, we extract the dynamic critical exponent $z = 1.92(1)$ and, notably, find a negative critical initial slip exponent $θ= -1.052(5)$. This unusual negative value contrasts sharply with the positive $θ$ typically observed in conventional critical dynamics. We attribute this anomalous behavior to the combined effects of the emergent SO(5) symmetry at criticality and the local U(1) gauge constraint (Gauss law), which enforces a conserved diffusive dynamics and enhances fluctuations in the short-time regime. Our results provide the first comprehensive characterization of nonequilibrium short-time criticality in the three-dimensional dimer model, shedding new light on the universal dynamical features of phase transitions beyond the Landau-Ginzburg-Wilson framework.

</details>

### [Unbounded Communication Power of a Qubit](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.16093v1)

**Summary.** This theoretical paper demonstrates that the quantum advantage inherent in single-qubit information processing can be sustained indefinitely across multiple sequential decoding steps. By carefully balancing the sender's preparation distinguishability against the receiver's measurement incompatibility, the authors show that information is not entirely exhausted, a stronger feature than previously understood.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work extends fundamental concepts of quantum information theory by showing that quantum advantages are not necessarily transient, suggesting new ways to model information persistence in open quantum systems.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper investigates how long the quantum advantage in information processing, initially demonstrated by a single qubit in a Random Access Code (RAC), can be maintained across an arbitrarily large number of sequential decoding measurements.

**Main result.** By utilizing preparation distinguishability as a sender resource and measurement incompatibility as a receiver resource, the authors prove that an arbitrarily long sequence of independent receivers can each extract information from the qubit while retaining a quantum advantage.

**Method.** The analysis involves deriving recursive relations for the distinguishabilities ($\Delta^{(k)}_1, \Delta^{(k)}_2$) under sequential, unsharp measurements, relating the problem to constrained binary discrimination tasks.

**Model / system.** The system is a single qubit undergoing sequential $2 	o 1$ Random Access Code (RAC) decoding. The protocol uses unsharp measurements (POVMs) rather than ideal projective measurements to mitigate information loss.

**Key observables.** Preparation distinguishability ($\Delta$), average quantum success probability ($	ext{P}_{	ext{avg}}$), and the sequence of measurement parameters ($\{\lambda_k\}$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** The initial preparation parameters $(\Delta_1^{(1)}, \Delta_2^{(1)})$ and the sequence of measurement unsharpness parameters $\{\lambda_k\}$ are crucial.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis assumes the use of unsharp (non-projective) measurements and relies on relating the RAC task to preparation discrimination bounds ($\Delta_1^2 + \Delta_2^2 \le 1$).

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the sequential passing of the qubit state through multiple receivers and compare the quantum bound ($\Delta_1^2 + \Delta_2^2 \le 1$) against classical bounds ($\Delta_1 + \Delta_2 \le 1$).

**Paper structure.** The paper builds from analyzing the single-step quantum advantage condition, deriving bounds on distinguishabilities, and culminates in proving the feasibility of an infinite sequence of measurements by constructing a suitable sequence of measurement parameters $\{\lambda_k\}$ that maintains the quantum advantage.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Quantum mechanics enables information-processing advantages even at the level of a single qubit. A paradigmatic example is the 2$\to$1 random access code (RAC), where a qubit outperforms a classical bit in retrieving encoded information. In the standard form, however, this quantum advantage is restricted to a single receiver, since decoding measurements inevitably destroy the encoded information. Contrary to this, we address how long the information encoded in a single qubit remains accessible even after multiple decoding, each with a quantum advantage. Introducing preparation distinguishability as an operational resource associated with the sender, we show that its interplay with measurement incompatibility on the receiver's side can mitigate measurement-induced disturbance, thereby enabling an arbitrarily long sequence of receivers to each retain a quantum advantage. Our results show that, even under repeated measurements, the information encoded in a qubit need not be entirely exhausted, revealing a stronger communication feature than previously recognised.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2605.15841"></a>
### [Critical quench dynamics of Wegner's $\mathbb{Z}_2$ gauge model: a geometric perspective](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.15841v1)

**Authors:** Ramgopal Agrawal, Leticia F. Cugliandolo, Marco Picco  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** statistical mechanics · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.15841v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** 🔥 `non-equilibrium dynamics` **4/5** · 🔥 `non-equilibrium universality` **4/5** · `analog quantum simulation` **1/5** · `driven-dissipative phase transition` **1/5**

<div class="figure-strip-hint">↔ Scroll figures horizontally</div>
<div class="paper-figures-scroll">
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.15841_figures/2605.15841_fig1.jpg" alt="Figure 1">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 1.</b> FIG. 1. Schematic of different geometrically defined objects in Wegner’s three-dimensional gauge</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.15841_figures/2605.15841_fig2.jpg" alt="Figure 2">

**Summary.** This work analyzes the non-equilibrium dynamics of the $\mathbb{Z}_2$ gauge model by tracking the relaxation of geometrically defined structures like FK clusters and line loops. Using finite-size scaling, the authors determine a universal dynamical exponent $z_p \simeq 2.6$ that governs the approach to criticality. This confirms that geometric measures are highly effective tools for characterizing critical dynamics in systems with global order parameters.

**Why it may be interesting.** The finding that geometrical observables yield a dynamical exponent consistent with the thermal critical exponent, despite the absence of local order parameters, provides a powerful, non-standard diagnostic tool for studying critical dynamics in gauge theories.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper investigates the critical quench dynamics of Wegner's $\mathbb{Z}_2$ gauge model, focusing on how geometric observables relax towards equilibrium after a sudden change in temperature.

**Main result.** The critical relaxation of percolation observables is governed by a dynamical exponent $z_p \simeq 2.6$, which is robust across different quench protocols and matches the energy density exponent $z_c$.

**Method.** The study employs time-dependent finite-size scaling analysis and analyzes the scaling of cluster size distributions $N(s, t)$ for various geometric objects.

**Model / system.** The system is Wegner's $\mathbb{Z}_2$ gauge model on a 3D cubic lattice, which exhibits a topological confinement-deconfinement phase transition. Dynamics are studied by quenching from high-temperature or zero-temperature states to the critical point.

**Key observables.** Percolation order parameter ($P(t)$), energy density ($e(t, L)$), and the size distribution of clusters $N(s, t)$ for FK clusters and line loops.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Dynamical exponents ($z_p, z_c$), critical exponents ($eta_p, 
u_p$), and the lattice size $L$.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis relies on dynamic scaling hypotheses, assuming that observables can be collapsed onto universal scaling functions dependent on a characteristic lengthscale $\xi(t) \sim t^{1/z}$.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the collapse of scaled energy density and percolation observables onto universal scaling functions, confirming the predicted power-law decay of cluster size distributions $N(s, t) \propto s^{-\tau_p}$ at late times.

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses by first establishing the geometric nature of the model's critical behavior, then applying finite-size scaling to measure the dynamical exponents ($z_p$ vs $z_c$), and finally analyzing the size distribution $N(s, t)$ to confirm dynamical scaling across different cluster types.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Wegner's $\mathbb{Z}_2$ gauge model is the earliest formulation of pure lattice gauge theory and predicts the topological nature of the confinement-deconfinement transition. In three dimensions ($D=3$), the equilibrium critical behavior of the model is understood in terms of geometrically defined objects, namely loop excitations and Fortuin-Kasteleyn (FK) clusters. This work investigates the critical quench dynamics of this model from a geometric perspective, following quenches from both a high-temperature percolation phase and the zero-temperature ground state. Using time-dependent finite-size scaling analysis, we find that the critical non-equilibrium relaxation of the percolation order parameter is governed by a dynamical exponent $z_{\rm p} \simeq 2.6$, consistent with that associated with the energy density, $z_{\rm c}$. Importantly, the value of $z_{\rm p}$ is robust with respect to the initial quench condition and the choice of geometrical objects. Furthermore, we provide a detailed characterization of the kinetics of different geometrical objects during the evolution from the percolation phase. Notably, we observe that the quench dynamics obeys dynamic scaling in terms of a growing lengthscale, $ξ_{\rm p}(t) \sim t^{1/z_{\rm p}}$, despite the absence of a local order parameter.

</details>

### [Sub-picosecond inter-core skew characterization in multicore fibers via Hong--Ou--Mandel interference](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.16135v1)

**Authors:** L. Lira Tacca, L. Marques Fagundes, M. Morales Lillo, M. Navarro, I. Machuca, S. Gómez, G. H. dos Santos, J. Cariñe, G. Saavedra, E. S. Gómez, G. Lima, S. P. Walborn

**Summary.** This paper presents a novel, high-precision method to measure inter-core skew (ICS) in multicore fibers using two-photon Hong-Ou-Mandel (HOM) interference. By achieving sub-picosecond resolution, the authors validated the expected random-walk scaling of ICS over long distances. This technique establishes a practical platform for ensuring timing uniformity in MCFs for both classical and quantum space-division multiplexing applications.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work provides a crucial, high-precision diagnostic tool for characterizing timing uniformity in optical fibers, which is essential for scaling quantum communication networks that rely on precise temporal synchronization.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper addresses the critical need to accurately characterize Inter-core Skew (ICS), which is the differential group delay between cores in Multicore Fibers (MCFs), for use in quantum photonic networks.

**Main result.** The authors achieved a high-precision measurement of ICS with a demonstrated accuracy of $\pm 0.11$ ps, validating the stochastic random-walk scaling of this delay over long fiber lengths.

**Method.** The measurement relies on two-photon Hong-Ou-Mandel (HOM) interference using either Spontaneous Parametric Down-Conversion (SPDC) or pulsed lasers to probe the differential group delay.

**Model / system.** The physical system is a multicore fiber (MCF), and the measurement utilizes HOM interference in a multiport beam splitter. The ICS is modeled as a zero-mean random walk, leading to an RMS growth $\sigma_{	au}(L) \propto \sqrt{L}$.

**Key observables.** Inter-core Skew ($	au_{jk}$), HOM interference dip/peak center positions, and the Root Mean Square (RMS) ICS scaling $\sigma_{	au}(L)$.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Measurement precision of $\pm 0.11$ ps; scaling coefficient $\kappa = 48.7 \pm 2.5 	ext{ ps}/\sqrt{	ext{km}}$; length range from $7.7 	ext{ m}$ to $1300 	ext{ m}$.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The HOM dip/peak shift is directly proportional to the differential group delay, and the measurement is limited by the delay-stage positioning uncertainty.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the HOM interference data, showing the characteristic bunching dips and anti-bunching peaks for different fiber lengths and sources (SPDC vs. pulsed laser).

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of ICS characterization, details the HOM interferometric method using both SPDC and pulsed lasers, presents the extraction of ICS from interference features, and finally validates the stochastic random-walk scaling over a wide length range.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Inter-core skew (ICS), the differential group delay between cores of a multicore fiber (MCF), is a critical parameter for both classical space-division multiplexed communications and quantum photonic networks. We present a high-precision measurement of ICS in a commercially available four-core fiber using two-photon Hong--Ou--Mandel (HOM) interference in a fiber-integrated $4\times4$ multiport beam splitter. By extracting the center position of HOM interference dips and peaks across all twelve core-pair combinations, we obtain individual ICS values with a demonstrated precision of $\pm0.11\,$ps, limited by the delay-stage positioning uncertainty. The root-mean-square ICS grows as $σ_τ(L) = κ\sqrt{L}+c$ with $κ= 48.7 \pm 2.5\,\mathrm{ps}/\!\sqrt{\mathrm{km}}$ and $c = 9.76 \pm 1.2\,$ps, over fiber lengths from $7.7\,$m to $1300\,$m. This first direct validation of the stochastic random-walk scaling across a length range spanning laboratory to field-deployed scales was made possible by HOM's immunity to first-order path fluctuations, which renders classical interferometric methods impractical for long installed fibers. The demonstrated $\pm0.11\,$ps precision represents a $\sim\!180$-fold improvement over correlation optical time-domain reflectometry (C-OTDR), the standard method for long-fiber ICS characterization. Fisher information analysis establishes a fundamental Cramér--Rao precision limit in the femtosecond range, indicating further improvement is achievable with better delay control. These results establish a practical platform for characterising timing uniformity in MCF-based networks for both quantum and classical space-division multiplexed applications.

</details>

**Summary.** This paper explores how uniaxial strain affects the thermodynamics of frustrated magnets using the elastocaloric effect ($\eta$). By analyzing Ising and Heisenberg models on triangular and kagome lattices, the authors show that the divergence of $\eta$ at low temperatures provides a universal signature for extensive ground-state entropy. This work offers a powerful theoretical tool to probe magnetic frustration in quantum materials.

**Why it may be interesting.** The connection between macroscopic thermodynamic responses (like the elastocaloric effect) and microscopic quantum properties (like ground-state degeneracy) is highly relevant for understanding quantum phase transitions in condensed matter systems.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study investigates the thermodynamic response of frustrated magnets to uniaxial strain to reveal the underlying physics of magnetic frustration.

**Main result.** For Ising models, the elastic Grüneisen ratio ($\eta$) diverges arbitrarily at low temperatures near maximal frustration, serving as a universal hallmark of extensive ground-state entropy.

**Method.** The authors calculate the entropy ($s$) and subsequently the elastic Grüneisen ratio ($\eta$) using theoretical models and analytical expansions.

**Model / system.** The work analyzes Ising and Heisenberg models on spatially anisotropic triangular and kagome lattices. The Hamiltonian is modified by uniaxial strain ($\epsilon$), which linearly alters the exchange couplings ($J$ and $J'$).

**Key observables.** Elastic Grüneisen ratio ($\eta$), entropy ($s$), and specific heat ($c$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Temperature ($T$), uniaxial strain ($\epsilon$), and the ratio of exchange couplings ($J'/J$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis relies on analytical expansions for $c$ and $s$ in terms of $\epsilon$, and comparisons are made between different temperature and strain limits.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the specific heat, entropy, and the elastocaloric effect ($\eta$) for the Ising model on the anisotropic triangular lattice, showing enhancement of $\eta$ as $|\epsilon| 	o 0$ at low $T$.

**Paper structure.** The paper systematically analyzes the Ising and Heisenberg models, deriving $\eta$ from the strain dependence of entropy. It contrasts the behavior of the two models, highlighting the role of ground-state entropy in the divergence of $\eta$ for the Ising case.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Motivated by experimental progress in pressure and strain tuning of quantum materials, we examine the thermodynamic response of frustrated magnets to uniaxial strain. Specifically, we study Ising and Heisenberg models on spatially anisotropic triangular (and, for the Ising model, also kagome) lattices. We determine the entropy as a function of temperature and strain, and use it to compute the elastic Grüneisen ratio $η$. The Ising models can be strain-tuned into and out of classical spin-liquid phases, and we show that $η$ can become arbitrarily large at low temperature $T$ near the point of maximal frustration, a universal hallmark of an extensive ground-state entropy. In contrast, the spin-$1/2$ Heisenberg model is moderately frustrated and displays multiple $T=0$ phase transitions. These transitions dominate $η$ at low $T$ while the intermediate-$T$ behavior is similar to that of the Ising model. We discuss the extent to which the elastic Grüneisen ratio can be used to deduce the phase diagram, and we connect our results to recent experiments on triangular-lattice magnets.

</details>

**Summary.** The authors report on the creation of a bright, entangled twin-beam source using PDC in PPLN, demonstrating precise control over its spatio-temporal mode structure. By tuning the pump pulse duration via dispersion, they can transition between single-mode and controlled multimode operation. This platform is highly valuable for quantum-enhanced metrology due to its strong non-degeneracy and quantifiable entanglement resource.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work provides a highly controllable, broadband source of entangled photons, which is crucial for advancing quantum sensing and nonlinear quantum optics experiments.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The goal is to develop and characterize an ultrafast, bright, entangled twin-beam source that maintains spatio-temporal mode control across varying brightness levels.

**Main result.** The source successfully operates in a near-single spatio-temporal mode regime ($K \approx 1$) and establishes a practical platform for quantum-enhanced metrology due to strong non-degeneracy.

**Method.** Characterization involves measuring photon-number statistics via $g^{(2)}(0)$ and performing singular-value decomposition (Schmidt decomposition) on the spectral density matrix.

**Model / system.** The system uses type-0 parametric down-conversion (PDC) in a periodically-poled lithium niobate (PPLN) crystal to generate entangled signal (near-IR) and idler (mid-IR) beams.

**Key observables.** Second-order correlation function $g^{(2)}(0)$, Schmidt numbers ($K$), and the separation of entanglement resource into modal and occupational degrees of freedom.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Pump pulse duration ($	au_p$), Group-Delay Dispersion (GDD), and the resulting effective mode number $K$.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis relies on the Schmidt decomposition of the Joint Spectral Amplitude (JSA) and assumes the validity of the Bloch-Messiah decomposition in the bright limit.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the experimental setup using PPLN, show the dependence of signal photons on pump photons, and plot photon number histograms and $g^{(2)}(0)$ vs. mode entropy.

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses from introducing the physical platform (PDC in PPLN) to detailing the diagnostics ($g^{(2)}(0)$, SVD), establishing the control mechanism (GDD tuning), and finally quantifying the entanglement resource distribution.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

We introduce an ultrafast, bright, entangled twin-beam source generated by type-0 parametric down-conversion in periodically-poled lithium niobate at MHz repetition rate, with continuously tunable Schmidt number $K$ set by the pump pulse duration. Photon-number statistics characterization via $g^{(2)}(0)$ and singular-value decomposition of the signal spectral density matrix yield $K\simeq1.05$ and $K\simeq1.03$, respectively, maintained over multiple orders of magnitude in brightness. Group-delay dispersion of the pump drives a continuous transition from single-mode operation to a controlled multimode regime, consistent with the temporal gain window departing from the inverse phase-matching bandwidth. Strong non-degeneracy of the source (signal at 1.37 um, idler at 4.0 um, $\sim 100$ fs duration) decouples a mid-infrared interaction wavelength, which overlaps with molecular vibrational resonances, from a near-infrared detection band, establishing a practical platform for quantum-enhanced metrology, nonlinear interferometry, and mid-infrared spectroscopic sensing. We show that in the bright few-mode limit, the total entanglement resource is clearly separated between modal and occupational degrees of freedom, and that our source allocates up to 95-97% of that resource to the occupational sector.

</details>
