<a id="paper-2605.16151"></a>
### [Generalized measurement incompatibility](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.16151v1)

**Authors:** Edwin Peter Lobo, Maria Balanzó-Juandó, Stefano Pironio

</div>

**Summary.** This work generalizes joint-measurability to partial joint-measurability (G-JM) for quantum measurements, providing necessary and sufficient mathematical conditions for this property. The authors derive quantitative bounds on detection efficiency ($\eta$) that determine when measurements are G-JM, which in turn sets fundamental limits on the security of device-independent quantum key distribution protocols.

**Why it may be interesting.** The rigorous mathematical framework for characterizing measurement compatibility, especially when accounting for realistic imperfections like detection loss, directly impacts the theoretical limits of quantum communication protocols.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper generalizes the concept of joint-measurability to 'partial joint-measurability' (G-JM) for quantum measurements, particularly in the context of untrusted measurement devices.

**Main result.** It provides mathematical formulations for G-JM, showing it is decidable by an SDP, and derives analytical thresholds on detection efficiency ($\eta$) that limit the robustness of device-independent quantum cryptographic protocols.

**Method.** The authors use mathematical formulations involving quantum instruments and POVMs, deriving bounds by optimizing over various parameters related to measurement settings and detection efficiency.

**Model / system.** The model involves quantum measurements acting on a Hilbert space, characterized by POVMs $\{B_{b|y}\}_{b}$ indexed by measurement settings $y$. The analysis extends to lossy photonic implementations and qubit observables.

**Key observables.** Detection efficiency ($\eta$), the angular aperture ($	heta$) of the measurement axes, and the conditional Shannon entropies $H(A|E)$ vs $H(A|E')$.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Detection efficiency ($\eta$), number of measurements ($n$), and the visibility ($
u$) of the measurement device.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis assumes an adversary Eve is limited to classical side information, and the results are derived for generic strategies independent of the specific POVM form.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the simulation process of measurements, the angular aperture $	heta$ of the measurement axes, and the maximum detection efficiency $\eta^*$ as a function of $	heta$ for different types of partial joint-measurability.

**Paper structure.** The paper develops the theory by first defining G-JM, providing multiple mathematical formulations, and then applying these concepts to derive quantitative bounds on $\eta$ for various partial joint-measurability cases (input, outcome, and combined). The structure culminates in applying these bounds to analyze the security of DIQKD protocols.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Quantum measurements can be incompatible, i.e., they can fail to be jointly measurable. Recently, a weaker notion of joint-measurability, called partial joint-measurability, was proposed by Masini et al. in [Quantum 8, 1574 (2024)]. In this work, we further generalize this notion to the setting where only a subset of the outcomes of each measurement is required to be jointly determined by classical variables. We provide two mathematical formulations of partial joint-measurability and show that, like full joint-measurability, it can be decided by solving a single semidefinite program. We prove that in the case of an untrusted measurement device, an adversary Eve, limited to classical side information, can perfectly guess the outcomes of the measurement device if and only if the set of measurements is partially jointly measurable. We derive analytical thresholds on the detection efficiency below which generic measurements become partially jointly measurable. Such bounds directly yield limits on the robustness of device-independent and semi-device-independent quantum cryptographic protocols against detection inefficiency. In particular, our results highlight the importance of a careful treatment of postselection in security analyses.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2605.15554"></a>
### [Interface Piezoelectric Loss in Superconducting Qubits](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.15554v1)

**Summary.** This paper investigates interface piezoelectricity as a dominant decoherence source in superconducting qubits fabricated on silicon. By coupling the qubit to SAW resonators, the authors experimentally and numerically demonstrate that energy loss into acoustic phonons significantly limits qubit lifetime. The findings are crucial for advancing quantum computing by identifying a structural loss channel and proposing device-level engineering solutions to mitigate it.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work is highly relevant as it tackles a fundamental decoherence mechanism in superconducting qubits, providing quantitative scaling laws ($\Gamma \propto f^{3.4}$) and engineering guidelines (metamaterials) for improving quantum hardware.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Dissipation, particularly interface piezoelectric loss, remains a major obstacle limiting the coherence time of superconducting quantum qubits.

**Main result.** The study provides direct evidence that interface piezoelectricity acts as a distinct, measurable loss channel, which can dominate over TLS loss at sufficiently high frequencies.

**Method.** The research combines experimental measurements of qubit decay rates with detailed multiphysics simulations (using finite-element methods) to model the energy exchange between the qubit and acoustic phonons.

**Model / system.** The system involves a transmon qubit coupled to a Surface Acoustic Wave (SAW) resonator on a silicon substrate. The physics is modeled using coupled Hamiltonian/Langevin equations describing the interaction between the superconducting circuit and the mechanical acoustic modes.

**Key observables.** Qubit lifetime ($T_1$), excited-state population ($p_e$), and the frequency dependence of the loss rate ($\Gamma_{	ext{piezo}} \propto f^{3.4}$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Interface Energy Participation Ratio (EPR), coupling strength ($g_{m,k}$), and the crossover frequency where piezoelectric loss dominates (predicted near 10 GHz).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis relies on distinguishing frequency-independent mechanical resonances from bias-voltage-shifting TLS features, and modeling the coupling via effective admittance calculations.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate device structures, measurement protocols (e.g., $p_e$ vs. $V_{bias}$), and simulation results showing the frequency scaling of the predicted piezoelectric decay rate compared to other loss sources.

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses from identifying the general problem of dissipation to proposing the piezoelectric mechanism, detailing the experimental diagnostic techniques (using SAW resonators and bias sweeping), and finally presenting theoretical models and simulations to quantify the loss and suggest structural mitigation strategies.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Dissipation remains a central obstacle to improving superconducting quantum circuits, yet the microscopic origins of loss in widely used materials platforms are not fully understood. Here, we report the observation of interface piezoelectricity-induced dissipation in superconducting qubits fabricated on high-resistivity silicon. Our devices use a transmon qubit with a shunt capacitor that simultaneously serves as an interdigital transducer embedded in a surface acoustic wave resonator. By tuning the qubit transition into resonance with discrete mechanical modes, we observe up to a factor-of-two reduction in qubit lifetime, consistent with energy exchange between the qubit and mechanical modes mediated by piezoelectric coupling at the aluminum-silicon interface. Our findings provide direct evidence for interface piezoelectricity as a distinct loss channel in superconducting qubits. Combined with multiphysics simulations, these findings suggest that interface piezoelectric loss can dominate over loss from two-level systems at sufficiently high frequencies.

</details>

**Summary.** This paper demonstrates a scalable Quantum Processing Unit (QPU) for trapped ions by integrating the ion trap with a time-multiplexing control unit. By using an analog multiplexer, the authors overcome the massive wiring challenge associated with large-scale ion traps. The results confirm that this multiplexing approach maintains the high fidelity required for quantum computation.

**Why it may be interesting.** The work directly addresses the engineering bottleneck of scaling trapped ion systems by proposing and experimentally validating a cryogenic, low-power multiplexing architecture for control electronics.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Scaling trapped ion quantum processors to thousands of qubits faces severe wiring and control line density challenges.

**Main result.** The demonstration shows that a time-multiplexing scheme using a surface ion trap and integrated analog multiplexers is compatible with high-fidelity quantum operations.

**Method.** The authors built a Quantum Processing Unit (QPU) by integrating a surface ion trap with a cryostat-based analog multiplexer using a sample-and-hold technique.

**Model / system.** The system is a trapped ion QPU fabricated on a surface ion trap, interfaced with an ASIC multiplexer. This setup allows routing multiple control voltages from few inputs, mitigating wiring bottlenecks.

**Key observables.** Motional heating rates (measured in phonons/s), gate error decay rates, and secular frequencies.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Multiplexing sampling intervals below 50 ms are sufficient for gate error fidelity; voltage decay rates are measured in V/min.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The multiplexing scheme is assumed to maintain high fidelity despite charge injection and voltage decay during the sample-and-hold process.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the QPU architecture combining the trap and multiplexer, and show measurements of heating rates and voltage decay dynamics.

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses from identifying the scaling challenge (wiring) to proposing and demonstrating the multiplexing solution, followed by detailed characterization of heating rates and charge injection effects.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

A fault-tolerant quantum computer is expected to require thousands of qubits. Trapped ion architectures provide a modular approach where the quantum register is divided into multiple subregisters connected by physically moving the corresponding ions. Transporting ions at scale comes with several challenges such as the need to connect thousands of control lines to an ion trap chip. Multiplexing the required control voltages from few input signals to multiple electrodes offers a solution to this wiring challenge. Here we demonstrate a quantum processing unit that combines a surface ion trap with a time multiplexer via a sample-and-hold technique that initially charges electrodes to fixed voltages and disconnects them during qubit operations. We characterize the unit's performance by measuring motional heating rates below one phonon per second in both open and closed switch configurations. We further characterize the sample and hold process and find that sampling intervals below 50 ms are sufficient to keep expected gate errors from decaying charges during the hold phase below $10^{-4}$. Our results indicate that the multiplexing scheme is compatible with high-fidelity operations.

</details>

<a id="paper-2605.16101"></a>
### [Quantum Measurement without Ontology](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.16101v1)

**Authors:** Richard Healey  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.16101v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** 🔥 `quantum measurements` **4/5** · `measurement-induced transitions` **1/5**

<div class="figure-strip-hint">↔ Scroll figures horizontally</div>

<figure class="paper-figure-card">

<figure class="paper-figure-card">

**Summary.** This paper argues that quantum theory does not describe objective, mind-independent facts about physical reality regarding states or probabilities. Instead, the objectivity of quantum knowledge is established by the norms and practices of the scientific community when applying the theory to predict outcomes in specific physical situations. It shifts the focus from 'what is real' to 'how we are justified in making claims.'

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides a deep philosophical underpinning to quantum measurement, which is crucial for understanding the interpretation of quantum field theories and open quantum systems where the environment plays a defining role.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper tackles the fundamental philosophical problem of objectivity in quantum mechanics, specifically questioning whether quantum states, Born probabilities, and measurement outcomes represent objective facts about a mind-independent reality.

**Main result.** The objectivity of quantum knowledge is argued to be instituted not by veridical representation of reality, but by conformity to epistemic norms and the successful application of quantum theory within specific 'agent-situations' or physical procedures.

**Method.** The analysis employs philosophical tools, comparing ontological interpretations with pragmatic and coherence-based views (like QBism), and drawing analogies from relativistic physics and quantum metrology.

**Model / system.** The discussion centers on abstract quantum systems, the mathematical formalism of quantum states, and the physical context of measurement, using examples like LIGO to ground the philosophical arguments.

**Key observables.** Measurement outcomes, Born probabilities, quantum states, and the relative frequencies of detection events.

**Important parameters / regimes.** The distinction between 'absoluteness' and 'objectivity'; the structure of the 'agent-situation' relative to decoherence environments.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The core assumption is that standard physical practice requires an explanation for the objectivity of its results, leading to the rejection of strict ontological realism for quantum concepts.

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses by first establishing the failure of classical notions of measurement objectivity in quantum theory, then contrasting various interpretations (QBism, RQM), and finally arguing that objectivity arises from normative constraints and successful application in physical procedures like metrology.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Measurement is an important scientific activity. In most of science, including classical physics, is may be understood as a way of finding out about the physical world and representing the results numerically. No-go theorems show that measurement of quantum observables is not like that: the recorded outcome is typically created rather than revealed in a quantum measurement, in which case there is no objective fact about the observable's prior value. Other no-go theorems show that unitary quantum theory can generally neither explain nor even represent a unique recorded outcome, thereby threatening that outcome's objectivity. Methodological norms inherent in quantum physical practice nevertheless institute the objectivity, not only of unique recorded outcomes of quantum measurements, but also of non-quantum features of the world that physicists and other scientists take their models to represent.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

### [Entropy Production from Spin--Vibrational Coupling in Endohedral-Fullerene Qubits Encapsulated in Suspended Carbon Nanotubes](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.15521v1)

**Authors:** Cristian Staii  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** statistical mechanics · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.15521v1>

The mechanical entropy flux Φm(t) and spin entropy flux Φs(t) are plotted using the weak-excitation expressions derived in the text. As g1 increases, coherent spin–phonon exchange occurs on a shorter timescale, leading to a faster buildup of entropy flow through the mechanical channel and a corresponding redistribution of dissipation away from direct spin relaxation and toward the vibrational mode.</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">

As dephasing increases, the nonclassical one-phonon structure is progressively smoothed and the distribution broadens in phase space, illustrating the combined effects of mechanical diffusion and spin decoherence. Increasing dephasing progressively damps the oscillatory behavior associated with coherent spin–phonon exchange. Right panel: corresponding irreversible contribution Π(t) obtained from the phenomenological weak-excitation model. **Summary.** This theoretical study models the interaction between molecular spin qubits (PEFs) and the quantized vibrations of suspended CNTs. By developing a hybrid open-system model, the authors derive an entropy balance equation to quantify how irreversibility is generated and distributed between the spin and mechanical baths. The results establish CNT-PEF hybrids as powerful platforms for studying non-equilibrium quantum thermodynamics.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work is highly relevant as it provides a detailed, quantitative framework for diagnosing non-equilibrium quantum processes (like decoherence and information loss) using thermodynamic quantities (entropy) in solid-state quantum devices.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper analyzes entropy generation and irreversibility in a hybrid quantum system coupling spin and mechanical (vibrational) degrees of freedom.

**Main result.** It demonstrates that the system exhibits crossovers between oscillator-dominated and spin-dominated entropy-production regimes, providing a quantitative basis for studying non-equilibrium quantum thermodynamics.

**Method.** The analysis employs a hybrid open-system model, using the Wigner function formalism for the mechanical mode and deriving an entropy balance equation from the Lindblad master equation.

**Model / system.** The system consists of paramagnetic endohedral fullerenes (PEFs) acting as qubits, coupled to the flexural modes of a suspended Carbon Nanotube (CNT) resonator. The dynamics are governed by a Lindblad master equation incorporating spin relaxation, mechanical damping, and the spin-vibrational interaction.

**Key observables.** Entropy production ($\Pi(t)$), entropy flux ($\Phi(t)$), mechanical Wigner function $W(q, p, t)$, and mutual information $I_{s:m}(t)$.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Coupling strength ($g_1$), mechanical damping ($\gamma_m$), spin relaxation rates ($\Gamma$), and external driving strength ($\epsilon$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis relies on projecting the spin manifold to two states and treating the mechanical mode using a Gaussian propagator, which assumes linear damping and quadratic Hamiltonians.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the time evolution of the reduced mechanical Wigner function, showing oscillations between vacuum and one-phonon profiles, and plot the time evolution of mechanical and spin entropy fluxes.

**Paper structure.** The paper builds from deriving the effective Jaynes-Cummings Hamiltonian, to applying the Wigner function formalism for the mechanical mode, and finally deriving the entropy balance equation to quantify entropy production and flux in various coupling regimes.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Hybrid carbon nanotube-fullerene architectures provide a controllable platform for studying irreversibility and information flow in structured quantum environments. We analyze entropy generation in a system where paramagnetic endohedral fullerenes, such as N@C$_{60}$ and P@C$_{60}$, are encapsulated inside a suspended carbon nanotube (CNT) resonator, with selected multi-level fullerene spin states forming an effective qubit coupled to quantized CNT flexural modes. Building on prior work on fullerene-filled CNTs, spin-phonon control in suspended nanotubes, and phase-space propagators for damped driven oscillators, we develop a hybrid open-system model combining driven quantum Brownian motion of the CNT with an effective Jaynes-Cummings spin-vibrational interaction. The resonator dynamics are represented by a Wigner function whose evolution is written analytically in terms of the initial Wigner distribution and a Gaussian propagator. This phase-space description separates drive-induced displacement, diffusion, and damping, and connects these processes directly to entropy flow. The coupled spin-mechanical dynamics are embedded in a Lindblad master equation including mechanical damping, spin relaxation, pure dephasing, and thermally activated excitation. Within this framework we derive the entropy balance, identify entropy flux and non-negative entropy production, and examine how spin-vibrational hybridization redistributes irreversibility between coherent exchange and dissipative channels. We show that magnetic-gradient-enhanced spin-phonon coupling, resonant driving, and moderate thermal occupation produce crossovers between oscillator-dominated and spin-dominated entropy-production regimes. The framework provides a basis for using CNT-PEF hybrids as nanoscale platforms to study nonequilibrium quantum thermodynamics, decoherence, and information loss in vibrational environments.

</details>

<a id="paper-2605.16247"></a>
### [Brownian motion: non-equilibrium states from equilibrium trajectories -- recovering hydrodynamic regimes from prepared displacement measurements](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.16247v1)

**Authors:** Jason Boynewicz, Michael C. Thumann, Giuseppe Procopio, Massimiliano Giona  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** statistical mechanics · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.16247v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `Keldysh / 2PI / non-Gaussian methods` **3/5** · `methods for driven-dissipative` **3/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **3/5** · `non-equilibrium universality` **3/5** · `quantum measurements` **3/5** · `correlated / nonlocal dissipation` **2/5** · `driven-dissipative phase transition` **2/5** · `measurement-induced transitions` **1/5**

**Summary.** This work develops a theoretical framework to extract microscopic fluid-particle interaction details from single Brownian trajectories by treating equilibrium states as superpositions of non-equilibrium paths. It analyzes the short-time scaling of the mean-squared displacement, predicting crossovers between $t^{5/2}$ and $t^4$ scaling depending on the inclusion of correlated stochastic forces. This provides a powerful method to test hydrodynamic models using single-trajectory measurements.

**Why it may be interesting.** The rigorous connection between non-equilibrium path measurements and equilibrium hydrodynamic properties is a powerful theoretical tool for characterizing complex many-body dynamics, relevant for understanding dissipation in open quantum systems.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper aims to unravel fine details of fluid-particle interactions at microscopic scales by analyzing non-equilibrium states derived from single Brownian trajectories, connecting them to macroscopic hydrodynamics.

**Main result.** The analysis confirms the $t^{5/2}$-law for short-time displacement statistics due to fluid inertial effects but predicts a crossover to a $t^4$-scaling at very short times when correlated stochastic forcings are properly accounted for.

**Method.** The approach utilizes the Chapman-Kolmogorov equation, viewing equilibrium trajectories as superpositions of non-equilibrium states, and analyzing the second-order moments of particle position via Generalized Langevin Equations (GLEs).

**Model / system.** The system is a Brownian particle moving in a solvent fluid, modeled using concepts from Markovian dynamics, path integrals, and memory kernels derived from hydrodynamic theory.

**Key observables.** Mean square displacement $\langle x^2(t) angle$, velocity autocorrelation function, and scaling exponents ($\phi, \zeta, d_v$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Short time $t$, fluid viscosity ($\eta$), and parameters related to fluid inertia and correlation time.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis relies on the Markovian embedding for non-Markovian processes and involves approximations in the Laplace domain for short-time limits.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the mean-squared displacement $m_{xx}(t)$ vs $t$ for various models, showing crossovers between $t^3$, $t^{5/2}$, and $t^4$ scaling regimes depending on the physical assumptions.

**Paper structure.** The paper builds theory by relating equilibrium paths to non-equilibrium states, develops the mathematical framework using GLEs and memory kernels, and concludes by deriving and comparing the short-time scaling laws for $\langle x^2(t) angle$ under different hydrodynamic assumptions.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Owing to the Chapman-Kolmogorov equation for Markovian dynamics,any equilibrium trajectory of a Brownian particle in a solvent fluid can be viewed as the superposition of an uncountable number of non-equilibrium states. This property permits the unraveling of fine details of fluid-particle interactions at microscales defined by its non-equilibrium properties from the analysis of a single Brownian trajectory and to connect them to the hydrodynamics of the solvent fluid, simply considering the lower-order (second) moments of particle position in trapped conditions. In this way, the acceleration due to thermal-hydrodynamic fluctuational forces is isolated from the other factors and the short-time displacement statistics is completely determined by the correlation properties of the fluctuational thermal-hydrodynamic force. This approach not only confirms the $t^{5/2}$-law obtained by Boynewicz et al. (2026), related to fluid inertial effects, but indicates that this scaling may be superseded by a $t^4$-scaling at very short times once the correlated nature of the stochastic forcings is taken into account. The latter result is related to the regularity properties of particle velocity realizations.

</details>

### [Entanglement Dynamics of Separable Squeezed States in Finite Memory Structured Reservoir](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.15426v1)

**Authors:** Austen Couvertier, Ting Yu

**Summary.** This paper analyzes entanglement dynamics in bosonic systems coupled to structured, memory-retaining reservoirs. By employing advanced Gaussian methods, the authors reveal three unique non-Markovian phenomena—including entanglement revival and integer-locked beating—that are impossible in simple Markovian baths. These findings establish structured reservoirs as controllable resources for continuous-variable quantum technologies.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work provides concrete, analytically derived mechanisms (like integer-locking and freezing laws) showing how structured environments can act as tunable resources for generating and controlling quantum entanglement, which is highly relevant for quantum optics and cavity QED.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study investigates how entanglement dynamics evolve in continuous-variable Gaussian systems when coupled to structured, finite-memory quantum reservoirs, addressing gaps left by Markovian or pre-entangled state assumptions.

**Main result.** The authors identify three non-Markovian mechanisms—detuning-induced freezing, entanglement revival from separable inputs, and integer-locked beating—that are absent in Markovian dynamics.

**Method.** The analysis employs Gaussian covariance methods, evolving the system dynamics using approximate Non-Markovian Quantum State Diffusion (QSD) and utilizing Bures distance diagnostics to quantify non-Markovianity.

**Model / system.** The model consists of two bosonic modes coupled to a common bosonic reservoir, whose memory effects are modeled using an Ornstein–Uhlenbeck kernel. The analysis is extended to finite temperatures via pseudomode embeddings.

**Key observables.** Logarithmic negativity ($E_N$), Bures distance ($D_B$), and the non-Markovianity witness ($N$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Detuning ($\delta_{AB}, \delta_{AE}$), reservoir correlation time ($\gamma$), thermal occupation ($ar{n}$), and modulation amplitude ($\delta_0/\kappa$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis relies on approximate QSD and pseudomode embeddings, though these methods successfully map the dynamics to maintain complete positivity.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the persistence of entanglement revivals and beating envelopes under structured coupling, and show the relative deviation of entanglement metrics across varying thermal occupations.

**Paper structure.** The paper builds from establishing the system Hamiltonian and initial states, developing the non-Markovian evolution equations (using QSD/pseudomodes), and then systematically analyzing the three key non-Markovian phenomena (freezing, revival, beating) under varying temperature and detuning conditions.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Entanglement in continuous-variable Gaussian systems is a key resource, and common reservoirs can both suppress and generate correlations. Existing work focused on pre-entangled states or Markovian baths, leaving open whether separable squeezed inputs entangle in structured environments or under modulation. We study two bosonic modes coupled to a common reservoir, each initialized in a separable squeezed vacuum. Dynamics are analyzed utilizing Gaussian covariance methods, evolved under approximate Non-Markovian quantum state diffusion (QSD), finite-temperature pseudomode embeddings, and Bures-based non-Markovian diagnostics. We identify three mechanisms absent in Markovian dynamics: (1) A detuning condition that freezes entanglement trajectories across reservoir correlation times; (2) birth, death, and revival of entanglement from orthogonal inputs; and (3) integer-locked beating with square-wave oscillations produced by periodic detuning. All mechanisms persist at finite temperature, with deviations bounded within 5% in cryogenic regimes and 20% at moderate occupations. These deviation bounds align with cryogenic cavity, phononic, and optomechanical platforms, where structured spectral densities and detuning modulation are already accessible. Structured reservoirs are shown to emerge as tunable entanglement resources for continuous-variable quantum technologies.

</details>

### [Active Model B$^-$ from Mass-Conserving Reaction-Diffusion Systems](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.15903v1)

**Authors:** Davide Toffenetti, Beatrice Nettuno, Henrik Weyer, Erwin Frey

**Summary.** This work derives Active Model B− (AMB−), an effective scalar field theory, by analyzing the late-time dynamics of a complex three-component mass-conserving reaction-diffusion system. The key finding is that AMB− exhibits a density-dependent interfacial coefficient that drives a finite-wavelength instability, stabilizing microphase separation. The methodology involves advanced field-theoretic reductions, providing a powerful framework for understanding pattern selection in non-equilibrium matter.

**Why it may be interesting.** While focused on classical pattern formation, the mathematical techniques used—deriving an effective, non-linear, gradient-based field theory from underlying conservation laws—are highly relevant to modeling non-equilibrium dynamics in quantum many-body systems or open quantum systems.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper addresses the theoretical challenge of understanding pattern formation in mass-conserving reaction-diffusion systems, specifically aiming to derive an effective active scalar field theory that captures finite-wavelength pattern selection.

**Main result.** The late-time dynamics reduce to Active Model B− (AMB−), a scalar active field theory characterized by a density-dependent interfacial coefficient $\kappa(\phi)$ that turns negative at high density, stabilizing microphase-separated patterns.

**Method.** The derivation uses adiabatic elimination and gradient expansion techniques to reduce a complex three-component mass-conserving system into a single, effective fourth-order partial differential equation.

**Model / system.** The model is based on a minimal three-component mass-conserving reaction-diffusion (McRD) system involving membrane-bound and cytosolic species. The effective theory, AMB−, is a scalar active field theory derived from this underlying conservation law.

**Key observables.** Microphase-separated patterns, finite-wavelength instability, and the structure of the steady-state interface profiles.

**Important parameters / regimes.** The reactivation rate $
u$, which interpolates between different physical regimes (e.g., coarsening vs. stable separation).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The derivation relies on the system being in the late-time, diffusion-limited regime, and involves approximations like gradient expansion and quasi-steady-state elimination.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the reaction scheme, the comparison between different model limits (PFC, 2-comp, AMB−), and the stability analysis showing transitions between different types of interface profiles (monotonic, non-monotonic, etc.).

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses by first defining the complex three-component McRD system, then applying adiabatic elimination and gradient expansion to derive the effective AMB− equation. It analyzes the resulting steady-state ODE via linear stability analysis to characterize the stability regimes and pattern selection mechanisms.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

We show that the late-time dynamics of a minimal three-component mass-conserving reaction--diffusion system reduce to a scalar active field theory, Active Model B$^-$ (AMB$^-$), in which a density-dependent interfacial coefficient $κ(φ)$ turns negative at high density. This drives a finite-wavelength instability and stabilises microphase-separated patterns, in contrast to the unbounded coarsening of two-component mass-conserving systems. Unlike Active Model B$^+$, AMB$^-$ retains a chemical potential that remains a state function, inherited from the underlying conservation law, but admits no equation of state for the pressure.

</details>

<a id="paper-2605.15884"></a>
### [Cycle affinity and winding localize eigenvalues of Markov generators](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.15884v1)

**Authors:** Artemy Kolchinsky, Naruo Ohga, Sosuke Ito  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** statistical mechanics · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.15884v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `correlated / nonlocal dissipation` **3/5** · `driven-dissipative phase transition` **3/5** · `methods for driven-dissipative` **3/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **3/5** · `non-equilibrium universality` **3/5** · `Keldysh / 2PI / non-Gaussian methods` **1/5** · `analog quantum simulation` **1/5**

<div class="figure-strip-hint">↔ Scroll figures horizontally</div>

</figure>
</div>

**Summary.** This work establishes fundamental mathematical bounds on the complex eigenvalues of Markov generators by linking them to the system's non-equilibrium cycles. It proves that the oscillatory behavior of the system is constrained by a tradeoff between thermodynamic driving forces (cycle affinity) and the geometric winding number of the eigenvectors. This provides powerful, generalizable tools for analyzing relaxation dynamics in complex stochastic processes.

**Why it may be interesting.** The connection between spectral properties (eigenvalues) and non-equilibrium thermodynamics (cycle affinity) provides powerful, quantitative bounds on relaxation dynamics, which is crucial for understanding open quantum systems and energy dissipation.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** To understand how complex eigenvalues of Markov generators, which govern oscillatory properties of relaxation and autocorrelation, are localized by non-equilibrium cycles.

**Main result.** The paper proves that complex eigenvalues are confined to regions determined by the cycle affinity and the eigenvector's winding number, establishing new thermodynamic bounds on relaxation modes.

**Method.** The analysis uses spectral theory applied to continuous-time Markov chains, relating eigenvalue properties to cycle affinities and winding numbers via geometric and convex hull constructions.

**Model / system.** The system is modeled by continuous-time Markov chains whose dynamics are governed by a rate matrix (generator) $R$. The analysis focuses on irreducible, unicyclic, and multicyclic generators.

**Key observables.** Complex eigenvalues ($\lambda$), Cycle Affinity ($F$), Winding Number ($\omega(u)$), and the relaxation/oscillation rates ($	ext{Re } \lambda, |	ext{Im } \lambda|$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Cycle affinity ($F$), system size ($n$), and the parameter $	au \in [0, 1]$ used to interpolate between different decay regimes.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The main theorems initially assume the generator is irreducible and unicyclic, though generalizations to multicyclic systems are presented.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the derived eigenvalue bounds, comparing the topology-dependent localization region (Theorem 4) against the simpler, topology-free region defined by the Farey sequence (Theorem 5).

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses from establishing bounds in unicyclic systems (Theorem 1 & 2), to generalizing these bounds to multicyclic systems (Theorem 3), and finally deriving two types of localization regions: one dependent on specific cycle topologies (Theorem 4) and one independent of topology (Theorem 5).

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

The complex eigenvalues of Markov generators govern oscillatory properties of relaxation, autocorrelation, and linear response. Here we show that these eigenvalues are localized by nonequilibrium cycles of the generator, thus revealing a fundamental tradeoff between thermodynamic driving, oscillation, and decay of eigenmodes. Specifically, we prove that each complex eigenvalue is confined to a region determined by the cycle affinity and the eigenvector ``winding number'' of some nonequilibrium cycle. In unicyclic systems, we also demonstrate that the winding number coincides with the ordered eigenvalue index, yielding new thermodynamic bounds on the slowest and fastest relaxation modes. In multicyclic systems, our approach unifies and extends several previous inequalities and proves the Uhl--Seifert ellipse conjecture.

</details>

**Summary.** This paper presents a gauge-engineering technique to achieve robust, tunable selection of specific eigenmodes in non-Hermitian directed-graph networks. By introducing synthetic gauge fields, the authors can force any desired pure decay mode to become the dominant state, bypassing the need for delicate gain/loss balancing. This advances applications in areas like single-mode lasers and quantum sensing.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work provides a powerful, generalizable theoretical tool for controlling spectral properties in open quantum systems, directly relevant to designing next-generation single-mode lasers and quantum sensors.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The core challenge is selectively isolating and controlling individual eigenmodes in open quantum and wave systems, especially in non-Hermitian settings where gain/loss balancing is difficult.

**Main result.** The authors introduce a gauge-engineering method that robustly promotes any desired pure decay mode to the dominant state, enabling loss/gain-free control over mode profiles.

**Method.** The technique involves adding synthetic gauge fields via phase-compensated non-reciprocal hopping to the directed-graph network Hamiltonian.

**Model / system.** The system is modeled using non-Hermitian directed-graph networks. The method is generalized from single-mode selection in fully connected graphs to paired and multi-mode selection in half-connected and higher-dimensional graphs.

**Key observables.** Pure decay modes (eigenstates with smooth exponential amplitude decay), the energy spectrum, and the ability to tune the dominant mode's energy gap.

**Important parameters / regimes.** The gauge field phase ($	heta_k$), the graph connectivity (fully connected, half-connected), and the hopping parameters ($t$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The method relies on the underlying graph geometry supporting pure decay modes, and the gauge field is implemented via phase compensation without altering intrinsic loss/gain.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the emergence of a singular mode in fully connected graphs, demonstrate gauge engineering for single-mode selection, and show extensions to double and multi-mode selection via dimension folding.

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses by first establishing the concept of geometry-protected pure decay modes, then detailing the gauge-engineering technique for single-mode selection, and finally generalizing this control to simultaneous paired and multi-mode selection in higher dimensions.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Non-Hermitian physics enables novel control over open quantum and wave systems, but selectively isolating individual modes without delicate balancing of gain and loss remains challenging. Here we introduce a gauge-engineering method in directed-graph networks that support geometry-protected pure decay modes-eigenstates exhibiting smooth exponential amplitude decay along directed paths. In fully connected configurations, a single dominant mode naturally emerges with a large, tunable energy gap from the rest. By adding synthetic gauge fields via phase-compensated non-reciprocal hopping, we can promote any desired pure decay mode to the dominant position, while preserving its amplitude profile. The approach extends to simultaneous selection of paired modes in half-connected graphs and customizable multi-mode distributions in higher dimensions via orthogonal folding. Our method enables robust, loss/gain-free control over mode profiles, advancing applications in single-mode lasers, sensors, and quantum processing.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

**Summary.** This paper proposes a novel quantum transducer using a single color center in a diamond resonator to convert microwave signals to optical photons. By operating under strong coupling and at ultra-low pump powers, the device achieves high efficiency and enables remote entanglement generation. This work presents a promising, low-power solid-state route for building scalable quantum networks.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work directly addresses a critical hardware bottleneck—the quantum interface—for distributed quantum networks, providing concrete metrics (efficiency, rate, fidelity) for a solid-state platform.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The central challenge is developing an efficient, low-power microwave-to-optical quantum transducer for scalable, fault-tolerant quantum computation at cryogenic temperatures.

**Main result.** The proposed device demonstrates a viable pathway by achieving a coherent conversion efficiency of 0.32 at extremely low pump powers (55 pW), enabling remote entanglement generation at $\sim 1$ kHz with fidelity exceeding 0.9.

**Method.** The analysis uses theoretical modeling involving the steady-state approximation for coupled cavity modes and calculating the single-photon response via Taylor series expansion of the output photon flux.

**Model / system.** The system is a quantum transducer based on a single color center ($	ext{NV}^0$) embedded in a diamond optomechanical resonator, modeled by coupling three resonators: microwave, mechanical, and optical.

**Key observables.** Coherent conversion efficiency ($P_{coh}$), remote entanglement generation rate ($R_{herald}$), and entanglement fidelity ($F_{1c}$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Operating at the triply resonant condition ($\omega_a = \omega_b = \omega_{10}$); low pump power ($\sim 55 	ext{ pW}$); and the comparison between spectral diffusion and conversion timescales ($	au_{diff}$ vs $	au_{conv}$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis assumes the drive frequency component is separable from the converted frequency component by a filter, and the single-photon response is obtained by linearizing the output flux calculation around zero input power.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the mode coupling diagram and the schematic of the transduction process; Figure 2 shows the dependence of total and coherent photon flux on input power, and the impact of spectral diffusion rates on efficiency.

**Paper structure.** The paper first establishes the physical model and Hamiltonian, then derives the input-output relation, calculates the single-photon response by linearizing the output flux, and finally discusses the performance metrics like entanglement generation rate and fidelity, while addressing noise limitations from spectral diffusion.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Scaling up superconducting quantum processors remains a central challenge for realizing fault-tolerant quantum computation. Although distributed architectures based on optical photons offer a promising route to scalability, they require an efficient microwave-to-optical quantum transducer that operates at cryogenic temperatures. Existing approaches typically rely on strong optical pumping, which induces undesirable heating and degrades single-photon coherence. Here, we propose a microwave-to-optical quantum transducer based on double-resonant scattering from a single color center embedded in a diamond optomechanical resonator. We show that strong coupling between the color center and the optical cavity enables coherent conversion at extremely low pump powers on the order of 10 pW. The proposed device enables remote entanglement generation on the order of 1 kHz with a fidelity exceeding 0.9, demonstrating a viable pathway toward ultra-low-power, high-efficiency quantum transducers based on individual solid-state defects for future distributed superconducting quantum networks.

</details>

### [Efficient quantum algorithm for linear matrix differential equations and applications to open quantum systems](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.16195v1)

**Authors:** Sophia Simon, Dominic W. Berry, Rolando D. Somma  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.16195v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `methods for driven-dissipative` **3/5** · `Keldysh / 2PI / non-Gaussian methods` **2/5** · `analog quantum simulation` **2/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **2/5** · `Kondo & dissipative impurity` **1/5** · `correlated / nonlocal dissipation` **1/5** · `driven-dissipative phase transition` **1/5** · `entanglement & information structure` **1/5** · `non-equilibrium universality` **1/5**

**Summary.** This paper presents an efficient quantum algorithm for solving linear matrix differential equations, crucial for simulating open quantum systems. By reformulating the problem as an overlap estimation using history states, it achieves near-optimal query complexity. This advances the field by providing a scalable quantum tool for dynamics that previously suffered from exponential resource requirements.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work provides a concrete, quantum-algorithmic tool for simulating complex, time-dependent open quantum dynamics, directly impacting how quantum simulation algorithms are designed for realistic physical systems.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Developing an efficient, near-optimal quantum algorithm to compute matrix elements of the solution to linear matrix differential equations (LMDES).

**Main result.** The algorithm achieves a query complexity of $\widetilde{\mathcal{O}}(
u\mathcal{L} t/\epsilon)$, which is proven to be optimal up to logarithmic factors.

**Method.** The approach utilizes 'history states' and block-encoding techniques to reduce the differential equation solving problem to an overlap estimation problem, avoiding exponential normalization issues of prior methods.

**Model / system.** The core model is the LMDES, $rac{d}{dt}X(t) = A^\dagger X(t) + X(t)B + C$, which is applied to simulate open quantum systems, such as non-interacting fermions.

**Key observables.** The primary observable is the matrix element $\langle\phi|X(t)|\psiangle$, representing the solution at time $t$.

**Important parameters / regimes.** The complexity depends on the time $t$, the desired precision $\epsilon$, and system-dependent constants $\mathcal{L}$ and $
u$.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis relies on the block-encoding model and often employs the Markovian approximation when simulating open quantum systems.

**Figures summary.** The notes reference comparisons of query costs between the linear systems approach and the LCHS approach in Table II, and complexity scaling in various theorems.

**Paper structure.** The paper develops by first establishing the general framework for LMDES solving, contrasting it with prior methods, then applying it to specific physical models (like free fermions), and finally proving optimality via lower bounds derived from QPE.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

We present an efficient, nearly optimal quantum algorithm for solving linear matrix differential equations, with applications to the simulation of open quantum systems and beyond. For unitary or dissipative dynamics, the algorithm computes an entry of the solution matrix with query complexity $\widetilde{\mathcal{O}}(ν\mathcal{L} t/ε)$, where the constant $ν$ depends on the problem parameters, $\mathcal{L}$ involves a time integral of upper bounds on the norms of evolution operators, and $ε$ is the error. In particular, $ν\mathcal{L}$ is linear in $t$ for unitary dynamics and can be a constant for dissipative dynamics. Our result contrasts prior quantum approaches for differential equations that typically require exponential time for this problem due to the encoding in a quantum state, which can lead to exponentially small amplitudes. We demonstrate the utility of the algorithm through an end-to-end application, namely the simulation of dissipative dynamics for non-interacting fermions, which can be extended to other quantum and classical systems. We compare with classical algorithms and give evidence of polynomial quantum speedups for systems in a lattice, which become more pronounced for systems with long-range interactions and can be shown to be exponential in general. We also provide a lower bound of $Ω(ν\mathcal{L} t/ε)$ for unitary or dissipative dynamics that proves our algorithm is optimal up to logarithmic factors.

</details>

### [Event-based spatiotemporal networks for modelling emergent phenomena in complex systems](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.15798v1)

**Summary.** This paper introduces Event-based Spatiotemporal Networks (EBSTNs) as a superior computational framework for modeling complex systems. By encoding system dynamics as discrete, spatio-temporally anchored events, it overcomes limitations in traditional methods that fail to capture micro-scale heterogeneity. The framework is successfully applied to model epidemic spread and delay propagation in public transport, demonstrating its versatility across diverse physical domains.

**Why it may be interesting.** While the application is in complex systems modeling, the focus on capturing fine-grained, non-equilibrium dynamics and emergent behavior from local interactions shares conceptual overlap with modeling non-equilibrium quantum many-body systems or open quantum systems where local interactions drive global behavior.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Modeling emergent phenomena in complex systems where macroscopic dynamics arise from fine-grained, heterogeneous micro-level processes across space and time.

**Main result.** Event-based spatiotemporal networks (EBSTNs) provide a unified, flexible, and computationally efficient framework to model such emergent behavior.

**Method.** The framework encodes system processes as discrete events anchored in space and time, contrasting with coarse-graining or fixed-time-step network approaches.

**Model / system.** The model is the Event-based Spatiotemporal Network (EBSTN), which captures four types of information per event: location, timing, involved actors, and duration. It is applied to real-world systems like pathogen spread and public transport delays.

**Key observables.** Cumulative pathogen transmissions, normalized entropy of infectious actors, and propagated delays in public transport services.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Pathogen transmission rate (beta), latent/infectious periods, and network parameters like scheduled slack time and exogenous delay distributions.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The primary assumption is that the system's dynamics are best captured by tracking discrete, causal events rather than assuming uniform mixing or fixed spatial/temporal resolutions.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the construction of EBSTNs from real-world trajectories, show pathogen spread simulations in Dutch municipalities, and map delay propagation across a railway network using space-time diagrams.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the EBSTN framework by contrasting it with limitations in traditional network models (TNs, ABMs). It then demonstrates its efficacy through two major case studies: infectious disease epidemiology in the Netherlands and delay propagation in the Zürich S-bahn system, concluding with broader applicability discussions.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Complex systems display emergent phenomena that vary significantly across spatial and temporal scales. These variations originate from fine-grained system processes, yet arriving at macroscopic dynamics from micro-level data -- particularly when large, high-resolution datasets are available -- remains a persistent challenge. Here we develop event-based spatiotemporal networks, a computational modelling framework that encodes system processes as discrete events anchored in space and time. Event-based spatiotemporal networks offer a unified, flexible and efficient approach to generate emergent behaviour in complex systems across space and time from these events. We demonstrate the effectiveness of event-based spatiotemporal networks through two illustrative real-world applications. First, following a local outbreak of a novel respiratory pathogen in the Netherlands, spatiotemporal networks enable fine-grained tracking of transmission routes and infection patterns through space and time. Second, we use spatiotemporal networks to model propagation of delays in a public transportation system (S-bahn) around Zürich, Switzerland. We also discuss broader uses of event-based spatiotemporal networks in fields like developmental biology and community ecology, where focusing on events rather than static system states can improve data analysis, simulation, and collection strategies.

</details>

### [Quantum mechanics for classical transport equations](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.15969v1)

**Authors:** Christof Wetterich  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** statistical mechanics · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.15969v1>

**Summary.** This work establishes a formal quantum mechanical description for classical transport equations governed by probabilistic initial conditions. By mapping the probabilistic evolution onto a complex wave function, the authors show that quantum features, such as interference and non-commuting observables, naturally emerge. This provides a powerful theoretical tool for analyzing complex classical stochastic processes through a quantum lens.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides a formal, non-standard framework for how quantum mechanical principles (like non-commutativity and interference) can manifest within purely classical, probabilistic dynamical systems, which is relevant for understanding open quantum systems.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper investigates the formal connection between classical transport equations with probabilistic initial conditions and the principles of quantum mechanics.

**Main result.** It demonstrates that the quantum formalism can be constructed for these classical probabilistic systems, allowing quantum features like superposition and interference to emerge naturally.

**Method.** The authors construct a complex functional integral and derive a Schrödinger-like equation for the time-local probabilistic information encoded in a classical wave function.

**Model / system.** The model involves classical transport equations, which are treated as probabilistic automata in a discrete setting. The quantum description utilizes a complex wave function $\psi(t, \gamma)$ evolving unitarily.

**Key observables.** Expectation values of observables $\langle A angle$, quantum energy $E_n$, and conserved quantities derived from symmetry generators.

**Important parameters / regimes.** The difference between quantum energy $E_n$ and classical energy $E_{cl}$ is a key diagnostic.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The approach assumes the existence of an invertible transport equation mapping $t 	o t+\epsilon$ and relies on constructing an extended weight distribution $	ilde{w}[\sigma, \gamma]$.

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses from viewing classical transport equations as quantum systems, deriving the Schrödinger equation for the wave function, establishing the role of non-commuting statistical observables, and finally developing the extended weight distribution to unify classical and quantum information.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Classical transport equations with probabilistic initial conditions can be viewed as quantum systems.   In a discrete version they are probabilistic automata. The time-local probabilistic information is encoded in a classical wave function.   Its unitary evolution obeys a Schrödinger equation.   Statistical observables are represented by operators which do not commute with the ones associated to classical observables.   Examples are functions of the quantum energy or the quantum angular momentum. They are important conserved quantities.   We construct a complex functional integral for the quantum system which describes the probabilistic classical transport equation.   The characteristic features of quantum mechanics, as the superposition of wave functions, interference, the importance of phases,   non-commuting operators or a unitary time evolution, are realized by probabilistic classical transport equations.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2605.15283"></a>
### [Stochastic Safety Limits and Scale-Dependent Power Fluctuations in Nuclear Reactors: A Critical Scaling Approach](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.15283v1)

**Authors:** V. V. Ryazanov

<div class="figure-strip-hint">↔ Scroll figures horizontally</div>
<div class="paper-figures-scroll">
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.15283_figures/2605.15283_fig1.jpg" alt="Figure 1">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 1.</b> Fig. 1 shows the dependence of the average time to reach &lt;T&gt;(a)=T(a) the level Φ, calculated using formula (22) with =1.</figcaption>

<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.15283_figures/2605.15283_fig2.jpg" alt="Figure 2">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 2.</b> Fig. 1. Dependence of the average time to reach &lt;T&gt;(a)=T(a) the level Φ= P/P0=0.1, for form (A) Rev. [17], calculated using formula (22).</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.15283_figures/2605.15283_fig3.jpg" alt="Figure 3">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 3.</b> Fig. 2. Dependence of the average time to reach &lt;T&gt;(a)=T(a) the level Φ= P/P0=0.1, for form (A) Rev. [17], calculated using formula (22)-(23) at =0.0932, veff=0.025, =1.</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.15283_figures/2605.15283_fig4.jpg" alt="Figure 4">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 4.</b> Fig. 3. Dependence of the average time to reach &lt;T&gt;(a)=T(a) the level Φ= P P/P0=0.1, for form (A) Rev. [17], calculated using formula (22)-(23) at =0.0632, veff=0.025, =1.</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.15283_figures/2605.15283_fig5.jpg" alt="Figure 5">

</figure>
</div>

**Summary.** This theoretical work applies advanced stochastic process theory, specifically using boundary functionals and Lévy statistics, to assess safety risks in nuclear reactors. It demonstrates that under certain conditions, neutron fluctuations exhibit heavy-tailed power-law behavior characteristic of Directed Percolation rather than simple Gaussian diffusion. This implies that standard safety estimates may underestimate the risk of rapid, localized power surges.

**Why it may be interesting.** The mathematical framework of Lévy flights and Directed Percolation, used here to model classical engineering safety, provides a powerful analogy for understanding non-equilibrium dynamics and anomalous transport in complex quantum or condensed matter systems.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper analyzes stochastic safety limits and scale-dependent power fluctuations in nuclear reactors by modeling the neutron population as a random process.

**Main result.** The system's risk profile is critically dependent on the statistical regime, transitioning from classical Gaussian diffusion to Directed Percolation (DP) when fluctuations exhibit heavy tails, especially at the mesoscale (fuel assembly size).

**Method.** The analysis employs boundary functionals and the Lundberg equation, connecting the process to Lévy-stable distributions via the characteristic function.

**Model / system.** The physical system is a nuclear reactor core, modeled by the stochastic evolution of neutron flux ($\Phi(t)$). The underlying stochastic process is characterized by the distribution of 'effective progenies' ($P(k) \sim k^{-a}$), which dictates the system's statistical behavior.

**Key observables.** First-passage time ($T_{FPT}$), power peaks, catastrophic power surges, and the scaling exponent $a$ of the power-law distribution.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Stability index ($\alpha$), power-law exponent ($a$), system size ($L$), and the asymmetry parameter ($eta$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis assumes the applicability of Lévy-stable process theory and models the transition between different stochastic regimes based on the divergence of higher moments.

**Figures summary.** The notes reference tables comparing safety time $\langle T angle$ across different asymmetry ($eta$) and scaling ($a$) regimes, showing significant reductions in safety margin when $eta=1$ and $1 < a < 2$.

**Paper structure.** The paper builds from general boundary functional theory to specific applications in reactor physics, contrasting Gaussian (classical) behavior with Lévy-DP regimes, and finally quantifying the impact of asymmetry ($eta$) and finite size ($L$) on the mean time to failure.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Applying boundary functionals of random risk processes to various physical problems makes it possible to determine many important characteristics of these problems. For example, a special case of boundary functionals is the time to first reach a level, which is widely and successfully applied to a variety of problems. We consider the application of boundary functionals to solving nuclear safety problems. In situations such as reactor startup, as well as for certain types of reactors, neutron behavior changes. Neutron clustering begins to play an important role, and the distributions characterizing neutron behavior change. The normal Gaussian distribution is replaced by stable limiting, distributions to which the sums of random variables converge. Boundary functionals allow us to accurately calculate the statistics of random events, determine the behavior of reactor power peaks, the probabilities of catastrophic power surges, and other quantities important for reactor safety, providing a mathematical bridge between the abstract theory of directed percolation and engineering calculations of protection parameters. This article examines the first-passage time to reach a certain level.

</details>

<a id="paper-2605.15968"></a>
### [Anomalous Transport from Effective Field Theory](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.15968v1)

**Authors:** Rémy Larue, Amaury Marchon, Jérémie Quevillon, Diego Saviot  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** statistical mechanics · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.15968v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `Keldysh / 2PI / non-Gaussian methods` **3/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **3/5** · `methods for driven-dissipative` **2/5** · `analog quantum simulation` **1/5** · `driven-dissipative phase transition` **1/5** · `non-equilibrium universality` **1/5**

<div class="figure-strip-hint">↔ Scroll figures horizontally</div>
<div class="paper-figures-scroll">
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.15968_figures/2605.15968_fig1.jpg" alt="Figure 1">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 1.</b> Figure 1. Consistent axial anomaly (black) and its repartition between the divergence of the axial current (blue) and the mass explicit breaking (brown).</figcaption>
</figure>
</div>

**Summary.** This work provides a rigorous, systematic calculation of anomalous transport effects in a fermionic system at finite temperature. By using an Effective Field Theory approach, the authors derive master formulae for the vector and axial currents and their anomalies. The key finding is that physical transport currents are generally not simply determined by the topological Chern-Simons terms, especially when mass corrections are included.

**Why it may be interesting.** While focused on high-energy QFT, the systematic use of EFT and the study of non-equilibrium transport coefficients (like anomalous currents) provide rigorous theoretical tools applicable to understanding thermalization and transport in strongly interacting quantum systems, which has parallels with non-equilibrium statistical mechanics.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper systematically studies chiral effects and anomalous transport phenomena in a fermionic system at finite temperature using an Effective Field Theory (EFT) framework.

**Main result.** The physical currents and anomalies are found to be complex, generally not reducible to simple Chern-Simons terms, and their calculation requires careful handling of regularization schemes, especially when considering finite mass corrections.

**Method.** The analysis employs the path-integral formalism, utilizing the Covariant Derivative Expansion (CDE) in imaginary time, and involves advanced techniques like Mellin transformations and dimensional regularization.

**Model / system.** The system is a fermion fluid described by a path integral coupled to background vector and axial gauge fields at finite temperature (T) and potentially finite mass (m).

**Key observables.** Vector and axial currents ($\langle j^V angle, \langle j^A angle$), and their associated chiral anomalies ($\mathcal{A}_V, \mathcal{A}_A$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Finite temperature (T), fermion mass (m), and background gauge fields (V, A). The analysis explores limits like $T \gg m$ and $m/T 	o \infty$.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis relies on the EFT framework, assumes the background fields are slowly varying, and often compares results derived from consistent versus covariant anomaly definitions.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the dependence of currents and anomalies on the ratio $m/T$, showing how the physical currents deviate from simple Chern-Simons forms unless the mass is very large.

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses by first establishing the EFT framework and anomalous Ward identities, then calculating the effective actions and currents in the massless limit, and finally incorporating mass corrections and comparing the results to known topological forms.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

A systematic study of chiral effects is presented using an Effective Field Theory framework. By integrating out a massive Dirac fermion at finite temperature in presence of vector and axial background fields, the currents and their anomalies are computed from the path-integral. Chiral effects previously considered separately naturally arise in a unified computation, including new mass corrections. The link between each anomalous transport effect and the anomalies is clearly established, beyond the identification of their coefficients. In particular, we can appreciate how these effects are sourced by the anomalous nature of the theory even in configurations where the anomaly itself vanishes. The consistent and covariant anomalies are both encapsulated in master formulae for the currents which result from a careful treatment of the regularisation. It is finally found that, at finite temperature, the physical currents cannot be inferred simply from the Chern-Simons terms whose divergence reproduce the chiral anomalies.

</details>

<a id="paper-2605.15914"></a>
### [The Directed Abelian Sandpile Model on Cylinders](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.15914v1)

**Authors:** Abdul Quadir, Nikita Kalinin, Ram Ramaswamy  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** statistical mechanics · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.15914v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `driven-dissipative phase transition` **3/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **3/5** · `non-equilibrium universality` **3/5** · `methods for driven-dissipative` **2/5**

<div class="figure-strip-hint">↔ Scroll figures horizontally</div>
<div class="paper-figures-scroll">
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.15914_figures/2605.15914_fig1.jpg" alt="Figure 1">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 1.</b> FIG. 1. Directed cylindrical sandpile of size n × L. Sites are periodic in the transverse (y) direction. Each toppling sends one grain to (x + 1, y) and one to (x, y ± 1). Grains leaving at x = n + 1 are absorbed at the sink, s (not shown). The red and green color sites show the driven and dissipation site respectively.</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.15914_figures/2605.15914_fig2.jpg" alt="Figure 2">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 2.</b> FIG. 2. Exponential growth of the mean return time (equiva- lently, the number of recurrent configurations) with the trans- verse width L for n = 1. The dashed line shows the exponen- tial fit |S(1, L)| ∼eb1L, where the fitted exponent is close to the theoretical value b1 = log(r+) = 0.96242.</figcaption>
</figure>
</div>

**Summary.** This work provides a rigorous mathematical characterization of the Directed Abelian Sandpile Model on a cylinder by identifying its sandpile group using the Laplacian matrix. The authors show that the group's algebraic structure dictates both the deterministic periodicity and the statistical nature of the system's evolution. This bridges abstract algebra with non-equilibrium statistical physics.

**Why it may be interesting.** The deep connection established between the algebraic invariants (sandpile group) and the non-equilibrium dynamics (periodicity/fluctuations) provides a powerful mathematical framework applicable to other driven, dissipative lattice models relevant in non-equilibrium statistical mechanics.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper investigates the algebraic structure and dynamic behavior of the Directed Abelian Sandpile Model (DASM) on a cylindrical lattice.

**Main result.** The sandpile group structure is determined by the co-kernel of the reduced directed Laplacian, and this algebraic structure governs both the deterministic periodicity and the stochastic fluctuations of the system.

**Method.** The analysis relies heavily on algebraic graph theory, specifically identifying the sandpile group using the Smith normal form of the Laplacian matrix, and applying spectral analysis to determine group invariants.

**Model / system.** The system is the Directed Abelian Sandpile Model (DASM) defined on a 2D lattice shaped as a cylinder, featuring periodic boundary conditions in one transverse direction and dissipation at a boundary sink.

**Key observables.** The size of the sandpile group $|S(n, L)|$, the period of deterministic driving, and the structure of the cyclic decomposition of the group.

**Important parameters / regimes.** The lattice dimensions ($n 	imes L$), the parity of the transverse width $L$, and the driving mechanism (fixed vs. random).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis focuses on the cylindrical geometry, although the results suggest extensions to more general directed graphs.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 illustrates the directed cylindrical sandpile setup. Figure 2 shows the exponential growth of the mean return time for the $n=1$ case, confirming theoretical predictions.

**Paper structure.** The paper proceeds by defining the model and identifying the sandpile group via the Laplacian. It then uses algebraic techniques (Smith normal form) to characterize the group structure, linking this structure to the periodicity of deterministic driving and the random walk dynamics.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

We study the abelian sandpile model in two dimensions on a directed cylindrical lattice with periodic transverse boundary conditions in the transverse direction and dissipation at one boundary. Recurrent configurations form a finite abelian group, and repeated grain addition at a specific site generates deterministic dynamics on this group. Using Dhar's formulation, the sandpile group is identified with the co-kernel of the reduced directed Laplacian. We show that the group structure admits an exact reduction to a transverse problem, allowing complete determination of its cyclic decomposition. Our results establish a direct connection between the algebraic structure of the sandpile group and the periodicity of the driven dynamics, establishing the manner in which the underlying algebraic structure governs both deterministic and stochastic evolution in directed sandpile.

</details>
