<a id="paper-2605.16149"></a>
### [Beyond Maxwell-Boltzmann: Transport in Quasiequilibrium Plasmas](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.16149v1)

**Authors:** Kamel Ourabah  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** statistical mechanics · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.16149v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `non-equilibrium dynamics` **3/5** · `non-equilibrium universality` **3/5** · `Keldysh / 2PI / non-Gaussian methods` **2/5** · `methods for driven-dissipative` **2/5**

<div class="figure-strip-hint">↔ Scroll figures horizontally</div>
<div class="paper-figures-scroll">
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.16149_figures/2605.16149_fig1.jpg" alt="Figure 1">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 1.</b> Figure 1. The system undergoes four successive stages: an initial linear regime, a nonlinear phase (often referred to as violent relaxation), a long-lived quasiequilibrium state, and fi- nally a collisional thermal equilibrium. The quasiequilibrium phase is characterized by a slow increase in coarse-grained entropy and can be effectively described within superstatis- tics. The final state, corresponding to maximal entropy, is governed by standard MB statistics.</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.16149_figures/2605.16149_fig2.jpg" alt="Figure 2">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 2.</b> Figure 2. The top panels show the three universality classes f(β): (a) χ2, (b) inverse χ2, and (c) log-normal. The bottom panels display the corresponding velocity distributions. All distributions are parametrized by q := ⟨β2⟩/β2 0. In the limit q →1, f(β) collapses to a Dirac delta function, and the associated velocity distribution reduces to the Maxwellian distribution.</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.16149_figures/2605.16149_fig3.jpg" alt="Figure 3">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 3.</b> Figure 3. Electron velocity distribution functions in the solar wind: (a) data from Feldman et al. [72] and (b) data from Salem et al. [73], together with the best fits obtained from the χ2 class (solid black line) and the log-normal class (dashed red line). The corresponding fitted parameters are given in the text.</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.16149_figures/2605.16149_fig4.jpg" alt="Figure 4">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 4.</b> Figure 4. Electric conductivity ratio σ(q)/σ as a function of q := ⟨β2⟩/β2 0 for the three universality classes of superstatis- tics.</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.16149_figures/2605.16149_fig5.jpg" alt="Figure 5">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 5.</b> Figure 5. Thermoelectric coefficient ratio α(q)/α as a function of q for the three universality classes of superstatistics.</figcaption>
</figure>
</div>

**Summary.** This work develops a theoretical framework using superstatistics to model transport in non-Maxwellian plasmas found in space environments. It derives explicit functional forms showing that these non-equilibrium fluctuations systematically enhance key transport coefficients like conductivity and viscosity compared to classical predictions. The findings provide a quantitative tool for understanding energy and momentum transport in complex, non-equilibrium astrophysical plasmas.

**Why it may be interesting.** While focused on classical plasma physics, the mathematical machinery of superstatistics and non-equilibrium statistical mechanics is highly relevant to understanding complex, non-Gaussian dynamics in open quantum systems or many-body systems far from equilibrium.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper addresses the challenge of modeling plasma transport in quasiequilibrium states where particle velocity distributions deviate significantly from the standard Maxwell-Boltzmann form.

**Main result.** Superstatistical fluctuations systematically enhance all calculated transport coefficients (conductivity, diffusion, viscosity) compared to their Maxwellian counterparts, with the enhancement quantified across three major universality classes.

**Method.** The analysis employs linear response theory applied to the Boltzmann equation, utilizing the superstatistical framework to average over local equilibrium statistics.

**Model / system.** The system is a plasma (e.g., solar wind) assumed to be in a quasiequilibrium state, described statistically as a continuous superposition of local Maxwellian distributions weighted by a distribution function f(beta).

**Key observables.** Electric and thermal conductivities, mobility, diffusion coefficient, shear viscosity, and bulk viscosity coefficients.

**Important parameters / regimes.** The superstatistical parameter $q = \langleeta^2angle/eta_0^2$, which quantifies the deviation from equilibrium ($q=1$ for MB).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The plasma is assumed to be in a quasiequilibrium state, and the analysis is performed within the linear response regime.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the evolution stages of plasma dynamics, compare the three superstatistical distributions ($\chi^2$, Log-normal, Inv. $\chi^2$) to solar wind data, and plot the normalized ratios of transport coefficients (e.g., $D(q)/D$, $\sigma(q)/\sigma$) versus the fluctuation parameter $q$.

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses from establishing the need for superstatistics to derive macroscopic relations, then derives specific transport coefficients (conductivity, diffusion), and finally extends the analysis to compute the shear and bulk viscosity coefficients.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Space plasmas are generally characterized by non-Maxwellian distributions with suprathermal populations, as routinely revealed by in situ observations. Such departures from standard Maxwellian distributions can be understood as signatures of quasiequilibrium states, in which the distribution of the medium can be expressed as a continuous superposition of Maxwellian distributions, namely through superstatistics. Here, we construct macroscopic relations linking fluxes to their associated driving forces in such plasmas, where superstatistical effects enter the picture through the transport coefficients. After comparing the resulting superstatistical distributions with observed electron distributions in the solar wind, we turn to the kinetic response of quasiequilibrium plasmas and derive the corresponding transport coefficients, including the electric and thermal conductivities, the mobility, and the diffusion coefficient. We further extend the analysis to viscous plasmas and compute the shear and bulk viscosity coefficients. Overall, quasiequilibrium effects are found to systematically enhance the transport coefficients relative to their Maxwellian values. We quantify this enhancement for the three main universality classes of superstatistics, which are the most commonly encountered in experimental and observational situations, and interpret it as a consequence of the increased population of energetic particles in the non-Maxwellian tails.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2605.15929"></a>
### [Experimental subdiffraction source discrimination enabled by spatial demultiplexing and single-photon detectors](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.15929v1)

**Authors:** Luigi Santamaria Amato, Danilo Triggiani, Cosmo Lupo  
**Type:** both · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.15929v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `quantum measurements` **3/5** · `quantum optics experiment` **3/5** · `interference shaping light` **2/5** · `QC/QI experiment` **1/5** · `entanglement & information structure` **1/5**

<div class="figure-strip-hint">↔ Scroll figures horizontally</div>
<div class="paper-figures-scroll">
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.15929_figures/2605.15929_fig1.jpg" alt="Figure 1">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 1.</b> FIG. 2. Representation of the optical setup employed for the experiment. A=LED A, B=LED B, FC=fiber collimator, L=Lens, P1=rotatable polarizer, M=Mirrors, BS=beam splitter, P2=fixed polarizer, RS=rotation stage, TS=translation stage, NW=nanowire detector, TT=time tagger.</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.15929_figures/2605.15929_fig2.jpg" alt="Figure 2">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 2.</b> FIG. 1. (Top) Plots of the quantum relative entropy DQ in Eq. (1) (solid blue) and of the relative entropies for di- rect imaging DDI in Eq. (2) (orange dashed) and SPADE detection DSD in Eq. (5) (green dash-dotted and red dotted) while varying the distances da (left) and the crosstalk C10 (right). On the right, we notice DSD &gt; DDI for C10 ≲0.1, namely SPADE outperforms direct imaging with commonly achievable experimental values of crosstalk. ϵ = 0.042, C01 = C10. (Bottom) Plot of the ratio DSD/DDI while varying the crosstalk factors C10 and C01. The black curve delimits the region where SPADE outperforms direct imaging.</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.15929_figures/2605.15929_fig3.jpg" alt="Figure 3">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 3.</b> FIG. 3. Plots of the theoretically predicted error rate βth in Eq. (8) (orange sold line), and the measured error rates of our experimental SPADE-based hypothesis test (blue dots). In dashed green, the theoretical error rate βDI in Eq. (9) of an optimal test with same values of α and N based on direct imaging.</figcaption>
</figure>
</div>

**Summary.** This work experimentally and theoretically demonstrates that using Spatial Demultiplexing (SPADE) with single-photon detectors allows for source discrimination beyond the diffraction limit. The method provides a superior test compared to direct imaging, especially when accounting for realistic optical imperfections like crosstalk. This paves the way for highly sensitive, photon-starved astronomical imaging.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides a concrete, experimentally validated quantum advantage in photon-starved imaging tasks, demonstrating how quantum measurement techniques can overcome fundamental diffraction limits.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper addresses the challenge of discriminating between faint and bright optical sources (like exoplanets near a star) when the sources are separated by distances smaller than the diffraction limit.

**Main result.** Spatial Demultiplexing (SPADE) combined with single-photon detectors provides a universal, parameter-independent test that significantly outperforms direct imaging, especially when realistic crosstalk is present.

**Method.** The authors employ quantum hypothesis testing, comparing the achievable error rate (false negative) using SPADE versus direct imaging, modeled via relative entropy calculations.

**Model / system.** The system is modeled using two fiber-coupled LEDs simulating a star and an exoplanet, analyzed through the lens of quantum relative entropy and hypothesis testing theory.

**Key observables.** False negative error rate ($eta$), Quantum Relative Entropy ($D_Q$), and the ratio of distinguishability measures ($D_{SD}/D_{DI}$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Crosstalk coefficients ($C_{10}, C_{01}$), source separation ($d_a$), and intensity ratio ($\epsilon$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis relies on Gaussian approximations for error rate calculations and assumes small source separations and low intensity ratios for the theoretical derivations.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 plots relative entropies ($D_Q, D_{DI}, D_{SD}$) showing SPADE's advantage for low crosstalk; Figure 3 compares predicted vs. measured error rates for SPADE vs. direct imaging.

**Paper structure.** The paper develops the theory by comparing the quantum relative entropy for two hypotheses (absence vs. presence of a faint source), derives the error rates for both SPADE and direct imaging, and validates the theory with an experimental setup.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

We experimentally demonstrate a universal, parameter-independent test for asymmetric source discrimination. The test allows us to discriminate faint sources well beyond the diffraction limit by exploiting spatial mode demultiplexing (SPADE) and single-photon detectors. Our test yields a rate of false negatives well below what can be achieved by diffraction-limited direct imaging. Our tabletop experimental setup is inspired by the problem of exoplanet detection, where one aims at detecting the presence of a faint source in the proximity of a brighter one. We present a complete theory, modelling arbitrary modal crosstalk, and collect data across a range of values for the source separations and intensity ratios. We show that SPADE retains an advantage over direct imaging in the relevant regime of small separations and low intensity ratios. Remarkably, we identify an experimentally accessible crosstalk threshold $C_{\mathrm{th}}\simeq 0.1$ below which the exponential rate of false negatives stays well below that of direct imaging. For example, for crosstalk of $10^{-2}$, SPADE needs up to one order of magnitude fewer photons than direct imaging to achieve the same error rate. These results demonstrate that SPADE offers an effective methodology for subdiffraction asymmetric hypothesis testing, under realistic imperfections and crosstalk, paving the way to photon-starved imaging tasks.

</details>

<a id="paper-2605.15930"></a>
### [Bak--Tang--Wiesenfeld model for various topologies and ranges of interaction](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.15930v1)

</figure>
</div>

**Summary.** This paper investigates the universality of the critical exponent in the Bak-Tang-Wiesenfeld sandpile model across different lattice structures and interaction rules. Using extensive computer simulations, the authors demonstrate that the exponent governing avalanche size distributions remains constant, suggesting a robust, universal critical behavior independent of the specific underlying geometry, provided system parameters are scaled correctly.

**Why it may be interesting.** While focused on classical sandpile dynamics, the study of universal exponents ($	au$) in non-equilibrium critical phenomena is a core concept in statistical mechanics, relevant to understanding phase transitions and critical behavior in complex quantum many-body systems.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper reconsiders the Bak-Tang-Wiesenfeld (BTW) model to study how varying substrate topologies and interaction ranges affect the power-law distribution of avalanche sizes.

**Main result.** The critical exponent governing the avalanche size distribution ($	au \approx 1.208(39)$) is found to be statistically universal, remaining constant regardless of the substrate topology or interaction range, provided the deposition ratio ($N/L^2$) is correctly chosen.

**Method.** The study employs computer simulations (Monte Carlo) to model the sandpile dynamics and analyzes the resulting probability distribution $P(s)$ using power-law fitting and Z-score statistical tests.

**Model / system.** The system is the BTW sandpile model, where grains are added to a lattice (square, triangular, or honeycomb) until an avalanche occurs, causing material to fall to neighbors based on defined interaction ranges (nearest vs. next-nearest neighbors).

**Key observables.** The power-law exponent $	au$ governing $P(s) \propto s^{-	au}$, and the statistical consistency of $	au$ across different lattice setups.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Substrate topologies (sq, tr, hc), interaction ranges (1NN, 2NN), and the ratio of deposited particles to system size ($N/L^2$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The observation of the power law is highly dependent on maintaining a specific, reasonably chosen ratio between the number of deposited particles ($N$) and the system size ($L^2$).

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the various lattices and neighborhoods, show the log-log binned probability distributions $P(s)$ for different setups, and Tables summarize the calculated exponent $	au$ and statistical comparison values.

**Paper structure.** The paper systematically tests the BTW model across multiple lattice types and interaction rules, using simulations to fit the power-law exponent $	au$ and statistically confirming its independence from the specific lattice geometry when system parameters are controlled.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

In this paper, the Bak--Tang--Wiesenfeld model for various substrate topologies and a variety of neighborhoods is reconsidered. With computer simulation, we study the distribution of avalanche sizes. Using the Z-score we confirm that independently of the substrate topology and the range of neighborhood, the exponent that governs the power law of the probability distribution of the size of avalanches is the same and approximately equal 1.208(39). However, this requires a smartly chosen number of deposited grains in relation to the linear size of the system.

</details>

**Summary.** This work details the Spherically Symmetric Self-Consistent Approach (RGM) for studying low-dimensional magnets, especially those with frustration. It applies this mean-field Green's function technique to Heisenberg models on lattices like the square lattice. The method successfully maps out complex magnetic phase diagrams, providing theoretical insights into magnetic ordering beyond the limitations of traditional analyses.

**Why it may be interesting.** While focused on magnetism, the use of advanced Green's function techniques and self-consistent mean-field theory provides a robust theoretical framework for analyzing complex many-body interactions, which has parallels in open quantum system dynamics.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper addresses the theoretical study of low-dimensional magnetic systems, particularly those exhibiting geometric frustration, while respecting fundamental constraints like the Mermin-Wagner and Marshall theorems.

**Main result.** The spherically symmetric self-consistent approach (RGM) provides a consistent framework to analyze various spin models, yielding predictions for magnetic phases (FM, AFM, helices) in the classical limit and for quantum spin systems.

**Method.** The core method is the Spherically Symmetric Self-Consistent Approach (SSCA) applied to the two-time retarded spin-spin Green's function, which is a mean-field technique.

**Model / system.** The primary models studied are low-dimensional Heisenberg-type spin models, including the $J_1-J_2-J_3$ Hamiltonian on a square lattice. The approach is also applicable to spin-pseudospin models and embedded in models with free carriers like the Hubbard or $t-J$ models.

**Key observables.** Spin-spin correlators, spin excitation spectrum ($\omega_q$), magnetic order (FM, AFM, stripe, helical phases), and static magnetic susceptibility ($\chi$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Temperature ($T$), exchange coupling ratios ($J_1, J_2, J_3$), and spin magnitude ($S$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The RGM assumes spherical symmetry (singlet state) and is fundamentally a mean-field approach, which limits its ability to calculate spin excitation damping.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate phase diagrams for the $J_1-J_2$ and $J_1-J_2-J_3$ models in the classical limit, showing the boundaries between different magnetic phases (AFM, stripe, FM, helicoids).

**Paper structure.** The paper develops the SSCA/RGM algorithm by calculating the spin-spin Green's function, applying it to the $J_1-J_2-J_3$ Hamiltonian, and then analyzing the resulting phase diagram across different temperature and coupling regimes.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

The main ideas and some of the most important results of the spherically symmetric self-consistent approach and a number of related theoretical algorithms are presented. These methods make it possible to study low-dimensional Heisenberg-type spin models, including frustrated ones, with careful consideration of the theoretic (Mermin-Wagner and Marshall) theorems, as well as the site spin constraint. Thus, the difficulties that may arise in the traditional analysis of low-dimensional magnetic systems are avoided. The approach can also be applied to the spin-pseudospin model, and is also embedded in more complex constructions when considering spin models with free carriers, such as the basic and three-band Hubbard models, t-J and s-d models, and the Kondo lattice.

</details>

### [The fractal dimension of Brownian dynamics in liquids](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.16252v1)

**Summary.** This paper investigates how fluid inertia modifies the expected fractal scaling of Brownian motion, moving beyond the classical Einstein-Langevin prediction. Through a combination of high-resolution experiments and advanced theoretical modeling, the authors demonstrate that the true velocity fractal dimension is $d_v = 7/4$. This work establishes a non-equilibrium universality class for particle dynamics in viscous fluids.

**Why it may be interesting.** The work provides a detailed, quantitative study of non-equilibrium dynamics in a classical system, using advanced scaling analysis techniques that are highly relevant to understanding memory effects in open quantum systems or complex many-body dynamics.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** To revise the understanding of the fractal dimension of Brownian velocity fluctuations, moving beyond the classical Einstein-Langevin theory prediction.

**Main result.** The inclusion of fluid-inertial memory effects fundamentally changes the scaling, establishing a distinct velocity fractal dimension of $d_v = 7/4$ instead of the classical $3/2$.

**Method.** The study combines experimental measurements of microspheres in liquids with theoretical analysis using generalized Langevin equations and scale-dependent fluctuation analysis.

**Model / system.** The system is Brownian microspheres moving in a liquid, modeled by incorporating fluid-inertial memory effects via the Basset kernel, moving beyond the simple Markovian Langevin assumption.

**Key observables.** Velocity fractal dimension ($d_v$), scaling exponent $\phi$ from Mean Squared Displacement ($	ext{MSD} \sim 	au^\phi$), and the scale-dependent absolute slope ($S_v(\Delta t)$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** The analysis distinguishes between the classical (Einstein-Langevin) and hydrodynamic (Basset-Boussinesq) regimes, focusing on short-time, non-equilibrium dynamics.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis relies on relating scaling exponents ($\phi, H_v, d_v$) and requires careful handling of short-time singularities and experimental noise/resolution limits.

**Figures summary.** Figures compare scaling functions ($L_x, S_x, L_v, S_v$) for different models, showing crossovers in scaling behavior and confirming the $d_v = 7/4$ result from experimental data.

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses from establishing the classical theory, introducing the non-Markovian hydrodynamic model, developing advanced measurement techniques (like $S_v(\Delta t)$), and finally deriving the non-equilibrium universality class using scaling analysis.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

The classical Einstein-Langevin theory of Brownian motion assumes a memoryless thermal bath, establishing a universal fractal dimension of $d_v = 3/2$ for the velocity fluctuations of a particle. In this Letter, we demonstrate experimentally and theoretically that fluid-inertial memory effects fundamentally redefine the fractal scaling of these fluctuations. In analyzing highly resolved measurements of Brownian microspheres in liquids, we show that the non-Markovian hydrodynamic thermal noise establishes a distinct velocity fractal dimension of $d_v = 7/4$. Coupled with theoretical analysis of non-equilibrium short-time dynamics and the initial scaling of the velocity autocorrelation function, this result establishes the non-equilibrium universality class of Brownian motion in fluid media possessing a finite non-vanishing density.

</details>

<a id="paper-2605.16148"></a>
### [A reason why we do not observe Schrödinger's cats](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.16148v1)

**Authors:** Fabio Siringo  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** statistical mechanics · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.16148v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `quantum measurements` **3/5** · `Keldysh / 2PI / non-Gaussian methods` **1/5** · `correlated / nonlocal dissipation` **1/5** · `measurement-induced transitions` **1/5** · `methods for driven-dissipative` **1/5**

<div class="figure-strip-hint">↔ Scroll figures horizontally</div>
<div class="paper-figures-scroll">
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.16148_figures/2605.16148_page2.jpg" alt="Figure 1">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 1.</b> Low-resolution page preview, page 2</figcaption>

<figure class="paper-figure-card">

</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.16148_figures/2605.16148_page4.jpg" alt="Figure 3">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 3.</b> Low-resolution page preview, page 4</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.16148_figures/2605.16148_page5.jpg" alt="Figure 4">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 4.</b> Low-resolution page preview, page 5</figcaption>

<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.16148_figures/2605.16148_page6.jpg" alt="Figure 5">

</div>

**Summary.** This paper proposes a dynamical resolution to the quantum measurement problem by showing that the standard Schrödinger equation leads to a stochastic equation for macroscopic variables. By analyzing this equation using Itô calculus, the authors demonstrate that any macroscopic superposition will rapidly collapse into a definite state, thereby deriving the Born rule without explicit postulation.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work offers a non-standard, dynamical approach to the measurement problem, providing a mechanism rooted in statistical physics (stochastic processes) that naturally enforces the Born rule for macroscopic systems.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper addresses the measurement problem by providing a dynamical reason why macroscopic quantum superpositions, such as Schrödinger's cat, are not observed in reality.

**Main result.** The standard Schrödinger equation, when analyzed through a stochastic framework, predicts that any macroscopic superposition will collapse almost instantaneously, and this collapse process naturally yields the Born rule.

**Method.** The analysis involves decoupling macroscopic and microscopic degrees of freedom and deriving a stochastic differential equation (SDE) for the macroscopic amplitudes, which is then analyzed using Itô calculus.

**Model / system.** The model uses the standard Schrödinger equation for a quantum system treated as a superposition of macrostates. The dynamics are mapped onto an effective stochastic equation whose noise term is self-generated by the microscopic ensemble average.

**Key observables.** The decay of off-diagonal coherence terms ($\langle p_k(t) p_m(t) angle$) and the recovery of the Born rule ($\langle p_m(t) angle = p_m(0)$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** The ratio of the inertial relaxation time ($	au_R$) to the noise correlation time ($	au_c$), and the macroscopic constraint $\Delta E \Delta X \gg \hbar c$.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The core assumption is that the macroscopic state can be treated as a statistical ensemble of microstates, and the derivation relies on the white noise approximation for the random matrix elements.

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses by deriving a stochastic equation from the Schrödinger dynamics, analyzing the required mathematical interpretation (Itô vs. Stratonovich) based on physical time scales, and finally demonstrating that the Itô interpretation leads to rapid collapse consistent with observation.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

A reason is discussed (may be not the only one) for why we do not see any superposition of macroscopic states in the real world. Under the general assumption that quantum macrostates are statistical ensembles of microstates, it is shown that any superposition of macrostates is reduced in a very short time by the unitary dynamics of the ordinary Schrödinger equation, deducing the Born rule without having to postulate it. In more detail, the macroscopic and microscopic degrees of freedom are decoupled in the Schrödinger equation, yielding an effective stochastic equation for the macroscopic variables, with the ensemble average of the microscopic amplitudes that acts as a self-generated internal white noise. The stochastic equation is shown to be a reducing Itô equation if some general causality conditions are met, predicting a very quick collapse of any macroscopic superposition upon formation, with probabilities which satisfy the Born rule. In the context of the von Neumann measurement scheme, the relevance of the result is discussed as a simple dynamical solution of the measurement problem.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2605.15899"></a>
### [Solving Classical and Quantum Spin Glasses with Deep Boltzmann Quantum States](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.15899v1)

**Authors:** Luca Leone, Arka Dutta, Markus Heyl, Enrico Prati, Pietro Torta  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** disordered systems and neural networks · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.15899v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `exotic spin models in light-matter` **3/5** · `analog quantum simulation` **2/5** · `methods for driven-dissipative` **2/5**

**Summary.** This work proposes Deep Boltzmann Quantum States (DBQS) as a powerful variational ansatz for simulating quantum many-body systems, particularly spin glasses. By integrating DBQS into a Neural Quantum Annealing (NQA) framework, the authors overcome the sampling and optimization hurdles associated with these disordered systems. The method achieves state-of-the-art results on complex models, including solving NP-hard scheduling problems.

**Why it may be interesting.** The combination of deep learning architectures (DBMs) with quantum many-body physics provides a powerful, non-adiabatic route to solving hard optimization problems that challenge traditional quantum simulation methods.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper addresses the difficulty of finding ground states for classical and quantum spin glasses due to their rugged energy landscapes with exponentially many local minima.

**Main result.** The authors successfully solved instances of classical and quantum Ising spin-glass models and even the NP-hard Job Shop Scheduling Problem, exceeding current quantum annealing hardware limitations.

**Method.** They introduce Deep Boltzmann Quantum States (DBQS) as a variational ansatz, trained using Neural Quantum Annealing (NQA) combined with natural-gradient updates and efficient block-Gibbs sampling.

**Model / system.** The focus is on spin glass models, such as the Ising spin-glass Hamiltonian, which are characterized by disorder and energy frustration. The state is parameterized by the DBQS, which extends Boltzmann Machines to the quantum domain.

**Key observables.** Variational energy $E(	heta)$, local energy $E_{	ext{loc}}(x; 	heta)$, and the ability to calculate these efficiently via matrix multiplications.

**Important parameters / regimes.** The annealing schedule parameter $s$ (interpolating from easy to hard Hamiltonian regimes), and optimization hyperparameters (e.g., learning rates, momentum $\mu$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The primary assumption is that the DBQS ansatz, combined with NQA, provides a sufficiently expressive and computationally tractable representation of the true ground state wavefunction.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the NQA trajectory for toy models, showing the projected parameter evolution and energy landscape minimization. Tables provide quantitative comparisons of energy errors ($\epsilon_B, \epsilon_Q$) against exact diagonalization results for various spin glass benchmarks.

**Paper structure.** The paper builds from general NQS methods (TDVP/VQA) to the specialized DBQS ansatz, emphasizing its computational advantages (block-Gibbs sampling, efficient local operator calculation). It then details the NQA training scheme, followed by extensive benchmarking across various spin glass and combinatorial optimization problems.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Variational neural network models have achieved remarkable success in solving ground-state problems of quantum many-body systems. However, addressing classical and quantum spin glasses remains challenging, as disorder and energy frustration give rise to an exponentially large number of local energy minima separated by high-energy barriers, hindering the efficiency of conventional Metropolis-based Monte Carlo methods. To bridge this gap, we introduce Deep Boltzmann Quantum States, a class of neural quantum states inspired by deep Boltzmann machines that inherit efficient block Gibbs sampling. We also propose two key advances in the training algorithm. Firstly, we combine natural-gradient updates with state-of-the-art stochastic optimizers. Secondly, we gradually tune the hardness of the problem Hamiltonian by interpolating from an easy to a hard regime, without the need to closely approximate the instantaneous adiabatic state at intermediate times. We match the exact solution or the best available estimate for several instances of classical and quantum Ising spin-glass models with infinite-range interactions and hundreds of spins. We also solve instances of the NP-hard Job Shop Scheduling Problem exceeding the current limitations of quantum annealing hardware. To summarize, deep neural architectures with efficient global update rules and trained within an annealing-like scheme, provide a powerful framework for solving real-world hard combinatorial optimization and for investigating disordered quantum many-body systems.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

### [Variational Autoregressive Networks with probability priors](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.16020v1)

**Authors:** Piotr Białas, Piotr Korcyl, Tomasz Stebel, Dawid Zapolski

**Summary.** This paper tackles the inefficiency of Monte Carlo simulations near phase transitions by employing Variational Autoregressive Networks (VANs). The key contribution is demonstrating that embedding physical knowledge, such as symmetries and local interactions, as priors into the neural network architecture drastically improves training stability and accuracy for simulating spin models like the Ising and Edwards-Anderson systems.

**Why it may be interesting.** The work directly addresses the intersection of advanced machine learning (autoregressive models) and complex many-body physics (spin glasses), providing a blueprint for physics-informed AI in condensed matter.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Monte Carlo simulations suffer from critical slowing down near phase transitions, and deep learning samplers struggle with training because they ignore underlying physical symmetries.

**Main result.** Incorporating physical priors into Variational Autoregressive Networks significantly reduces the training burden and improves simulation efficiency for discrete spin models.

**Method.** The authors propose a framework using VANs, approximating the true probability distribution by incorporating physical constraints (priors) derived from the system's symmetries and interactions.

**Model / system.** The study focuses on discrete spin systems, specifically the Ising model and the Edwards-Anderson spin glass model, defined by Hamiltonians involving nearest-neighbor interactions.

**Key observables.** Autocorrelation time, Free Energy (F), Magnetization (M), and Effective Sample Size (ESS).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Temperature parameter beta ($eta$), critical temperature ($eta_c$), and system size (e.g., $32 	imes 32$ lattice).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The core assumption is that restricting the network's dependency structure using physical priors (instead of a 'blank slate' approach) is beneficial for training stability and accuracy.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate training histories (ESS, magnetization) comparing different approximation levels ($t_1, t_2, t_3$) for both the Ising and Edwards-Anderson models, showing convergence towards true values.

**Paper structure.** The paper systematically develops the VAN framework, applies it to the Ising model, and then extends the methodology to the more complex Edwards-Anderson spin glass model, comparing results across various temperature regimes and approximation orders.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Monte Carlo methods are essential across diverse scientific fields, yet their efficiency is frequently hampered by critical slowing down-a sharp increase in autocorrelation times near phase transitions. Although deep learning approaches, such as neural-network-based samplers, have been proposed to alleviate this issue, they face another serious problem: the difficulty of training the models. This difficulty partially stems from the overly general nature of original machine-learning architectures, which often ignore underlying physical symmetries and force networks to relearn them from scratch. In this paper, we demonstrate that incorporating physical priors into the model significantly enhances performance. Building upon existing strategies that integrate spin-spin interactions, we propose a framework that utilizes a prior probability distribution as a starting point for training. Our results for the Ising model, as well as for the Edwards-Anderson spin glass model, suggest that moving away from `blank slate' models in favor of physics-informed priors reduces the training burden and facilitates the simulation of larger system sizes in discrete spin models.

</details>

<a id="paper-2605.15601"></a>
### [Bayesian Sequential Verification for Budget-Aware Quantum Program Testing](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.15601v1)

**Authors:** Lei Zhang  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.15601v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `quantum measurements` **3/5** · `analog quantum simulation` **1/5** · `entanglement & information structure` **1/5** · `measurement-induced transitions` **1/5**

<div class="figure-strip-hint">↔ Scroll figures horizontally</div>
<div class="paper-figures-scroll">
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.15601_figures/2605.15601_fig1.jpg" alt="Figure 1">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 1.</b> Fig. 1. Overall illustration of the Bayesian sequential verification workflow.</figcaption>
</figure>
</div>

**Summary.** This paper introduces Bayesian sequential verification, a statistically robust method for testing quantum programs under finite measurement budgets. It uses Bayesian Hypothesis Testing to sequentially update confidence in a program's success probability, allowing for early termination when sufficient evidence is gathered. This provides a practical, resource-aware workflow for building reliable quantum continuous-integration pipelines.

**Why it may be interesting.** While focused on quantum computation, the rigorous statistical framework for resource-constrained decision-making (budget-aware testing) is highly relevant for characterizing the reliability of complex quantum simulations or measurements in AMO physics.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper addresses the challenge of verifying quantum programs, which yield statistical distributions, by developing a method that is both statistically rigorous and mindful of limited computational resources (shot budgets).

**Main result.** Bayesian sequential verification significantly reduces the required measurement cost compared to fixed-budget testing when the quantum program's success probability exceeds the target threshold.

**Method.** It formulates the verification as a reference-based Bayesian Hypothesis Testing workflow, iteratively updating posterior distributions batch-by-batch as measurement evidence accumulates.

**Model / system.** The work applies to general quantum programs tested on quantum backends, using case studies like Bell-state preparation and QAOA for MaxCut optimization.

**Key observables.** Property success probability ($	heta$), conservative lower credible bound (LCB), and the resulting pass/fail decision.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Prior strength ($s$), target threshold ($	au$), batch size ($b$), and the required pass-streak ($r$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The outcomes are assumed to be conditionally independent and identically distributed (i.i.d.) given the true probability, and the prior must be accurately estimated from reference runs.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 illustrates the overall workflow from reference source to decision. Algorithm 1 details the sequential Bayesian verification procedure, including LCB calculation and stopping rules.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem, details the Bayesian sequential verification methodology (priors, sequential updates, LCB decision rule), evaluates it on Bell-state and QAOA workloads, and compares its efficiency against fixed-budget baselines.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Quantum programs often produce probability distributions rather than deterministic outputs, making verification inherently statistical and increasingly costly on real hardware. In practice, developers still frequently rely on testing with fixed shot budgets on simulators, which are simple but time-consuming and poorly suited to noisy backends. What is missing is a verification approach that is both statistically explicit and budget-aware.   This paper formulates Bayesian sequential verification as a reference-based Bayesian hypothesis testing workflow in which priors are derived from explicit reference sources, such as finite-shot reference runs or ideal/statevector-based computation, and verification decisions are updated batch by batch as measurement evidence accumulates.   This approach is evaluated in Qiskit on two complementary workloads: Bell-state and QAOA-MaxCut. Across both case studies, the results show that Bayesian sequential verification can substantially reduce measurement costs compared to fixed-budget baselines when the success probability of the program exceeds the target threshold.   The findings position Bayesian sequential verification as a practical verification workflow for quantum programs. The approach provides a foundation for future quantum continuous-integration pipelines that require reliable, budget-aware pass/fail decisions and motivates validation on real quantum hardware.

</details>

### [When Noisy Quantum Order Finding Remains Recoverable for Shor's Algorithm](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.16074v1)

**Authors:** Qingxin Yang, Stefano Markidis  
**Type:** both · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.16074v1>

</figure>
</div>

**Summary.** This work analyzes the robustness of order finding, a key step in Shor's algorithm, when subjected to noise on current quantum hardware. By analyzing measured probability distributions, the authors find that successful recovery depends most strongly on how concentrated the verified probability mass is on the correct candidate denominator. This highlights that post-processing-aware metrics are more predictive of algorithmic success than global measures of distribution structure.

**Why it may be interesting.** The focus on the interplay between quantum measurement noise and classical signal processing techniques (like continued fractions) provides concrete, actionable metrics for diagnosing the performance limits of quantum algorithms on NISQ hardware.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper investigates the conditions under which the true multiplicative order, a core subroutine of Shor's algorithm, can be recovered from a measured, noisy quantum phase estimation distribution.

**Main result.** Recoverability is primarily governed not by the global structure of the noise-affected distribution, but by the concentration of verified probability mass on the correct candidate denominator after classical post-processing.

**Method.** The authors analyzed 680 measured distributions using classical post-processing (continued-fraction) and characterized them using four features: autocorrelation peak strength, normalized entropy, dominant verified mass fraction, and verified margin fraction.

**Model / system.** The study uses measured precision-register output distributions collected from IBM quantum systems simulating noisy order finding circuits. The underlying process involves the ideal comb-like structure being distorted by quantum noise.

**Key observables.** Autocorrelation peak strength, Normalized entropy, Dominant verified mass fraction, and Verified margin fraction.

**Important parameters / regimes.** The true multiplicative order ($r$), the measured distribution $p(y)$, and the parameters defining the noise model (e.g., $\epsilon$ in the convolution model).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis assumes the standard classical post-processing routine (continued-fraction) is applied, and it separates global distribution structure from post-processing-aware mass competition.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate examples of recoverable/non-recoverable cases, and feature comparisons (e.g., $M_{1,frac}$ vs. $\Delta_{ver,frac}$) show trends where higher values correlate with recoverability. Random forest permutation importance confirms $M_{1,frac}$ as the most predictive feature.

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses from defining the problem (noisy order finding) to developing diagnostic features based on post-processing outcomes. It then employs statistical classifiers (AUROC, Random Forest, Decision Tree) to determine which features best predict recoverability, culminating in the identification of the dominant verified mass fraction as the key indicator.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Order finding is the core subroutine of Shor's algorithm. On NISQ hardware, phase estimation output distributions are often distorted by noise, making correct order recovery difficult. We study recoverability in noisy order finding: given a measured precision-register distribution, when does standard classical post-processing still return the true order? We analyze 680 distributions from IBM quantum systems across problem instances and circuit settings. For each distribution, we apply continued-fraction post-processing with modular verification and define recoverability as whether the recovered order equals the true one. We characterize each distribution using four features: autocorrelation peak strength, normalized entropy, dominant verified mass fraction, and verified margin fraction. We evaluate these quantities using marginal feature comparisons, single-feature AUROC analysis, and multivariate tree-based classifiers. We use random-forest permutation importance to assess which quantities contribute distinct predictive information once the other features are known. To make classification behavior interpretable, we train a decision tree that exposes threshold rules for recoverable and non-recoverable distributions. We find that recoverability is strongly associated with residual comb-like structure in the measured distribution and the way verified probability mass is organized across candidate denominators. The dominant verified mass fraction is the strongest single-feature indicator of recoverability, and tree-based analysis shows it also provides the primary split in an interpretable threshold description. Some highly distorted distributions remain recoverable when one verified denominator dominates the post-processing mass, while some visibly structured distributions fail because classical post-processing favors an incorrect verified denominator.

</details>

<a id="paper-2605.15272"></a>
### [A general proof of integer Rényi QNEC](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.15272v1)

**Authors:** Tanay Kibe, Pratik Roy  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.15272v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `entanglement & information structure` **3/5** · `Keldysh / 2PI / non-Gaussian methods` **2/5**

<div class="figure-strip-hint">↔ Scroll figures horizontally</div>
<div class="paper-figures-scroll">
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.15272_figures/2605.15272_fig1.jpg" alt="Figure 1">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 1.</b> Figure 1. Schematic of the null cut algebras. The transverse directions are perpendicular to the plane of the figure. The null planes x± = 0 are labelled. Light blue regions correspond to the Rindler wedge R : {x+ &gt; 0, x−&lt; 0} and its commutant R′. Dark blue regions correspond to the null cut algebra Mb and its commutant M ′ b.</figcaption>
</figure>
</div>

**Summary.** This theoretical paper proves the Rényi Quantum Null Energy Condition for integer parameters $n \ge 2$ within the framework of local Poincaré-invariant Quantum Field Theory. It achieves this by demonstrating the log-convexity of the Rényi relative entropy using sophisticated tools from operator algebras and non-commutative functional analysis. The result provides a powerful mathematical constraint on the structure of quantum states in QFT.

**Why it may be interesting.** The rigorous mathematical framework connecting quantum energy conditions (a fundamental constraint in QFT) to the convexity properties of generalized entropy measures (Rényi divergences) is highly relevant for understanding quantum field theory constraints in curved or null-geometry backgrounds.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper aims to prove the Rényi Quantum Null Energy Condition (Rényi QNEC), which is a generalization of the standard Quantum Null Energy Condition (QNEC). This conjecture relates to the non-negativity of the second null shape variation of the Sandwiched Rényi Divergence (SRD).

**Main result.** The authors prove the Rényi QNEC for all integer Rényi parameters $n \ge 2$ for von Neumann algebras possessing a half-sided modular inclusion structure. This is achieved by proving the log-convexity of the associated Kosaki $L_n$ norm.

**Method.** The proof employs advanced techniques from operator algebras, specifically utilizing von Neumann algebras, Haagerup's non-commutative $L_p$ spaces, and analyzing the dynamics generated by null translations via semigroups.

**Model / system.** The context is local Poincaré-invariant Quantum Field Theory (QFT) on Minkowski spacetime, analyzed using null coordinates and associated with von Neumann algebras defined over null-cut regions.

**Key observables.** Sandwiched Rényi Divergence (SRD), $D_n(\Psi\|\Omega; M_A)$, and the Kosaki $L_n$ norm of normal positive functionals.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Integer Rényi parameter $n \ge 2$, and the continuous parameter $c$ governing the null translation flow.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The existence of a half-sided modular inclusion (HSMI) structure on the von Neumann algebras, and the finiteness of the SRD of the excited state relative to the vacuum.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 schematically illustrates null cut algebras, such as the Rindler wedge and the null cut algebra $M_b$.

**Paper structure.** The paper builds from defining the SRD and the RQNEC conjecture, establishing the necessary mathematical framework using non-commutative $L_p$ spaces and null translations, and culminating in the proof of log-convexity via a cyclic Ward identity.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

The Rényi quantum null energy condition conjectures that the second null shape variation of the sandwiched Rényi divergence (SRD) of an excited state relative to the vacuum is non-negative in local Poincaré-invariant quantum field theory, giving a one-parameter generalization of the quantum null energy condition (QNEC). We prove Rényi QNEC for all integer Rényi parameters $n\geq 2$ for von Neumann algebras carrying a half-sided modular inclusion structure. The only assumption on the excited state is finiteness of its SRD relative to the vacuum. Concretely, for any $σ$-finite von Neumann algebra with such an inclusion, we prove log-convexity, under the associated null-translation semigroup, of the Kosaki $L^n$ norm of any normal positive functional with finite $L^n$ norm.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2605.15784"></a>
### [Quantum compressed sensing](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.15784v1)

**Authors:** Jianyong Hu, Wei Li, Shuxiao Wu, Liwen Zhang, Yongchuang Sun, Jiazhao Tian, Guosheng Feng, Zhixing Qiao, Jianqiang Liu, Changgang Yang, Ruiyun Chen, Chengbing Qin, Guofeng Zhang, Liantuan Xiao, Suotang Jia  
**Type:** both · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.15784v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `quantum measurements` **3/5** · `entanglement & information structure` **1/5** · `measurement-induced transitions` **1/5**

<div class="figure-strip-hint">↔ Scroll figures horizontally</div>
<div class="paper-figures-scroll">
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.15784_figures/2605.15784_fig1.jpg" alt="Figure 1">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 1.</b> Figure 1. QCS paradigm and frequency-domain validation. (A) The QCS workflow comprises four steps. Step 1, quantum</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.15784_figures/2605.15784_fig2.jpg" alt="Figure 2">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 2.</b> Figure 1(B) shows the probability distribution over a four-dimensional sparse vector for a single-tone signal</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.15784_figures/2605.15784_fig3.jpg" alt="Figure 3">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 3.</b> Figure 1(C) shows that the frequency identification accuracy reaches 47% with a single photon detection event</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.15784_figures/2605.15784_fig4.jpg" alt="Figure 4">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 4.</b> Figure 2. Frequency-domain sparse signal reconstruction based on DFT scheme. (A-C) Reconstruction of a single-tone signal</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.15784_figures/2605.15784_fig5.jpg" alt="Figure 5">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 5.</b> Figure 3. QCS results for time-domain sparse signals. (A) Reconstruction success rate versus measurement number M for</figcaption>
</figure>
</div>

**Summary.** This work introduces Quantum Compressed Sensing (QCS), a paradigm that uses unitary quantum evolution to reconstruct sparse signals. By encoding positional information into the quantum state evolution rather than requiring explicit measurements, QCS bypasses the classical measurement lower bound. This results in a measurement requirement scaling linearly with sparsity ($M \sim K$), offering a major leap in information acquisition efficiency for sensing and imaging.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides a concrete physical pathway—using unitary transformations—to overcome fundamental information-theoretic limits in measurement-based signal processing, which has implications for quantum sensing and metrology.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper addresses the fundamental limitation of classical Compressed Sensing (CS) by proposing Quantum Compressed Sensing (QCS) to achieve superior signal acquisition efficiency.

**Main result.** QCS reduces the required measurement number from the classical bound of $M = O(K\log(N/K))$ to $M \sim O(K)$, achieving ultimate information acquisition efficiency.

**Method.** The methodology reframes signal acquisition as a unitary quantum evolution, using domain-alignment evolution to physically execute the support-set search at the quantum level.

**Model / system.** The system involves preparing a quantum probe state, mapping the signal onto the state, applying a unitary domain-alignment evolution, and performing projective measurements on the output state.

**Key observables.** Measurement number ($M$), reconstruction fidelity, and the success rate of recovering sparse coefficients.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Sparsity ($K$), signal dimension ($N$), and the measurement number ($M$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The process relies on the existence of a physically realizable unitary operator for domain alignment and assumes a linear relationship between detection probability and signal amplitude.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the QCS workflow, compare the linear scaling of required measurements ($M \sim K$) against the classical bound, and show reconstruction examples in both time and frequency domains.

**Paper structure.** The paper builds from establishing the classical CS lower bound, introducing the QCS paradigm via unitary evolution, detailing the four-step physical implementation (probe state, mapping, alignment, detection), and validating the $M \sim K$ scaling experimentally.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

How many measurements are fundamentally required to capture a signal. Shannon's information theory established the bedrock of this question in 1948, the Nyquist Shannon theorem set the first answer, and compressed sensing (CS) rewrote it in 2006 by reducing the required measurement number to M = O(Klog(N/K)) for a K sparse signal. Here, we propose quantum compressed sensing (QCS), a paradigm that reframes signal acquisition as a unitary quantum evolution. By encoding high dimensional signal information into a single quantum probe state, then introducing domain-alignment evolution,a physically realizable unitary transformation that maps the sparse basis directly onto the measurement basis. QCS executes the support-set search at the quantum level without consuming measurement trials. The logarithmic penalty vanishes, compressing the required measurement number from the classical bound to M =O(K) and reducing reconstruction from ill posed optimization to linear estimation. We experimentally validate QCS using frequency and time domain sparse signals, confirming that the measurement number scales linearly with sparsity and decouples entirely from the signal dimension. Our work provides a physical pathway toward ultimate information acquisition efficiency, with broad implications for sensing, imaging, and communication.

</details>

<a id="paper-2605.15829"></a>
### [Thermodynamic signatures of a field-induced ordered intermediate phase in Na$_2$Co$_2$TeO$_6$](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.15829v1)

**Authors:** Prashanta K. Mukarjee, Sebastian Erdmann, R. Kalaivanan, R. Sankar, Kwang-Yong Choi, Alexander A. Tsirlin, Philipp Gegenwart  
**Type:** experiment · **Category:** strongly correlated electrons · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.15829v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `exotic spin models in light-matter` **3/5** · `analog quantum simulation` **2/5**

<div class="figure-strip-hint">↔ Scroll figures horizontally</div>
<div class="paper-figures-scroll">
<figure class="paper-figure-card">

<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 1.</b> FIG. 1. The grey dotted lines indicate phase transitions. (c) Field-dependent magnetization, M(B), for NCTO at different temperatures down to 2 K. (d,e) The first and second derivative of M(B). The grey dotted lines indicate the positions of the critical fields.</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.15829_figures/2605.15829_fig2.jpg" alt="Figure 2">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 2.</b> FIG. 2. Residual moment: Temperature dependent magnetiza- tion measured in zero field after cooling the sample in weak train- ing fields for (a) in-plane (B ∥a∗) and (b) out-of-plane (B ∥c) directions.</figcaption>
</figure>

<img src="2605.15829_figures/2605.15829_fig3.jpg" alt="Figure 3">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 3.</b> FIG. 3. Field dependence of ΓB and specific heat: (a) Specific heat as a function of magnetic field at T = 2 K. (b) Magnetic Grüneisen parameter ΓB as a function of magnetic field at T = 2 K. (c) Field evolution of magnetic entropy at T = 2 K. (d) Field evolution of ΓB at different temperatures down to 1 K. The data are vertically offset for clarity. The gray lines indicate the position of the critical fields. The region between Bc2 and Bc3 is presented in yellowish-gray color. The data in this figure are shown for the increasing field-sweep cycle.</figcaption>

<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 4.</b> FIG. 4. Colour map: Colour map of the adiabatic MCE as a function of temperature T and magnetic field B for B ∥a∗. For field sweeps, the phase boundaries were determined from peaks and broad minima in dM/dB and d2M/dB2, maxima in the field-dependent specific heat Cp(B), and zero crossings in the magnetic Grüneisen pa- rameter ΓB.</figcaption>

**Summary.** This work presents a detailed thermodynamic investigation of $	ext{Na}_2	ext{Co}_2	ext{TeO}_6$ under high magnetic fields to probe its potential as a Quantum Spin Liquid. By measuring specific heat and magnetization, the authors identify three distinct field-induced phase transitions. Crucially, they argue that the observed thermodynamic behavior contradicts the expected signatures of a QSL in one of the intermediate phases.

**Why it may be interesting.** The detailed thermodynamic mapping of field-induced transitions in a strongly correlated magnetic material like a cobaltate provides crucial experimental constraints for theoretical models of exotic quantum states, such as QSLs.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study investigates the nature of field-induced ordered intermediate phases in the honeycomb cobaltate $	ext{Na}_2	ext{Co}_2	ext{TeO}_6$, specifically testing the hypothesis that it hosts Quantum Spin Liquid (QSL) behavior.

**Main result.** The thermodynamic measurements reveal successive field-induced transitions, but the phase between $B_{c2}$ and $B_{c3}$ lacks the expected enhanced magnetic entropy of a QSL, suggesting a distinct ordered state.

**Method.** The research employed a comprehensive thermodynamic study using magnetization, specific heat, and magnetocaloric-effect measurements across a low-temperature, high-field phase diagram.

**Model / system.** The physical system is the honeycomb cobaltate $	ext{Na}_2	ext{Co}_2	ext{TeO}_6$, which is studied as a candidate material for realizing QSL behavior, often compared to theoretical models like the Kitaev model.

**Key observables.** Magnetization ($M$), specific heat ($C_p$), magnetic Grüneisen parameter ($\Gamma_B$), and magnetic entropy ($\Delta S$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** In-plane magnetic fields ($B \parallel a^*$) at critical values $B_{c1} \simeq 6 	ext{ T}$, $B_{c2} \simeq 7.8 	ext{ T}$, and $B_{c3} \simeq 10.4 	ext{ T}$.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis assumes that sharp anomalies in thermodynamic quantities correspond to well-defined phase transitions, and that the QSL state would exhibit specific entropy signatures.

**Figures summary.** Figures show $M$ and $C_p$ vs $T$ and $B$, and derivatives like $dM/dB$ and $d^2M/dB^2$, highlighting the three critical fields $B_{c1}, B_{c2}, B_{c3}$ through various anomalies.

**Paper structure.** The paper systematically presents thermodynamic measurements ($M, C_p, \Gamma_B$) across the field-temperature phase diagram, identifying three successive transitions and analyzing the thermodynamic signatures of the resulting intermediate phases.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

The honeycomb cobaltate Na$_2$Co$_2$TeO$_6$ has recently been proposed as a candidate material for hosting field-induced quantum spin liquid (QSL) behavior. Here, we present a comprehensive thermodynamic study of its low-temperature, high-field phase diagram using magnetization, specific heat, and magnetocaloric-effect measurements down to 1 K. In zero field, we observe a weak residual moment that provides further insight into the nature of the magnetic ground state. For in-plane magnetic fields ($B \parallel a^*$), we identify three field-induced transitions at $B_{c1} \simeq 6$ T, $B_{c2} \simeq 7.8$ T, and $B_{c3} \simeq 10.4$ T. The magnetic Grüneisen parameter and specific heat reveal clear thermodynamic signatures of these successive phase transitions enclosing two intermediate phases. Contrary to expectations for a field-induced QSL, the phase between $B_{c2}$ and $B_{c3}$ lacks enhanced magnetic entropy but instead shows behavior consistent with a distinct ordered state. Above $B_{c3}$, the absence of additional anomalies indicates a crossover to a conventional spin-polarized regime. Our results place stringent thermodynamic constraints on the proposed QSL scenario in Na$_2$Co$_2$TeO$_6$, calling for further microscopic investigations to establish the precise nature of the field-induced phases.

</details>

### [Supergravity flows, wormholes and their pseudo-Hermitian holographic duals](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.16168v1)

**Authors:** Jose M. Begines, Antón F. Faedo, Carlos Hoyos, Daniele Musso  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.16168v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `entanglement & information structure` **3/5** · `Keldysh / 2PI / non-Gaussian methods` **1/5**

<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.16168_figures/2605.16168_fig1.jpg" alt="Figure 1">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 1.</b> Figure 1. Possible formation of the wormhole. Two stacks of branes and anti-branes form throats inside an asymptotically flat spacetime when they backreact. Condensation of the open string tachyon makes the two throats connect forming a wormhole. In the near-horizon limit the geometry is restricted to the interior of the wormhole, that connects two asymptotically AdS spacetimes.</figcaption>

**Summary.** This work explores supergravity solutions featuring traversable wormholes, interpreting them holographically as dual to entangled states of quantum field theories. The key theoretical insight is that these dual theories exhibit pseudo-Hermitian and PT-symmetric properties. The methodology combines advanced techniques from general relativity, string theory, and quantum information theory.

**Why it may be interesting.** The connection between gravitational phenomena (wormholes, supergravity) and the mathematical structure of PT-symmetry and pseudo-Hermiticity provides a rich theoretical framework relevant to non-Hermitian quantum mechanics.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper investigates supergravity flows and wormhole solutions, focusing on their holographic duals which are hypothesized to be pseudo-Hermitian and PT-symmetric.

**Main result.** The authors demonstrate that wormhole solutions can be interpreted as the low-energy theory of branes/anti-branes after tachyon condensation, dual to an entangled state of two copies of the boundary QFT.

**Method.** The analysis combines analytic continuation of supergravity solutions, stability analysis of fluctuations around domain walls, and calculating mutual information for the dual quantum field theories.

**Model / system.** The model involves consistent truncations of supergravity theories, leading to domain wall solutions connecting asymptotically Anti-de Sitter (AdS) spacetimes. The dual system is a quantum field theory exhibiting pseudo-Hermiticity and PT-symmetry.

**Key observables.** Mutual information, Goldstone bosons, the metric remaining real-valued after analytic extension, and the spectrum of metric fluctuations ($M^2$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** The periodicity of the superpotential $W(\eta)$ (e.g., $2\pi$), the separation $X$ between brane stacks, and the radial coordinate $r$ or $\eta$.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis assumes the dual theory is best understood when the metric remains real, and it relies on linearizing fluctuations around background solutions.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the formation of wormholes via tachyon condensation connecting two throats, and the general structure of the effective action and potential governing the dynamics.

**Paper structure.** The paper proceeds by studying analytic extensions of solutions, interpreting wormholes via tachyon condensation, arguing for the pseudo-Hermitian dual, and concluding with stability analysis of fluctuations.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

We find solutions to consistent truncations of supergravity where some real scalars are analytically extended to imaginary values, ensuring the metric remains real-valued. Among the solutions there are Lorentzian traversable wormholes connecting two asymptotically Anti-de Sitter spacetimes and flows that have a real metric also when uplifted to ten or eleven dimensions. We argue that the holographic duals are pseudo-Hermitian and $PT$-symmetric theories. Wormhole solutions also admit an interpretation as the low-energy theory of two stacks of branes and antibranes after tachyon condensation. The wormhole is then dual to an entangled state of two copies of the theory that lives on a stack of branes. We present some evidence by computing the mutual information between the theories at each boundary and by identifying the Goldstone bosons associated to the breaking of the two copies of Poincaré symmetry to their diagonal subgroup.

</details>

### [The role of Wigner rotation in estimating the specific angular momentum of a Kerr spacetime](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.15600v1)

**Summary.** This theoretical paper develops a method to measure the specific angular momentum of a Kerr spacetime using a quantum geodesic interferometer. By analyzing the interference visibility, which depends on both gravitational time delay and polarization rotation (Wigner phase), the authors derive an estimate for the spacetime parameter $a$. The results suggest that such measurements could achieve high precision with sufficiently large detector separations.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides a concrete, quantum-metrology approach to measuring fundamental parameters of General Relativity (like $a$) using quantum interference effects, bridging quantum optics and gravitational physics.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper aims to estimate the specific angular momentum ($a$) of a Kerr spacetime by analyzing the polarization rotation of light.

**Main result.** The interferometric visibility is a signature of two relativistic effects (time delay and polarization rotation), allowing for an estimate of $a$ with a relative error potentially reaching $10^{-6}$ for large baselines.

**Method.** The analysis uses a geodesic interferometer model, calculating the detection probability based on phase differences derived using the WKB approximation in the weak-field and slow-rotation limits.

**Model / system.** The system is light (single photon) propagating along null geodesics within the curved background of a Kerr spacetime, analyzed using the tetrad formalism.

**Key observables.** Interferometric visibility, detection probability ($P_+$), gravitational time delay phase difference ($\Delta\vartheta_	au$), and Wigner phase difference ($\Delta\vartheta$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Specific angular momentum ($a$), mass ($m$), interferometer separation ($\Delta r$), and probability error ($\delta p$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis relies on the slow rotation and weak field approximations, and the geometric optics approximation for the photon's localized qubit representation.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the geodesic interferometer setup, showing the dependence of phase differences ($\Delta\vartheta_	au$ and $\Delta\vartheta$) on source positions and angular coordinates, and the relative error $\delta a/a$ vs. baseline separation $\Delta r$.

**Paper structure.** The paper develops the theoretical framework by defining the interferometer setup, calculating the two distinct phase contributions (time delay and Wigner rotation) using the WKB approximation, and finally deriving the estimate for $a$ from the measured detection probability $P_+$.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

We study the rotation of the polarization due to the gravitational field in the Kerr spacetime and the possibility of estimating the specific angular momentum that parameterizes this metric. Our approach is based on a geodesic interferometer, that is, a Mach-Zehnder interferometer whose arms are defined by null geodesics, and a single photon propagating within it. We show that the detection probability at the output ports of the interferometer is a function of two phase differences, one arising from the gravitational time delay and the other from the polarization rotation, both computed under the slow rotation and weak field approximations. Thereby, the interferometric visibility is a signature of two relativistic effects. Using the detection probability, we obtain an estimate for the specific angular momentum and characterize its uncertainty.

</details>

### [Additivity Results for the Rényi-2 Entanglement of Purification](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.15439v1)

**Authors:** Shokoufe Faraji, Zahra Baghali Khanian  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.15439v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `entanglement & information structure` **3/5**

<div class="figure-strip-hint">↔ Scroll figures horizontally</div>
<div class="paper-figures-scroll">
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.15439_figures/2605.15439_page2.jpg" alt="Figure 1">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 1.</b> Low-resolution page preview, page 2</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">

<figure class="paper-figure-card">

<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.15439_figures/2605.15439_page6.jpg" alt="Figure 5">
