**Summary.** This paper rigorously analyzes the additivity and multiplicativity of the Rényi-2 entanglement of purification for quantum channels. By reformulating the entanglement measure as a constrained Schatten norm optimization, the authors derive a general criterion that guarantees multiplicativity for certain classes of channels, proving this property for the important transpose-depolarizing channel.

**Why it may be interesting.** The results provide concrete, general mathematical criteria for when entanglement measures exhibit simple scaling (multiplicativity) under tensor products, which is crucial for understanding resource theories in quantum information.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper determines the additivity/multiplicativity properties of the Rényi-2 entanglement of purification, $
u_2(\Omega)$, for various quantum channels.

**Main result.** The entanglement measure $
u_2(\Gamma^{\otimes n}_t)$ is proven to be multiplicative, $
u_2(\Gamma^{\otimes n}_t) = (
u_2(\Gamma_t))^n$, and a general criterion for this multiplicativity is established for CP maps.

**Method.** The analysis reformulates the entanglement measure as a constrained maximum output Schatten $p$-norm problem, utilizing techniques involving the Choi-Jamiołkowski isomorphism and quadratic forms.

**Model / system.** The study focuses on quantum channels, particularly the transpose-depolarizing channel $\Gamma_t$, and its tensor powers acting on bipartite quantum states.

**Key observables.** The Rényi-2 entanglement of purification, $
u_2(\Omega)$, and the associated output Schatten 2-norm, $\upsilon_2(\Omega)$.

**Important parameters / regimes.** The Rényi parameter $p$ (focusing on $p=2$), the dimension $d$, and the channel parameter $t$.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The derivation relies on reducing the optimization problem to extreme points and establishing structural properties of the channels (e.g., the criterion $N^\dagger \circ N = a 	ext{id}_A + b 	ext{Tr}[\cdot]	ext{Id}$).

**Paper structure.** The paper proceeds by first reformulating the entanglement measure, calculating the value for the specific channel $\Gamma_t$ (establishing bounds and a closed form), and then generalizing the result by proving the multiplicativity property for tensor powers and establishing the general criterion for additivity.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

We reformulate the Rényi entanglement of purification as a constrained minimum output Rényi entropy problem. Equivalently, for $p>1$, this formulation can be expressed in terms of a constrained maximal output Schatten $p$-norm. More precisely, for a completely positive map $Ω:L(B')\to L(A)$, we consider the quantity $\upsilon_p(Ω)$ defined by optimizing $\|(Ω\otimes \mathrm{id}_E)(σ^{B'E})\|_p$ over all bipartite states $σ^{B'E}$ whose $B'$-marginal is maximally mixed. We focus on the case $p=2$. First, we compute $\upsilon_2$ for the transpose-depolarizing channel and prove that it is multiplicative under tensor powers. We then establish a general multiplicativity criterion: whenever a completely positive map $N:L(B')\to L(A)$ satisfies $N^{\dagger} \mathbin{\circ} N=a\,\mathrm{id}_A+b\,\mathrm{Tr}[\cdot]\,I_d$ for some constants $a,b\ge 0$, where $N^{\dagger}$ denotes the Hilbert-Schmidt adjoint of $N$, the quantity $\upsilon_2(N)$ is multiplicative under tensor powers. Examples of channels satisfying this criterion include the transpose-depolarizing channel, the depolarizing channel, and their respective complementary channels. Furthermore, we show that, for every completely positive map $Ω$, multiplicativity of $\upsilon_p(Ω)$ implies multiplicativity for its complementary map. This yields the corresponding additivity statements for the associated Rényi-2 entanglement of purification.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2605.16218"></a>
### [Lattice Relaxation Flattens Chern Bands in Rhombohedral Graphene Stacks](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.16218v1)

**Summary.** This theoretical work models moiré graphene stacks on hBN, demonstrating that lattice relaxation is not a minor perturbation. By coupling strain fields with electron-electron interactions via Hartree-Fock theory, the authors show that these effects are necessary to stabilize and isolate topological Chern bands with a quantized Chern number.

**Why it may be interesting.** The interplay between geometric strain (lattice relaxation) and long-range Coulomb interactions to stabilize topological phases is highly relevant to understanding emergent quantum phenomena in solid-state systems.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study investigates how lattice relaxation effects influence the topological electronic structure, specifically the flattening and isolation of Chern bands, in moiré rhombohedral graphene stacks.

**Main result.** Lattice relaxation is shown to be crucial, working in tandem with long-range Coulomb interactions, to flatten and isolate a valley-polarized electron band exhibiting a non-zero Chern number (|C|=1).

**Method.** The research employs self-consistent Hartree-Fock calculations to model the interacting electron system, incorporating strain-induced pseudo-gauge fields derived from lattice relaxation.

**Model / system.** The system is rhombohedral graphene stacked on hBN, forming a moiré superlattice. The model Hamiltonian incorporates intralayer hopping modified by strain fields and interlayer tunneling terms.

**Key observables.** Chern number (C), band structure (gaps, flattening), pseudo-magnetic field (B), and electronic differences between stacking configurations.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Twist angles ($	heta$), filling factor ($
u=1$), dielectric constant ($\epsilon$), and the strength of the displacement field ($D$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis assumes lattice relaxation manifests as a propagating lattice mismatch and that the strain fields decay exponentially with the number of layers.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the exponential decay of displacement fields, the band structure comparison with and without relaxation, and the resulting topological character (Chern number) under varying interaction strengths.

**Paper structure.** The paper systematically builds the model by first defining the lattice relaxation effects on the Hamiltonian, then calculating single-particle properties, and finally incorporating electron-electron interactions via Hartree-Fock theory to determine the topological state.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Motivated by recent observations of integer and fractional Chern insulators in rhombohedral graphene stacks aligned with hexagonal boron nitride (hBN), we propose and study a model in which the moiré potential is defined by the pattern of layer-shear strain fields produced by lattice relaxation in these heterostructures. Although these strain fields decrease exponentially with the number of layers, their imprints on electrons residing away from the contact layer are non-negligible. In the absence of a displacement field, lattice relaxation effects amplify the electronic differences among the two different stackings with hBN. These differences, although attenuated at the single-electron level, survive in the so-called moiré-distant regime and are further enhanced with the inclusion of electron interactions. We find that lattice relaxation plays a crucial role in flattening and isolating a valley-polarized Hartree-Fock electron band with $|C|=1$ Chern number. Our results challenge the conventional wisdom on moiré effects in these heterostructures by illustrating the intertwined effects of long-range Coulomb interactions and lattice relaxation, and opens the door to explore different regimes of twist angles and displacement fields for the search for topological states.

</details>

### [Second-order moment equivalence of twisted Gaussian Schell model beams and orbital angular momentum eigenmodes](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.15408v1)

**Authors:** T. Ferreira, G. Santos, S. Ayala, Lucas Hutter, E. S. Gómez, G. Lima, G. Cañas, S. P. Walborn

**Summary.** This paper proves that the second-order statistical description (covariance matrix) of any cylindrically symmetric OAM beam is mathematically identical to that of a Twisted Gaussian Schell-model (TGSM) beam. This equivalence allows researchers to use the well-established TGSM propagation toolbox to predict the beam-width evolution and divergence of complex OAM modes. It is significant because it unifies the description of coherent and partially coherent light in terms of their second-order moments.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work provides a powerful, non-trivial connection between the description of highly structured, coherent light (OAM modes) and the description of partially coherent light (Schell model), unifying their second-order propagation characteristics.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper establishes a rigorous mathematical equivalence between the second-order moment (covariance matrix) of coherent Orbital Angular Momentum (OAM) eigenmodes and Twisted Gaussian Schell-model (TGSM) beams.

**Main result.** The covariance matrix of any cylindrically symmetric OAM eigenmode takes a universal form identical to that of a TGSM beam, allowing for direct, second-order indistinguishable parameter mapping between the two beam types.

**Method.** The analysis involves deriving and comparing the covariance matrices for both beam types, utilizing the fact that the covariance matrix governs second-moment evolution under symplectic transformations.

**Model / system.** The study compares two models: (1) Coherent OAM eigenmodes (including Laguerre-Gaussian, Bessel-Gaussian, and Perfect Vortex Beams) and (2) Partially coherent TGSM beams, both analyzed under the assumption of cylindrical symmetry.

**Key observables.** Covariance matrix elements ($\langle r^2 angle, \langle k_r^2 angle$), beam-width evolution, far-field divergence, and the $M^2$ beam-quality factor.

**Important parameters / regimes.** OAM quantum number ($\ell$), TGSM twist parameter ($u$), beam waist ($w$), and coherence parameters ($\sigma, \delta$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The equivalence is established for the second-order moments, and certain analyses for PVB and BG beams rely on leading-order approximations in specific parameter regimes.

**Figures summary.** The notes reference a summary table (Table I) detailing the correspondence between the covariance matrix parameters for TGSM, LG, PVB, and BG beams.

**Paper structure.** The paper develops the theory for both beam types independently, then compares their covariance matrices to derive the parameter mapping. It details the correspondence for specific families (LG, BG, PVB) and concludes by showing the implications for beam propagation and basis completeness.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

We show that the covariance matrix of any cylindrically symmetric coherent orbital angular momentum (OAM) eigenmode with quantum number $\ell$ takes a universal form depending only on $\langle r^2\rangle$, $\langle k_r^2\rangle$, and $\ell$, independently of the radial profile, and that this form is identical to the covariance matrix of a twisted Gaussian Schell-model (TGSM) beam.} More specifically, both matrices share the same pattern of zero and nonzero entries, with the off-diagonal blocks proportional to $\ell$ and the TGSM twist parameter $u$, respectively. This result holds for an arbitrary radial profile and provides direct term-by-term identification of parameters between the two sets of beams. We work out the correspondence in detail for three important families: Laguerre--Gaussian (LG), Bessel--Gaussian, and perfect vortex beams (PVBs), and derive the conditions under which each coherent OAM mode maps onto a physically realizable TGSM beam. {Because the covariance matrix governs second-moment evolution under arbitrary ABCD (symplectic) transformations, any two beams sharing the same covariance matrix are second-order indistinguishable at every propagation plane. In particular, the matched TGSM and coherent OAM beams share identical beam-width evolution, far-field divergence, and $M^2$ beam-quality factor.} In particular, the well-developed TGSM propagation toolbox applies directly to the second-order moment evolution of the three coherent families. We further show that within each beam family the covariance matrix uniquely determines the beam parameters, with exact uniqueness established for LG modes. Additional results include cross-family second-moment equivalence conditions and a proof that PVB modes form a complete orthonormal basis in the limit $w\to 0$.

</details>

### [Fast convergence of Dynamic Capacities of GNS-Symmetric Quantum Channels](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.15953v1)

**Authors:** Omar Fawzi, Li Gao, Mizanur Rahaman, Mostafa Taheri

**Summary.** This work rigorously analyzes how the information capacities of quantum systems decay over time when subjected to noise modeled by a quantum channel. By focusing on GNS-symmetric channels, the authors derive explicit exponential convergence bounds for both classical and quantum capacities. Crucially, they use these bounds to demonstrate the precise crossover point where active error correction strategies provide a provable advantage over passive ones.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides rigorous, quantitative bounds on how fast quantum information degrades under noise and precisely determines the threshold where active quantum error correction techniques outperform passive noise mitigation.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper studies the time evolution and convergence rate of information capacities (classical and quantum) for quantum systems subjected to repeated noise modeled by a quantum channel.

**Main result.** For GNS-symmetric channels, the one-shot and asymptotic classical and quantum capacities converge exponentially fast to their infinite-time limits. Furthermore, the analysis quantifies the advantage of active error correction over passive methods.

**Method.** The analysis employs functional inequalities, quantum relative entropy bounds, and techniques involving the peripheral projection of the quantum channel to derive explicit exponential convergence bounds.

**Model / system.** The system is a finite-dimensional quantum memory subjected to noise described by a quantum channel $\Phi$ applied at discrete time steps, or continuous-time quantum Markov semigroups $\{T_t\}$. The focus is on GNS-symmetric channels.

**Key observables.** One-shot $\delta$-error classical/quantum capacities ($C_\delta, Q_\delta$), asymptotic capacities ($C, Q$), and the convergence rate quantified by exponential decay terms.

**Important parameters / regimes.** The convergence rate depends on entropic properties like the entropy contraction coefficient ($\alpha_c$) and the Pimsner–Popa constant ($\Lambda_c(P)$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The primary results are restricted to quantum channels exhibiting GNS-symmetry with respect to a full-rank invariant state $\sigma$.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 compares the passive upper bound on quantum capacity (derived from Theorem 3.2) against active lower bounds (using 5-active and 25-active codes) for Pauli noise, showing a crossover time where active correction becomes superior.

**Paper structure.** The paper builds from establishing the convergence of capacities to the peripheral projection, deriving explicit exponential bounds for one-shot and asymptotic capacities, and finally applying these bounds to compare active versus passive error correction strategies.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

We consider a quantum system described by a quantum channel $Φ$ that is applied at every time step and study the time evolution of its information capacities. When $Φ$ is a GNS-symmetric channel (this includes Pauli channels, for example), we give explicit exponential convergence bounds for the classical and quantum capacities. These bounds are in terms of entropic properties of $Φ$. We further illustrate how these results help quantify the performance of active versus passive error-correction setups.

</details>

### [Reweighting free energy profiles between universal machine learning interatomic potentials for fast consensus building](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.15630v1)

**Summary.** This work presents a scalable framework to calculate free energy profiles (PMFs) by reweighting data sampled with one machine learning potential (MLIP) to many others. By deploying analytical corrections, the authors overcome the limitations of direct reweighting when potentials differ significantly. This allows researchers to achieve reliable, low-cost consensus on materials properties across multiple computational models.

**Why it may be interesting.** While focused on condensed matter potentials, the core mathematical machinery—reweighting distributions and estimating free energy differences from limited sampling—is highly relevant to calculating free energy barriers in quantum dynamics simulations.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Accurately estimating the Potential of Mean Force (PMF) across different interatomic potentials (MLIPs) is computationally prohibitive due to the high cost of high-accuracy simulations like DFT.

**Main result.** The authors developed a robust reweighting framework that successfully recovers high-fidelity target thermodynamics across multiple MLIPs, even when phase-space overlap is critically low.

**Method.** They employ systematic reweighting techniques, utilizing analytical corrections like the energy-only approximation to bypass the failure modes of traditional exponential reweighting methods.

**Model / system.** The study applies this framework to model $	ext{Li}^+$ transport in a complex 601-atom nanoconfined electrolyte (zeolite), comparing results from various MLIPs trained with different DFT functionals.

**Key observables.** Potential of Mean Force (PMF), reaction free energy, activation free energy barrier, and pairwise Jensen–Shannon (JS) divergence.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Temperature ($k_B T$), system size (601 atoms), and the degree of phase-space overlap (quantified by reweighting entropy $S$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The primary assumption is that the energy-only approximation provides a stable and accurate enough estimate to achieve cross-model consensus, despite neglecting entropic contributions.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the reweighting strategy, PMF comparisons using various estimators (Gaussian vs. energy-only), and heatmaps showing pairwise JS divergence between potentials, revealing natural clustering of the MLIPs.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the PMF estimation challenge, details the reweighting formalism (including Gaussian and energy-only approximations), validates the method on a complex electrolyte system, and concludes by establishing the framework as a diagnostic protocol for cross-model consensus.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Free energy profiles serve as a fundamental bridge between microscopic atomic fluctuations and macroscopic thermodynamic observables. Estimating the free energy profile along a reaction coordinate, referred to as the potential of mean force (PMF), with density functional theory (DFT) accuracy is computationally expensive. Universal machine learning interatomic potentials (MLIPs) drastically reduce this cost, but their accuracy is strongly determined by their training data and hence can be uncertain for a given system. In this work, we present a systematic and scalable framework for reweighting PMFs, initially sampled with a single 'source' MLIP, across a representative suite of target MLIPs. Because traditional direct exponential reweighting fails for large system sizes due to low phase-space overlap between potentials, we deploy robust analytical corrections. Applying this to a complex 601-atom system of Li$^+$ transport in a nanoconfined electrolyte, we demonstrate that a mean energy-gap approximation effectively bypasses statistical collapse, producing a highly stable PMF matching the target PMF. Using this approach, we recover high-fidelity target thermodynamics across multiple DFT reference levels (PBE+D3, PBE-sol, r$^2$SCAN,r$^2$SCAN-D4) at a fraction of the computational cost of full simulations. Furthermore, thermodynamic analysis reveals that the studied MLIPs partition into two distinct clusters driven by their training data. Our reweighting framework successfully recovers target thermodynamic properties--specifically, reaction and activation free energies--even when the phase-space overlap between potentials is critically low. Ultimately, this approach establishes a vital diagnostic protocol to achieve affordable cross-model consensus on materials chemistry properties without redundant, resource-intensive simulations.

</details>

**Summary.** This theoretical paper determines the large-$N$ scaling of Tan's contact for a harmonically trapped Tonks-Girardeau gas at finite temperature. It establishes a two-term scaling law, identifying the subleading correction as a quantifiable difference between canonical and grand-canonical ensemble calculations. The results are validated using advanced mathematical techniques like contour integration and Padé approximants across all temperature regimes.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work provides a rigorous, quantitative understanding of finite-temperature corrections to universal correlation measures in strongly interacting quantum gases, bridging the gap between different thermodynamic ensembles.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper derives the large-$N$ scaling law for Tan's contact, a measure of short-range correlations, for a harmonically trapped Tonks-Girardeau gas at finite temperature.

**Main result.** The contact exhibits a two-term large-$N$ expansion, $C_N(	au) = A(	au) N^{5/2} + B(	au) N^{3/2}$, where the subleading coefficient $B(	au)$ is identified as the ensemble difference between canonical and grand-canonical calculations.

**Method.** The derivation employs contour integration techniques on the canonical partition function, followed by saddle-point approximations and the construction of Padé approximants to ensure uniform accuracy across different temperature regimes.

**Model / system.** The system is $N$ identical bosons confined by a harmonic potential and interacting via a contact potential, analyzed in the Tonks-Girardeau limit, which maps the system to noninteracting spinless fermions.

**Key observables.** Tan's contact ($C$), the leading scaling coefficient $A(	au)$, and the novel subleading coefficient $B(	au)$.

**Important parameters / regimes.** The dimensionless temperature ratio $	au = T/T_F$, where $T_F$ is the Fermi temperature of the noninteracting gas.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis is performed in the large-$N$ limit, and the validity of the Padé approximants is assumed across the working temperature range.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the raw data for $C_N(	au)$ verifying the scaling law, and later show the constructed Padé approximants interpolating between the low-temperature Sommerfeld and high-temperature virial regimes.

**Paper structure.** The paper systematically derives the leading coefficient $A(	au)$ first, followed by the novel subleading coefficient $B(	au)$. It then compares canonical and grand-canonical ensemble formulations to identify $B(	au)$ as the ensemble correction, culminating in the construction and validation of Padé approximants for both scaling functions.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

We derive the canonical-ensemble scaling of Tan's contact for $N$ harmonically trapped Tonks--Girardeau bosons at finite temperature in the large-$N$ limit. The leading scaling coefficient reproduces the local-density-approximation result and is obtained from a contour-integral representation of the canonical partition function followed by a saddle-point reduction to a phase-space integral with a self-consistent scaled chemical potential. The subleading coefficient is the central new object of this work: it admits an explicit representation in terms of universal phase-space integrals of the Fermi factor, has closed-form Sommerfeld and virial limits, and is identified with the canonical-versus-grand-canonical ensemble difference at fixed mean particle number. In the high-temperature Boltzmann regime the ratio of subleading to leading coefficients collapses to a universal value, traceable to the Poissonian particle-number statistics of the dilute grand-canonical gas. We construct Padé approximants for both scaling functions that interpolate uniformly between the low-temperature Sommerfeld and high-temperature virial regimes; for the subleading coefficient we report a form that is uniformly accurate on our working range of temperatures and asymptotically correct beyond. The scaling law is verified against canonical contour-integration data across the full temperature range.

</details>

<a id="paper-2605.16150"></a>
### [Gaussian fluctuations in the tunneling probability of a closed universe](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.16150v1)

**Authors:** L. Salasnich  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.16150v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `Keldysh / 2PI / non-Gaussian methods` **2/5** · `analog quantum simulation` **1/5**

<div class="figure-strip-hint">↔ Scroll figures horizontally</div>
<div class="paper-figures-scroll">
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.16150_figures/2605.16150_fig1.jpg" alt="Figure 1">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 1.</b> FIG. 1: Graphical summary of the main results discussed in this paper: the analytical calculation of the quantum tunnel- ing probability of a closed universe including Gaussian fluc- tuations.</figcaption>
</figure>
</div>

**Summary.** This paper calculates the quantum tunneling probability for a closed universe using quantum cosmology methods. It refines previous estimates by providing an exact analytical expression for the prefactor arising from Gaussian fluctuations around the classical instanton solution. The work is a rigorous semiclassical estimate of the universe's nucleation rate.

**Why it may be interesting.** While focused on quantum gravity, the rigorous mathematical techniques used to calculate the prefactor—involving functional determinants and solving Sturm-Liouville type equations—are highly relevant to calculating correlation functions or determinants in quantum field theories relevant to AMO physics.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper calculates the quantum tunneling probability for the creation of a closed universe using the Euclidean path-integral formalism.

**Main result.** It derives a self-consistent semiclassical estimate for the nucleation rate, providing an exact analytical expression that includes both the exponential suppression from the instanton action and the Gaussian prefactor from quadratic fluctuations.

**Method.** The calculation employs the semiclassical approximation, expanding the Euclidean action quadratically around the instanton solution and utilizing advanced techniques like the Gel'fand-Yaglom theorem to determine the functional determinant prefactor.

**Model / system.** The model is a closed Friedmann-Lemaitre-Robertson-Walker (FLRW) universe treated within a fixed-interval minisuperspace formulation. The dynamics are governed by the Hamiltonian constraint in the Euclidean path integral.

**Key observables.** The tunneling probability ($P_T$) and the associated prefactor ($F_E$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** The semiclassical regime ($S_E/\hbar \gg 1$), the cosmological constants ($\Lambda$), and the gravitational coupling ($G$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The calculation is a minisuperspace approximation that neglects the full lapse integration and does not enforce the Hamiltonian constraint beyond the leading semiclassical order.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 provides a graphical summary of the analytical calculation of the quantum tunneling probability, explicitly including the contributions from Gaussian fluctuations.

**Paper structure.** The paper develops from the general formalism of the Euclidean path integral to the specific calculation of the prefactor ($F_E$). It compares initial approximations (like the harmonic oscillator model) with the final, exact result derived using the Gel'fand-Yaglom theorem applied to the functional determinant.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

We consider the quantum creation of a closed universe within the Euclidean path-integral formalism. An analytical expression for the tunneling probability is derived, including both the exponential suppression and the exact Gaussian prefactor due to quadratic fluctuations around the instanton. The calculation is performed in a fixed-interval minisuperspace formulation, where the Hamiltonian constraint is imposed at the level of the classical instanton, while the full lapse integration is not included beyond the leading semiclassical approximation. The result provides a transparent and self-consistent semiclassical estimate of the nucleation rate, refining previous analyses with the inclusion of Gaussian fluctuations.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2605.15869"></a>
### [HOPPER: A Hop-by-hop Entanglement Distribution Protocol for Asynchronous Quantum Networks](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.15869v1)

**Authors:** Claudio Cicconetti  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.15869v1>

A memory cell associated with a link is identified as a triplet n-i-r, where n is the node identifier, i is a 0-based index, and r is the role (Master ‘M’ or Slave ‘S’); such a notation is only for illustration purposes in the diagram, while in the signaling messages, numerical identifiers are used. The sequence diagram shows two possible courses of action: the ebit is successfully established and used by the applications on the end-nodes (green dashed box) vs. failure to create the ebit due to...</figcaption>
</figure>
</div>

**Summary.** This paper introduces HOPPER, a novel, asynchronous protocol for distributing entanglement across quantum repeaters. It overcomes the limitations of traditional serial models by allowing intermediate nodes to manage multiple ebit requests concurrently. Simulations prove that HOPPER achieves significantly higher throughput and better fidelity than synchronous methods in long-distance quantum networks.

**Why it may be interesting.** The focus on asynchronous resource management and multiplexing concurrent requests directly addresses scalability bottlenecks in quantum network architectures, which is crucial for realizing large-scale quantum communication.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The core challenge is efficiently distributing entangled quantum bits (ebits) across long-range quantum networks, which is hampered by the fragility of entanglement and the inefficiency of serial operation models.

**Main result.** The proposed HOPPER protocol significantly outperforms synchronous alternatives by enabling autonomous, hop-by-hop entanglement distribution, especially when handling multiple concurrent ebit requests.

**Method.** The authors propose and simulate the HOPPER protocol, which utilizes asynchronous signaling and defines Master/Slave roles for managing quantum memory consistency during entanglement swapping.

**Model / system.** The model is a quantum repeater network featuring end nodes and intermediate repeaters, relying on entanglement swapping via Bell State Measurements (BSM) and quantum memory cells.

**Key observables.** Throughput (ebit/s), Fidelity, and comparison of performance between synchronous (SYNC) and asynchronous (HOPPER) protocols.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Quantum memory size (Q), number of concurrent applications (1 to 50), and path length (short vs. long-distance regimes).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The protocol assumes local generation of entangled pairs is heralded and focuses on bipartite entanglement; it trades strong consistency guarantees for reduced signaling overhead.

**Figures summary.** Figures compare throughput and fidelity between HOPPER and SYNC protocols across varying parameters like memory size and number of applications, demonstrating HOPPER's superior scaling.

**Paper structure.** The paper establishes the need for ebit distribution, critiques existing synchronous/serial models, introduces the asynchronous HOPPER protocol with detailed memory management (Master/Slave), and validates its performance through extensive numerical simulations comparing it to synchronous methods.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

The quantum Internet relies on the ability to distribute entangled quantum bits (ebits) between quantum memories at the end nodes, to perform applications like blind or distributed quantum computing that are impossible if end nodes are connected via a classical, i.e., non-quantum network. This need creates new challenges due to the fragile nature of entanglement, which decoheres over short timescales and cannot be amplified, buffered, or retransmitted. Two broad categories of approaches have been proposed in the scientific literature to realize such an entanglement distribution in a given path: one relying on a synchronous time-slotted model, and another one where intermediate nodes interact asynchronously. However, both of them implicitly assume a serial operation, where one ebit is established and made available to the application on end nodes before creating a new one. This is inefficient in long-range networks, with high transmission latencies, if the intermediate nodes have multiple memory qubits that could be used in parallel. To overcome this limitation, in this paper, we study the implications of multiplexing concurrent ebit requests on the same quantum, for both synchronous and asynchronous operation. Furthermore, for the latter, we define a novel distribution protocol, called HOPPER, where the intermediate nodes make autonomous and hop-by-hop decisions on the use of their local resources when establishing an ebit. With numerical simulations, we show that HOPPER is effective in handling multiple ebit requests in parallel, and it exhibits significantly better performance than a synchronous alternative in different scenarios.

</details>

### [Measurement-Efficient Variational Quantum Linear Solver for Carleman-Linearized Nonlinear Dynamics](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.15366v1)

**Summary.** This work presents a hybrid quantum-classical method to solve nonlinear differential equations by first linearizing them using Carleman linearization. The resulting linear system is then solved using a Variational Quantum Linear Solver (VQLS) implemented on quantum hardware simulators. The key finding is that optimizing the cost function (using local cost) allows the VQLS to accurately track classical solutions for these complex dynamics.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides a concrete, quantum-computable recipe for simulating complex, non-linear physical systems (like those found in AMO or quantum optics) by mapping them onto linear algebra problems solvable on near-term quantum hardware.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Developing a measurement-efficient Variational Quantum Linear Solver (VQLS) to solve linear systems derived from nonlinear dynamics.

**Main result.** The VQLS successfully recovers quantum states proportional to classical solutions for Carleman-structured systems, demonstrating high fidelity using local cost functions.

**Method.** The approach combines Carleman linearization to transform a nonlinear ODE into a linear system, which is then solved using a hybrid quantum-classical VQLS algorithm.

**Model / system.** The canonical benchmark is the damped, externally forced Duffing oscillator, which is linearized via the Carleman-Euler operator into a block-banded linear system $L Y = B$.

**Key observables.** Fidelity ($F_{sol}, F_{dir}$), relative residuals ($R_r$), global and local cost function values ($C_G, C_L$), and convergence rates.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Truncation order ($N$), stationary-extension length ($p$), and the choice between global vs. local cost formulations.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The method assumes the applicability of Carleman linearization and relies on the VQLS framework, which is tested on NISQ-era hardware.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate Hadamard Test circuits, the block-banded structure of the linear matrix $L$, and cost function histories comparing global and local cost approaches.

**Paper structure.** The paper first introduces the problem (nonlinear ODEs) and the linearization technique (Carleman). It then details the VQLS implementation, comparing global and local cost functions, and benchmarking different Hermitianization and ansatz architectures.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

We present hybrid quantum-classical pipelines for solving the Duffing equation that leverage Carleman linearization and the Variational Quantum Linear Solver (VQLS). First, we demonstrate that Carleman linearization accurately approximates the weakly nonlinear Duffing equation, with errors diminishing as the truncation order increases. Next, across IBM and Xanadu platforms, we deploy VQLS with symmetry-grouped Hadamard Test evaluations under both global and local cost formulations, compare distinct Hermitianization within a common cost framework, and benchmark hardware-efficient ansatz architectures under a fixed Hermitianization. Across block-banded test cases, each method achieves near-unity fidelity and vanishing relative residuals. These results show that topology-agnostic ansatz, optimized Hermitianization, and efficient cost formulation enable VQLS to recover quantum states proportional to classical solutions for Carleman-structured systems, providing a portable recipe for quantum-in-the-loop simulation of nonlinear dynamics.

</details>

**Summary.** This paper proposes a novel method to improve quantum simulation accuracy by redesigning Trotter decomposition based on local SU(2) symmetries rather than just Hamiltonian commutativity. By grouping terms into symmetry-respecting local clusters, the method drastically reduces residual errors and circuit depth for models like the Kagome Heisenberg lattice. This establishes local symmetry as a practical and powerful design principle for simulating complex many-body quantum systems.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work provides a powerful, systematic design principle for Hamiltonian simulation that moves beyond simple algebraic commutation rules, directly impacting the feasibility of simulating complex, strongly correlated quantum materials on near-term quantum hardware.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The primary challenge is improving the accuracy and efficiency of digital quantum simulation by redesigning the Trotter decomposition method.

**Main result.** The authors introduce a symmetry-guided decomposition that significantly suppresses commutator-induced errors and reduces circuit depth compared to conventional methods.

**Method.** The method exploits the underlying local SU(2) symmetry of Hamiltonian terms, grouping them into local three-site clusters rather than relying solely on direct Pauli commutativity.

**Model / system.** The work is demonstrated on several physically relevant spin-lattice models, including the Kagome Heisenberg model with triangular spin-chirality interactions and the 1D transverse-field Ising model.

**Key observables.** State infidelity, average spin-chirality bias, and the number of required quantum gates (e.g., CNOT gates).

**Important parameters / regimes.** The simulation time $t$ and the number of Trotter steps used for approximation.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The core assumption is that the Hamiltonian structure can be effectively decomposed based on local SU(2) symmetry, which is superior to general commutativity assumptions.

**Figures summary.** Figures schematically illustrate the decomposition strategies on lattices (Kagome, 1D), comparing the number of required clusters (e.g., 10 vs. 2) and showing error/fidelity comparisons.

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses from identifying the limitations of standard Trotter decomposition to proposing the symmetry-based decomposition principle. It then applies this principle to specific models (Heisenberg, Ising) to derive concrete, error-suppressing product formulas.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

The product formula, commonly known as Trotter decomposition, is a central tool for digital quantum simulation, whose performance depends critically on how the Hamiltonian is partitioned into tractable blocks. Standard decompositions typically rely on direct commutativity among Hamiltonian terms in a chosen operator representation, which can lead to large residual errors and deep circuits for complex, practically relevant many-body quantum systems. We address this fundamental bottleneck by introducing a new decomposition principle that goes beyond commutativity, grouping Hamiltonian terms into local three-site clusters according to the underlying SU(2) symmetry of the local dynamics. We show that three-site generators fall into at most four SU(2)-symmetry classes, each admitting an effective two-qubit SU(4) representation with exact and efficient implementations. By reducing the number of clusters, this decomposition principle substantially suppresses commutator-induced errors and circuit overhead while preserving underlying physical structures that commutativity-based decompositions may violate. We demonstrate the proposed method on several physically relevant spin-lattice models, where the reduced cluster structure can even realise the second-order product formula without doubling the circuit depth, as would be required by conventional decompositions. Numerical simulations of a Kagome Heisenberg model with triangular spin-chirality interactions show that the proposed method reduces both state infidelity and average spin-chirality bias by more than three orders of magnitude compared with conventional decompositions, while using substantially fewer gates. These results establish local symmetry as a flexible and practical design principle for product-formula simulation, opening a route to more accurate and hardware-efficient simulations of broader classes of many-body systems.

</details>

**Summary.** This theoretical paper bridges functional analysis and quantum physics by showing that concepts like operator ideals are fundamental to quantum theory. It rigorously connects the tensor product of Hilbert spaces to specific classes of bounded operators, offering a deep mathematical insight into quantum information and quantum field theory structures.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides a highly abstract, mathematical underpinning for quantum information protocols (like teleportation) and quantum field theory, suggesting that the underlying mathematical structure is governed by operator ideal theory.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper aims to reveal deep, structural connections between abstract mathematical tools—specifically operator ideals (like trace-class and $p$-nuclear operators)—and the foundational formalism of quantum physics, including AQFT and quantum information theory.

**Main result.** It establishes that the tensor product structure of Hilbert spaces can be rigorously identified with spaces of absolutely $p$-summing operators, providing a linear algebraic description for processes like quantum teleportation.

**Method.** The methodology combines advanced functional analysis (Pietsch's operator ideals, Grothendieck theory) with the mathematical framework of von Neumann algebras and the analysis of conjugate Hilbert spaces.

**Model / system.** The theoretical framework involves general complex Hilbert spaces, their tensor products, and the algebraic structures underpinning quantum field theories (von Neumann algebras). Applications include modeling quantum teleportation and analyzing compatibility in General Probabilistic Theories (GPTs).

**Key observables.** Operator ideals ($\mathcal{L}_2, \mathcal{N}, \mathcal{P}_2$), the compatibility degree $\gamma$, and the modular operator $\Delta$.

**Important parameters / regimes.** The parameter $p$ in $p$-nuclear operators ($1 \le p < \infty$), and the number of measurements $g$ in GPTs.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis relies heavily on advanced theorems regarding isometric isomorphisms between a Hilbert space and its dual, and the assumption of 'split' properties for commuting von Neumann algebras.

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses from analyzing the conjugate Hilbert space and its relation to the dual space, to establishing the identification of tensor products with operator ideals, and finally applying these concepts to AQFT and GPTs.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Starting from a thorough analysis of the conjugate $\overline{H}$ of a complex Hilbert space $H$, including its significant importance regarding a representation of the tensor product of two complex Hilbert spaces and its impact to the theorem of Fréchet-Riesz over to a revisit of applications of nuclear and absolutely $p$-summing operators in algebraic quantum field theory (AQFT) in the sense of Araki, Haag and Kastler ($p=2$) and more recently in the framework of general probabilistic spaces ($p=1$), we will outline that Banach operator ideals in the sense of Pietsch, or equivalently tensor products of Banach spaces in the sense of Grothendieck are even lurking in the foundations and philosophy of quantum physics and quantum information theory. In particular, we concentrate on their importance in AQFT (Theorem 5.27). In doing so, we revisit the role of trace-class operators in quantum theory and construct the enveloping $\tup{C}^\adj$-algebra, corresponding to an arbitrarily given normed operator ideal (Proposition 5.3 and Theorem 5.5).   Applications are presented, including a purely linear algebraic description of the quantum teleportation process, thereby showing a link to quantum information theory, also due to the emergence of the Hadamard-Walsh transform and the controlled NOT gate (Example 4.18).   All Hilbert spaces discussed in this paper may be nonseparable (and hence infinite-dimensional).

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2605.15344"></a>
### [Fire and ice: Partially fault-tolerant quantum computing with selective state filtering](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.15344v1)

**Summary.** This paper proposes a practical, partially fault-tolerant quantum computation scheme by concatenating established quantum error-correcting codes. It demonstrates that by accepting non-strict fault tolerance and using selective filtering, the scheme achieves low logical error rates efficiently at realistic noise levels. This work offers a tangible roadmap for scaling quantum computation on near-term hardware.

**Why it may be interesting.** The detailed analysis of error scaling, overhead management, and the comparison between different gate implementations (e.g., teleported vs. direct CNOT) provides concrete, actionable engineering guidelines for building fault-tolerant quantum processors.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** To develop a quantum computation scheme that is reliable and resource-efficient for near-term quantum hardware, bridging the gap between physical and required logical error rates.

**Main result.** The concatenated error-correcting scheme is shown to be reliable and resource efficient at realistic simulated noise rates, demonstrating a practical path toward scalable quantum computation.

**Method.** The authors develop the scheme by concatenating the five-qubit Laflamme code onto the four-qubit Iceberg code, relying on selective filtering rather than strict fault tolerance.

**Model / system.** The analysis focuses on quantum error correction codes (e.g., J20, 2, 6K; J36, 2, 8K) applied to near-term quantum hardware, specifically mentioning platforms like trapped ions or neutral atoms.

**Key observables.** Logical Error Rates ($p_L$), Rejection Rates ($p_R$), and Overhead (e.g., CNOT gate counts per accepted state).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Physical error rates ($p \sim 10^{-4}$ to $4 	imes 10^{-3}$), required logical error rates ($< 10^{-4}$ or $10^{-6}$), and code distance ($d$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The scheme is explicitly not strictly fault-tolerant, and the analysis relies on simplified circuit noise models and postselection.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the concatenation process, stabilizer generators, and circuit diagrams comparing one-stage vs. two-stage ancilla factories for state preparation and CNOT gate implementation.

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses by first establishing the concatenated code structure, then analyzing the role of postselection in managing error rates, and finally comparing the overhead and performance of implementing logical gates (like CNOT) using different circuit designs.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

We develop an error-corrected quantum computation scheme based on concatenating the five-qubit Laflamme code onto the four-qubit Iceberg code. The approach skates a thin line: it is explicitly not fault tolerant, risking higher logical error rates, and it relies on selective filtering to prepare encoded states for error correction, risking significant overhead. Yet, at realistic simulated noise rates, the scheme is reliable and resource efficient. It forges a practical path toward scalable quantum computation.

</details>

### [Towards Deploying Optimistic Quantum Fourier Transforms: An Architecture-Algorithm Co-Design Study](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.15297v1)

<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 4.</b> Fig. 4. Mesoscale routing analysis. (a) Two near-equivalent realizations of a QFT: reversing the addition order requires only a register negation and a unit increment. In both cases the most-significant target bit shifts as the protocol progresses. (b) Cyclic swapping of the phase-gradient register (red edges) keeps addition inputs and targets aligned. Yellow diamonds mark the regular (serial) QFT baseline. (a) Time to solution. Saturating the hot-zone count (hz = max) yields constant-time execution at the cost of a ∼6× increase in logical qubits; at fixed counts, hz = 2 suffices to match the regular QFT, while hz = 4 halves the execution time. (b) Qubit footprint. (c) Combined protocol volume as product of footprint-time. (d) Average concurrent degrees of freedom (DOF). **Summary.** This paper presents a detailed co-design study for running the Optimistic Quantum Fourier Transform on neutral-atom quantum hardware. It introduces a 'hot-zone' architecture to manage mobile quantum resources, achieving significant speedups by optimizing the scheduling of core components like quantum adders. The work establishes critical resource requirements, showing that performance hinges on managing parallelism and reaction-limited operations.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides a concrete, hardware-aware roadmap for implementing complex quantum algorithms like QFT, directly linking algorithmic complexity to physical resource constraints (mobility, parallelism) in neutral-atom platforms.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper addresses the challenge of efficiently deploying the Optimistic Quantum Fourier Transform (OQFT) algorithm on near-term, fault-tolerant quantum hardware.

**Main result.** By implementing a hot-zone architecture and optimizing adder circuits, the OQFT can achieve significant speedups, potentially halving the runtime compared to serial QFTs, provided sufficient parallelism is available.

**Method.** The authors employ an architecture-algorithm co-design study, simulating resource routing and scheduling across specialized hardware components.

**Model / system.** The study is scoped to reconfigurable neutral-atom hardware utilizing the surface code for fault tolerance. It models the system using a 'hot-zone' architecture that separates stationary data storage from mobile processing units.

**Key observables.** Time to solution, protocol volume (space-time cost), peak Degrees of Freedom (DOF), and the required number of logical ancillae.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Code distance (d=15), hot-zone count (2 to 4+), and the size of the quantum register (256–2048 bits).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis assumes reaction-limited operation and is currently scoped to cultivation-only magic-state factories and surface codes.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the decomposition of OQFT into sub-blocks, compare T-counts across different implementations, and show macro-scale architectural layouts detailing the hot zone and data regions.

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses from analyzing the OQFT structure and resource needs, to developing the hot-zone architecture, comparing and selecting optimal adder circuits (Gidney vs. Cuccaro), and finally integrating these components to analyze the full OQFT performance scaling.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

We present an architecture-algorithm co-design study of the Optimistic Quantum Fourier Transform (OQFT) under a surface-code fault-tolerant execution model for reconfigurable neutral-atom hardware. Analyzing the OQFT structure, particularly its reliance on phase-gradient resources and small-scale blocks, highlights architectural requirements for resource mobility and parallel execution. Guided by that, we introduce a hot-zone architecture that decouples data storage from processing and dynamically routes mobile resource packages (magic-state factories, bridge qubits, and phase-gradient registers) to stationary data regions. To expose dominant costs, we route rotation insertions via catalytic phase-gradient addition and heuristically micro-schedule ripple-carry adders to patch-level moves. Under this model, leading Gidney~\cite{Gidney2018halvingcostof} and Cuccaro~\cite{cuccaro2004} adders have similar space-time volume but require different levels of parallelism. At the algorithm level, the five-layer OQFT shows a tunable parallelism/latency trade-off: two hot zones match serial-QFT latency, four hot zones roughly halve runtime, and additional hot zones asymptotically approach constant-time execution at substantial resource cost. Across 256-2048-bit instances, the requirements for half-time performance converge to about 500 additional logical ancillae and a peak parallelism of 128 logical qubits. We also identify broader algorithm-architecture bottlenecks, including endianness mismatches between phase-gradient and data registers, addressed via cyclic phase-gradient swaps and alternating QFT reflections. Scoped to surface codes and cultivation-only magic-state factories, our analysis identifies reaction-limited operation and parallelism demand as primary drivers of resource estimation and establishes a generalizable foundation for primitive-based architectural studies.

</details>

### [Thermodynamic Invariants of Coupled Channels: A Many-Channel Tolman-Ehrenfest Effect](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.15653v1)

**Authors:** Benjamin Hamblin, Victor Calo, Klaus Regenauer-Lieb  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** statistical mechanics · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.15653v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `Keldysh / 2PI / non-Gaussian methods` **1/5** · `analog quantum simulation` **1/5** · `correlated / nonlocal dissipation` **1/5** · `driven-dissipative phase transition` **1/5** · `entanglement & information structure` **1/5** · `methods for driven-dissipative` **1/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **1/5** · `non-equilibrium universality` **1/5**

<div class="figure-strip-hint">↔ Scroll figures horizontally</div>
<div class="paper-figures-scroll">
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.15653_figures/2605.15653_fig1.jpg" alt="Figure 1">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 1.</b> FIG. 1. Three-panel schematic of the MCTE framework (col- umn width). (a) Classical (gij = 0): entropy level sets are straight lines; each intensity Ti is independently uniform. (b) MCTE (gij ̸= 0): level sets curve; a closed loop accumu- lates a non-trivial Ruppeiner holonomy H ωi ̸= 0 (red), and the coupling coefficient ζV grows in the direction of increasing off-diagonal curvature (orange). The unique first integral of this flow is ζiTi = C. (c) ζV as a function of packing fraction ϕ: unity in the dilute limit, O(1) departure at jamming ϕJ, explaining the observed rise in Rowe’s Kµ.</figcaption>

<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.15653_figures/2605.15653_fig2.jpg" alt="Figure 2">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 2.</b> FIG. 2. MCTE invariance on the toy entropy surface S = a ln(V −VJ) + b ln(σmax−σ) −cσ/(V −VJ) with c = 0.3, V0 = 0.78. (a) Bare compactivity χ varies by a factor of 3 along the entropy level set. (b) Coupling coefficient ζ1 compensates. (c) Product ζ1T1 is constant to 10−13 (machine precision), verifying the MCTE relation. Full parameter study in the Supplemental Material [13].</figcaption>
</figure>
</div>

**Summary.** This theoretical paper generalizes the Tolman-Ehrenfest effect to coupled thermodynamic channels, deriving a universal invariant $\zeta_i T_i = C$. It applies this geometric framework to granular materials, successfully resolving long-standing puzzles regarding state-dependent material constants. The work demonstrates how geometric invariants derived from the entropy manifold can predict macroscopic physical laws in complex, coupled systems.

**Why it may be interesting.** While focused on granular physics, the mathematical machinery—using geometric invariants (Ruppeiner connection, curvature) to define equilibrium in coupled systems—is highly relevant to understanding non-equilibrium thermodynamics in complex quantum many-body systems.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper addresses the failure of single-channel equilibrium conditions when multiple thermodynamic channels are coupled, aiming to derive a universal invariant condition.

**Main result.** The unique equilibrium invariant is found to be $\zeta_i T_i = C$, where $\zeta_i$ is the holonomy of the Ruppeiner connection, which resolves long-standing puzzles in granular physics.

**Method.** The analysis extends the Tolman-Ehrenfest effect to the entropy manifold, utilizing the Ruppeiner metric and deriving the equilibrium condition as a first integral of the level-set flow.

**Model / system.** The framework is applied to systems with $n$ coupled thermodynamic channels, with the canonical realization being the $N=2$ volume–stress granular ensemble.

**Key observables.** The invariant $\zeta_i T_i = C$, the off-diagonal curvature $g_{V\sigma}$, the dilatancy ratio, and the MCTE-corrected Rowe relation $\sigma'^{1/3} = \zeta_V(\sigma) K_\mu R$.

**Important parameters / regimes.** The coupling coefficient $\zeta_i$, the jamming point ($\phi_J$), and the dimensionality of the system ($N=2$ being the primary focus).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The $N=2$ volume–stress ensemble is used as the canonical model, and the results are derived independently of the full $N=3$ non-associated flow dynamics.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 contrasts classical decoupled vs. coupled (MCTE) behavior showing the invariant. Figure 2 illustrates the MCTE invariance on a toy entropy surface, showing the product $\zeta_1 T_1$ remains constant despite variations in bare compactivity.

**Paper structure.** The paper first establishes the failure of single-channel equilibrium, derives the MCTE invariant $\zeta_i T_i = C$ using the Ruppeiner metric, and then applies this general formalism to predict and resolve specific issues in granular plasticity (dilatancy, energy restriction, $K_\mu$ dependence).

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

When multiple thermodynamic channels are coupled, single-channel equilibrium conditions fail. Extending the Tolman--Ehrenfest effect to the entropy manifold, we derive the unique $n$-channel invariant $ζ_i T_i = C$, where $ζ_i$ is the holonomy of the Ruppeiner connection. For the granular volume--stress ensemble, Rowe's dilatancy ratio and energy restriction emerge as geometric consequences of the off-diagonal curvature $g_{Vσ}$, and the 60-year puzzle of state-dependent $K_μ$ is resolved: the correction $ζ_V$ reaches $O(1)$ near jamming. The prediction $ζ_Vχ=\mathrm{const}$ across a shear band is experimentally testable.

</details>

### [Wave packet landscape in linear open quantum systems](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.15658v1)

**Authors:** Kang Xu, Miao-Miao Yi, Zi-Hong Yan, C. P. Sun  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.15658v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `Keldysh / 2PI / non-Gaussian methods` **1/5** · `analog quantum simulation` **1/5** · `correlated / nonlocal dissipation` **1/5** · `driven-dissipative phase transition` **1/5** · `methods for driven-dissipative` **1/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **1/5** · `non-equilibrium universality` **1/5**

(b) In the free-particle case, the fluctuation adds a linear term along the flat direction, tilts the fluctuation-free valley, removes the degenerate minimum manifold, and...</figcaption>
</figure>
</div>

**Summary.** This paper introduces a quantum landscape approach to analyze how wave packets evolve in open quantum systems. It shows that the long-time behavior—whether the packet collapses, localizes, or diffuses—is determined by the underlying geometry of a potential landscape in the covariance space. This geometric picture unifies these distinct dissipative quantum dynamics.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work provides a powerful geometric framework to unify disparate phenomena (diffusion, localization, collapse) in open quantum systems, offering deeper physical insight than standard master equation treatments.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** To develop a quantum landscape approach to characterize the long-time behavior of wave packet spreading in linear open quantum systems.

**Main result.** The long-time dynamics (diffusion, localization, collapse) are unified by the geometry of a potential landscape in covariance space, showing that symmetry breaking dictates the asymptotic behavior.

**Method.** The dynamics of the covariance matrix are mapped onto a generalized gradient flow equation, $\dot{\sigma} = -M
abla L(\sigma)$, where $L$ is the quantum landscape function.

**Model / system.** The system consists of linear open quantum systems, modeled by coupled harmonic oscillators interacting with a bath (Caldeira-Leggett type). The dynamics are governed by the quantum Langevin equation.

**Key observables.** The covariance matrix $\Sigma$ (or its vectorization $\sigma$), and the position width $\Delta q(t)$.

**Important parameters / regimes.** The oscillator frequency ($\omega$), damping rate ($\Gamma$), and the presence/strength of bath fluctuations ($\xi(t)$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis relies on casting the covariance evolution into a gradient flow form and analyzing the zero modes of the system matrix.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the deformation of the landscape: a bowl shape for confined systems (implying collapse) versus a flat valley for free particles, which becomes tilted by fluctuations.

**Paper structure.** The paper first introduces the landscape approach to the covariance dynamics, then analyzes specific cases (e.g., harmonic oscillator vs. free particle) to demonstrate how the landscape geometry dictates the asymptotic state, culminating in a discussion of symmetry breaking due to fluctuations.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

We develop a quantum landscape approach to characterize the long-time behavior of wave packet spreading in linear open quantum systems. Instead of treating diffusion, localization, and collapse of the wave packet as separate dynamical phenomena, we show that they originate from the symmetry structure of an underlying landscape in covariance space. The geometry of this landscape determines these distinct long time behaviors. Trapping potentials and bath fluctuations act as distinct symmetry-breaking perturbations, leading to noncommuting long-time limits and abrupt changes in the asymptotic wave-packet width. This geometric picture provides a unified origin for wave-packet diffusion, localization, and collapse in dissipative quantum dynamics.

</details>

**Summary.** This work analyzes the collective dynamics of neural networks modeled by QIF neurons coupled on sparse random graphs. By combining linear stability analysis with random matrix theory, the authors predict the onset of instabilities, which manifest as high-dimensional chaos in directed networks. The findings establish a theoretical link between network topology, coupling parameters, and the emergence of complex, chaotic spatiotemporal activity.

**Why it may be interesting.** The rigorous mathematical framework for analyzing collective dynamics in large, sparse, and disordered systems is highly relevant to understanding complex quantum many-body systems where local interactions lead to emergent global behavior.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study investigates the emergence of spatiotemporal patterns and high-dimensional chaos in coupled neural populations modeled by exact firing rate models on sparse random networks.

**Main result.** The system exhibits extensive high-dimensional chaos in directed networks for both excitatory and inhibitory coupling, while undirected inhibitory networks show winner-takes-all steady states.

**Method.** The analysis combines linear stability analysis of homogeneous states with random matrix theory to determine bifurcation boundaries, followed by Lyapunov exponent calculations to quantify chaos.

**Model / system.** The model uses $N$ populations of Quadratic Integrate-and-Fire (QIF) neurons coupled via a sparse random network with row-normalized topology. The dynamics are governed by coupled differential equations.

**Key observables.** Lyapunov exponents ($\ell_i$), Kaplan-Yorke fractal dimension ($D_{KY}$), and the dispersion relation linking growth rates to connectivity matrix eigenvalues ($\Lambda_k$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Coupling strength ($J$), network sparsity ($\kappa$), and the diagonal coupling parameter ($\mu$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis assumes the system can be linearized around homogeneous states and utilizes the row-normalization constraint on the connectivity matrix.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate bifurcation diagrams showing instability boundaries, and others map the dynamical landscape using Lyapunov exponents and the scaling of the fractal dimension with system size.

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses from defining the QIF model and performing linear stability analysis to deriving spectral properties using random matrix theory, and finally characterizing the resulting dynamics using Lyapunov exponents to confirm high-dimensional chaos.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Exact firing rate models, also known as next-generation neural mass models (NG-NMMs), provide a rigorous description of the dynamics of neural populations. While in its simplest form a single population only displays fixed-point activity, multi-population models may display a range of different behaviors. In this work, we study the dynamics of all-excitatory or all-inhibitory NG-NMMs coupled through sparse random networks with row-normalized network topology. Linear stability analysis of the homogeneous states of the system, representing asynchronous neural activity, provides a dispersion relation linking the emergence of spatiotemporal dynamics to the spectra of the connectivity matrix. Using bounds from random matrix theory, we identify the parameter regions where instabilities occur. In undirected networks, only inhibitory systems produce heterogeneous stationary patterns, corresponding to a winner-takes-all mechanism. In directed networks, exotic rhythmic states with high frequencies emerge in both, excitatory and inhibitory systems. Numerical simulations reveal that these hectic oscillatory states correspond to high-dimensional chaos with extensive properties.

</details>

<a id="paper-2605.15410"></a>
### [Diagonal Adaptive Non-local Observables on Quantum Neural Networks](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.15410v1)

**Authors:** Huan-Hsin Tseng, Yan Li, Hsin-Yi Lin, Samuel Yen-Chi Chen  
**Type:** both · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.15410v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `analog quantum simulation` **1/5** · `entanglement & information structure` **1/5** · `quantum measurements` **1/5**

<div class="figure-strip-hint">↔ Scroll figures horizontally</div>
<div class="paper-figures-scroll">

<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 1.</b> Fig. 2: The training process searching for optimal circuit U(eθ) results in observable changes θ 7→H(θ) := U †(θ) H U(θ).</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.15410_figures/2605.15410_fig2.jpg" alt="Figure 2">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 2.</b> Fig. 1: A VQC diagram of (11), (12), which is also imple- mented in Sec. IV. The variational block represented by a blue box is repeated L times to increase the circuit depth.</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.15410_figures/2605.15410_fig3.jpg" alt="Figure 3">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 3.</b> Fig. 3: Observable space H(n) is partitioned into equivalent classes labeled by R2n. In other words, H(n) is classified by its eigenvalues, and VQC falls in only one particular class.</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.15410_figures/2605.15410_fig4.jpg" alt="Figure 4">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 4.</b> Fig. 5: [MNIST test accuracy.] All models share the same VQC architecture. Increasing measurement non-locality im- proves test accuracy over the fixed-Pauli baseline (pure VQC).</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.15410_figures/2605.15410_fig5.jpg" alt="Figure 5">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 5.</b> Fig. 4: Sliding k-local DANO measurements of k = 3, n = 4.</figcaption>
</figure>
</div>

**Summary.** This paper introduces Diagonal Adaptive Non-local Observables (DANO) to enhance Variational Quantum Algorithms by making the measurement observable trainable. By restricting the observable to a diagonal form, DANO significantly reduces the classical computational cost and parameter count compared to general methods, while mathematically retaining the full expressive power of the original approach. This makes complex, high-locality quantum machine learning tasks feasible on near-term hardware.

**Why it may be interesting.** While focused on QML, the core technique of parameterizing observables via restricted structures (diagonalization) and proving equivalence to a larger space is relevant to understanding constraints and effective Hamiltonians in open quantum systems or when modeling complex interactions in condensed matter.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** To improve Variational Quantum Algorithms (VQAs) by enlarging the function space beyond the circuit ansatz alone, while mitigating the exponential increase in parameters and classical optimization cost associated with general trainable observables.

**Main result.** The proposed Diagonal Adaptive Non-local Observables (DANO) framework achieves the full expressivity of general Adaptive Non-local Observables (ANO) while reducing the observable parameter complexity from $O(4^k)$ to $O(2^k)$, making large-scale measurements practical.

**Method.** The authors restrict the trainable observable $H$ to a diagonal form $\Lambda$, which is mathematically shown to be equivalent in expressivity to the full ANO space under certain conditions.

**Model / system.** The work focuses on Quantum Neural Networks (QNNs) implemented via Variational Quantum Circuits (VQCs) trained on NISQ devices. The model involves training parameters $	heta$ for the unitary circuit $U(	heta)$ alongside parameters $\lambda$ for the diagonal observable $\Lambda(\lambda)$.

**Key observables.** The expectation values of the trainable diagonal observables $\Lambda_j$, which are measured via sliding $k$-local measurements.

**Important parameters / regimes.** The locality $k$ of the observable, the number of qubits $n$, and the circuit parameters $	heta$ and observable parameters $\lambda$.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The core assumption is that restricting the observable to a diagonal form retains full ANO expressivity, which is supported by a density proof relying on the circuit generating set spanning $SU(K)$.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the VQC structure, the training trajectory involving observable changes, and the partitioning of the observable space. Tables summarize performance gains on MNIST and Yale B face recognition when using DANO vs. pure VQC.

**Paper structure.** The paper first introduces the challenge of ANO, then proposes DANO by restricting observables to diagonal forms. It provides theoretical guarantees of expressivity equivalence, followed by empirical validation on classification tasks (MNIST and Yale B) demonstrating performance gains and cost reductions.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Adaptive Non-local Observables (ANOs) have shown that making quantum observables dynamic can substantially enlarge the function space of Variational Quantum Algorithms, partly shifting hardware demands from circuit synthesis to measurement design. However, this advantage is accompanied by a steep increase in the number of parameters, as well as the classical optimization cost for varying general Hermitian observables.   We propose a special form of ANO that significantly reduces this burden by considering only diagonal observables paired with quantum circuits. Mathematically, this is equivalent to the full ANO of a large parameter space since diagonal matrices are canonical representatives of the ANO space modulo unitary similarity. As a result, Diagonal ANO retains the same capability of full ANO while reducing $k$-local observable complexity from $O(4^k)$ to $O(2^k)$ and lowering the corresponding measurement-side classical computation. In this sense, diagonal ANO preserves much of the benefit of full ANO while encompassing conventional VQCs as a special case.

</details>

### [Itinerant antiferromagnetism in the antagonistic pair compound Y$_4$Co$_3$Ag](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.15495v1)

**Authors:** Rafaela F. S. Penacchio, Nao Furukawa, Joanna M. Blawat, John Singleton, Zhouqi Li, Raquel A. Ribeiro, Sérgio L. Morelhão, Sergey L. Bud'ko, Paul C. Canfield, Tyler J. Slade
