**Summary.** This paper reports on the synthesis and characterization of Y4Co3Ag, a novel compound designed to host quasi-one-dimensional Co chains. Through magnetic and transport measurements, the authors establish that the material orders antiferromagnetically at 14.9 K, exhibiting signatures of itinerant magnetism. The findings suggest Y4Co3Ag is a promising platform for investigating magnetic interactions in low-dimensional correlated electron systems.

**Why it may be interesting.** The work provides an experimental realization of designing magnetic materials based on structural motifs (antagonistic pairs) to isolate and study specific magnetic interactions, which is highly relevant for understanding correlated electron physics in low dimensions.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study investigates the nature of antiferromagnetism in the novel compound Y4Co3Ag, aiming to characterize its magnetic interactions within a quasi-one-dimensional structure.

**Main result.** The material exhibits antiferromagnetic ordering at $T_N=14.9$ K, suggesting the presence of small, itinerant magnetic moments and strong magnetic fluctuations in a low-dimensional Co chain system.

**Method.** The research employed a combination of structural characterization (XRD) and multiple physical measurements, including temperature-dependent resistivity, specific heat, and high/low-field magnetization.

**Model / system.** The physical system is the intermetallic compound Y4Co3Ag, which crystallizes in a monoclinic structure featuring one-dimensional Co chains running along the b-axis, designed using the 'antagonistic pair' concept to avoid Co-Ag nearest-neighbor contacts.

**Key observables.** Antiferromagnetic ordering temperature ($T_N$), effective magnetic moment ($\mu_{	ext{eff}}$), specific heat anomaly, and magnetization isotherms $M(H)$.

**Important parameters / regimes.** The system is characterized by quasi-one-dimensional Co chains, exhibiting itinerant magnetic behavior, and the magnetic coupling is analyzed via Curie-Weiss fits ($\Theta/T_N$ ratio).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The primary assumption is that the structural design successfully isolates the Co chains, allowing the study of itinerant magnetism in a quasi-1D setting. Contamination from the growth crucible ($	ext{Al}$) is also analyzed as a systematic perturbation.

**Figures summary.** Figures show crystal structures, temperature dependence of $\chi$, $ho$, and $C_p$ around $T_N=14.9$ K. Magnetization isotherms $M(H)$ are presented for various fields and temperatures, comparing results from different growth conditions (Ta vs. $	ext{Al}_2	ext{O}_3$).

**Paper structure.** The paper first introduces the material and its structure based on the antagonistic pair concept. It then presents comprehensive measurements (transport, magnetic, specific heat) confirming antiferromagnetic ordering. The analysis focuses on interpreting these results to classify the magnetism as itinerant, contrasting it with localized models, and finally discusses the sensitivity of the magnetic properties to sample growth conditions.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Low dimensional crystallographic motifs have long been associated with desirable physical properties. The confinement of electrons to low dimensions is thought to enhance quantum fluctuations and may promote correlated phenomena. Here, using the antagonistic pair concept, we add Y to the immiscible Co-Ag pair to discover Y$_4$Co$_3$Ag. This compound adopts a monoclinic $I$2/$m$ structure consisting of Y channels that are filled by one-dimensional zigzag and hexagonal Co chains, which extend along the crystallographic $b$-axis with no nearest neighbor contacts between Co and Ag atoms. Transport, magnetic, and specific heat measurements reveal that Y$_4$Co$_3$Ag orders antiferromagnetically at $T_N=14.9$ K with an effective magnetic moment $μ_{\text{eff}}$ = 1.4 $μ_{\text{B}}$/Co. Specific heat measurements show only a small entropy loss on the order of $0.1\,R\ln2$ associated with magnetic order, and magnetization isotherms, in DC fields up to 70 kOe at 1.8 K and in pulsed fields up to 600 kOe at 500 mK, indicate a small ordered moment of less than 0.2 $μ_B$/Co. Taken together, our results imply the presence of small, itinerant moments and strong fluctuations in Y$_4$Co$_3$Ag, suggesting that Y$_4$Co$_3$Ag may be a promising candidate material to investigate itinerant magnetic interactions in a quasi-one dimensional system.

</details>

### [Hybrid Quantum-Classical Density Functional Theory: A Structured Framework](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.15979v1)

**Authors:** Namrata Manglani, Samrit Kumar Maity, Shashank Sharma, Soham Phulare, Sanjay Wandhekar  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** strongly correlated electrons · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.15979v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `Keldysh / 2PI / non-Gaussian methods` **1/5** · `analog quantum simulation` **1/5**

<div class="figure-strip-hint">↔ Scroll figures horizontally</div>

<img src="2605.15979_figures/2605.15979_fig2.jpg" alt="Figure 2">

</div>

**Summary.** This paper introduces a comprehensive, structured framework for Hybrid Quantum-Classical DFT (HQ-DFT) to overcome the limitations of classical DFT methods. By organizing research along axes of intervention level, functional role, and hardware capability, it guides researchers on the most promising near-term quantum approaches. It is crucial for advancing accurate simulations of strongly correlated electronic systems.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work directly addresses the computational bottlenecks in simulating strongly correlated materials by systematically mapping out how quantum computation can be integrated, providing a roadmap for future theoretical work.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Density Functional Theory (DFT) suffers from limitations due to inaccurate exchange-correlation functionals and high computational scaling ($	ext{O}(N^3)$).

**Main result.** The authors propose a structured, three-dimensional framework to organize and evaluate hybrid quantum-classical DFT (HQ-DFT) efforts.

**Method.** They introduce a structured design space based on the intervention level (Hamiltonian, Solver, Embedding), the quantum role (Accuracy vs. Acceleration), and the hardware regime (NISQ vs. Fault-Tolerant).

**Model / system.** The focus is on electronic structure calculations, particularly for systems exhibiting strong electronic correlation, such as transition-metal oxides or $f$-electron compounds.

**Key observables.** Accuracy (treating electron correlation), Computational Cost (scaling reduction), and Convergence.

**Important parameters / regimes.** NISQ (Noisy) vs. Fault-Tolerant (FTQC) hardware regimes; the size of the correlated region (fragment size).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The need for a structured framework is highlighted because current research efforts are scattered across various quantum-classical integration points.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 illustrates the proposed three-axis design space for HQ-DFT. Figure 2 maps intervention levels within the Self-Consistent Field (SCF) workflow.

**Paper structure.** The paper first outlines DFT limitations, then proposes the three-dimensional design space, analyzes existing methods within this space, and concludes with guidelines on near-term viability.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Density Functional Theory (DFT) is widely used for atomistic simulations. However, its reach stays limited due to several limitations such as lack of accurate exchange-correlation functional, requirement of costly O(N 3) diagonalization etc. Although quantum computing offers paths forward, including variational techniques, embedding strategies, and quantum linear solvers, the discussion remains scattered. Without shared terms or structure, evaluating progress in hybrid quantum-classical DFT efforts becomes challenging. To bring order, we introduce a three-axis scheme based on where the method connects into DFT, whether the quantum part boosts precision or cuts time, alongside intended device type: current noisy machines or future error-corrected ones. Sorting known approaches in this way shows why embedding frameworks fit modern tools better, while faster linear algebra waits for more advanced systems.

</details>

### [Machine learning potential as a guide for eutectic in ultra-refractory multicomponent ceramics](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.16091v1)

**Summary.** This work proposes using machine learning potentials to overcome the experimental difficulty of finding eutectic points in ultra-refractory alloys. By simulating the supercooled liquid state, the authors identified characteristic signatures in the concentration derivatives of thermodynamic and transport properties. This provides a powerful, computationally guided tool for materials discovery in extreme conditions.

**Why it may be interesting.** While focused on condensed matter thermodynamics, the core methodology—using deep learning potentials to model complex, high-temperature, multi-component liquid states—is highly relevant to simulating non-equilibrium dynamics in quantum many-body systems.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The primary challenge is determining eutectic points in ultra-refractory multicomponent ceramics, as conventional melting experiments are too costly and technically difficult for materials with melting points exceeding 3000 K.

**Main result.** The authors successfully demonstrated that a machine-learning interatomic potential (MLIP) can accurately predict eutectic compositions by analyzing liquid-phase thermodynamic and transport properties.

**Method.** The approach uses MLIPs, trained on DFT data, to simulate the supercooled liquid state and identify eutectic regions by analyzing concentration derivatives of properties like density and energy.

**Model / system.** The study focuses on the Ti-B-C system, analyzing pseudo-binary sections ($	ext{TiB}_2-	ext{TiC}$ and $	ext{TiB}_2-	ext{C}$). The core computational tool is a neural network-based MLIP, validated against high-accuracy DFT methods.

**Key observables.** Physical density, total energy, diffusion coefficient, bulk modulus, and viscosity coefficient, analyzed via their concentration derivatives.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Ultra-refractory temperature regimes (above 3000 K); the supercooled liquid state ($T=2500$ K) is used for simulation.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The method assumes that local extrema in the concentration derivatives of liquid properties reliably pinpoint the eutectic composition, bypassing the need for solid-state structural information.

**Figures summary.** Figures show pseudo-binary sections and liquid phase diagrams, MLIP performance metrics (RMSE), and plots illustrating the concentration dependence of various properties (density, energy, etc.) highlighting the eutectic region.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem, details the MLIP methodology (training, validation against other ML potentials), applies the method to the Ti-B-C system, and concludes by showing that characteristic anomalies in liquid properties confirm the eutectic location.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

The experimental determination of eutectic points is a long-established and widely used technique, but it is generally only practical for systems with relatively low melting points. Many modern, promising materials, however, are ultra-refractory, with melting points exceeding 3000 K. For these systems, conventional melting experiments become prohibitively expensive and technically challenging. Advanced AI modeling can serve as a powerful precursor to guide successful experimentation in such cases. This work proposes a novel criterion for determining the eutectic point concentration in ultra-refractory alloys. The approach is verified using the Ti-B-C system - the most thoroughly studied three-component refractory system to date. The core of the algorithm is a machine-learning interatomic potential, based on a neural network, which achieves accuracy comparable to ab initio methods. Crucially, the algorithm operates effectively in the liquid phase, eliminating the need for information about the solid alloy's crystalline structure to estimate eutectic points.

</details>

**Summary.** This work compares three interatomic potentials—EAM, MEAM, and a machine-learned EDDP—to map the high-pressure phase diagram of Lead. By using advanced sampling methods like Nested Sampling, the authors demonstrate that MLIPs are superior to empirical potentials because they accurately predict key solid-solid phase transitions. This validates the use of MLIPs combined with rigorous statistical mechanics sampling for predictive materials science.

**Why it may be interesting.** While focused on condensed matter, the rigorous application of advanced sampling techniques (NS/RENS) to calculate partition functions and phase stability is highly relevant to theoretical physics dealing with complex many-body systems.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper benchmarks different interatomic potentials (empirical vs. machine-learned) to accurately predict the phase behavior and phase transitions of Lead under high pressure.

**Main result.** The machine-learned potential (EDDP) successfully captured the experimentally-observed FCC-to-HCP transition at $\sim 15 	ext{ GPa}$, whereas the empirical potentials failed to do so, highlighting the importance of model flexibility.

**Method.** The study employs advanced simulation techniques like Nested Sampling (NS) and Replica-Exchange Nested Sampling (RENS) to calculate thermodynamic properties and map the Pressure-Temperature phase diagram.

**Model / system.** The physical system is elemental Lead (Pb). The models compared are the Embedded Atom Method (EAM), Modified EAM (MEAM), and an Ephemeral Data-Derived Potential (EDDP), a neural network-based MLIP.

**Key observables.** Density ($ho$), Heat Capacity ($C_p$), Phase transition pressures (e.g., FCC $\leftrightarrow$ HCP), and relative free energy differences between solid phases.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Pressures up to 60 GPa, Temperatures, and the specific training data used for the MLIPs.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis relies on the assumption that the MLIPs can accurately represent the potential energy surface, and that the simulation methods (NS/RENS) provide an unbiased sampling of the phase space.

**Figures summary.** Figures summarize density comparisons, heat capacity vs. temperature curves showing solid-liquid and solid-solid transitions, and P-T phase diagrams comparing the predictions of the three potentials.

**Paper structure.** The paper systematically compares the predictions of EAM, MEAM, and EDDP across multiple thermodynamic regimes, using NS/RENS to calculate thermodynamic observables and structural order parameters to identify stable phases.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

We compare the predicted phase behaviour of lead (Pb) using three different interatomic potential models, including an embedded atom method (EAM), a modified embedded atom method (MEAM), and a neural network-based machine-learned model in the form of an ephemeral data-derived potential (EDDP). Using nested sampling and replica-exchange nested sampling simulations, we computed thermodynamic and structural properties at pressures up to 60 GPa, mapping both melting behaviour and solid-phase stability. Both the EAM and MEAM models predict the face-centred cubic (FCC) phase to remain stable up to approximately 60 GPa. In contrast, the EDDP model captures the experimentally-observed FCC-to-hexagonal close-packed (HCP) transition at around 15 GPa. These results highlight the importance of training data and model flexibility in accurately describing high-pressure phase behaviour, and demonstrate the effectiveness of nested sampling as a robust framework for exploring phase stability in materials. Particularly, the combination of nested sampling with modern machine-learned interatomic potentials - delivering near ab initio accuracy at tractable cost - opens the door to truly predictive and exhaustive exploration. EDDPs trained on diverse, out-of-equilibrium configurations appear particularly well suited to this task, offering a robust and transferable framework for unbiased phase discovery.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

**Summary.** This paper tackles the systematic bias inherent in the SMO optimization technique used within Variational Quantum Eigensolvers (VQE). By analyzing the source of this bias, the authors propose a novel regularization method. This method successfully corrects the energy estimate while stabilizing the optimization process, leading to consistent performance improvements across various quantum simulation models.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides crucial theoretical insights into the practical limitations of variational quantum algorithms, offering a robust, hardware-agnostic optimization technique that improves convergence reliability.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper analyzes and seeks to correct the systematic bias introduced during the optimization process of Sequential Minimal Optimization Variational Quantum Eigensolvers (SMO-VQE).

**Main result.** A proposed regularization method successfully maintains unbiased energy estimation while improving overall optimization performance compared to the original biased or purely corrected estimators.

**Method.** The authors derive the analytical form of the bias accumulation in SMO-VQE by analyzing the energy expectation value's trigonometric dependence on circuit parameters. They then propose a controlled regularization term to mitigate this bias.

**Model / system.** The work is applied to Variational Quantum Eigensolvers (VQE) used to find the ground state of a general Hamiltonian. Specific examples include the Transverse Field Ising Model and general spin Hamiltonians using the EfficientSU(2) ansatz.

**Key observables.** Estimated energy expectation value ($\langle H angle$), Energy Estimation Error ($\Delta 	ext{Energy}$), and Fidelity ($\Delta 	ext{Fidelity}$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Circuit parameters ($	heta$), number of shots ($N_{	ext{shots}}$), circuit depth, and the regularization strength ($r$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis primarily focuses on mitigating statistical bias arising from finite measurement shots (shot variance), neglecting hardware noise. It relies on first-order expansions in the minimizer displacement.

**Figures summary.** Figures compare the energy estimation error and fidelity across different SMO variants (biased, corrected, regularized) for various Hamiltonians (TFIM, XX, XXZ, XXX) under varying noise conditions.

**Paper structure.** The paper systematically analyzes the bias origin in SMO-VQE, derives the bias term analytically, shows that simple correction destabilizes optimization, and finally proposes a controlled regularization scheme that balances bias removal with optimization stability.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

The Nakanishi Fujii Todo (NFT) algorithm, also known as Rotosolve, implements Sequential Minimal Optimization for Variational Quantum Eigensolvers (SMO-VQE) by exploiting the trigonometric dependence of the energy on individual circuit parameters. This enables analytical one-dimensional minimization using only a few , typically two, energy evaluations, but introduces bias in the estimated energy. Although performing additional measurements every few tens of iterations can mitigate bias accumulation, we find that such corrections often degrade optimization performance. In this paper, we analyze the origin and accumulation of bias during the SMO-VQE process. Specifically, we show that the bias can be accurately estimated without additional measurements. Furthermore, we find that bias correction destabilizes optimization along directions with small curvature, whereas the original biased estimator implicitly acts as a regularizer. Based on these insights, we propose a simple regularization method that implements error accumulation while maintaining unbiased energy estimation. The resulting algorithm consistently improves performance across different system sizes, circuit depths, target Hamiltonians, and measurement shots, with minimal hyperparameter tuning.

</details>

### [Mutually Unbiased Bases for Variational Quantum Initialization: Basis-Union Optimality and Adaptive Family Search](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.16060v1)

**Authors:** Abed Semre, Steven Frankel  
**Type:** both · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.16060v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `entanglement & information structure` **1/5**

<div class="figure-strip-hint">↔ Scroll figures horizontally</div>

<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 1.</b> Figure 1. Adaptive MUB-XRot warm-start mechanism. The method applies local 𝑋-rotations, a selected MUB-family circuit 𝐹𝑗, and ordinary QAOA layers 𝑈𝑝(𝜃). During optimization, neighboring families 𝑗′ = 𝑗⊕2𝑏are screened, and a family switch is accepted only when the screening objective improves. This is a warm-start/pre-circuit method, not a fully matched-mixer MUB-QAOA construction.</figcaption>

<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.16060_figures/2605.16060_fig2.jpg" alt="Figure 2">

Facets separate problem family, size, and depth. The figure shows both the broad MIS improvements and the MaxCut ceiling effect.</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.16060_figures/2605.16060_fig3.jpg" alt="Figure 3">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 3.</b> Figure 3. Solved rate by problem family in the cross-problem benchmark. </figure>

<img src="2605.16060_figures/2605.16060_fig4.jpg" alt="Figure 4">

</figure>
</div>

**Summary.** This paper theoretically proves that Mutually Unbiased Bases (MUBs) provide the optimal structure for initializing variational quantum algorithms by maximizing coverage of the random-Hamiltonian landscape. Empirically, it demonstrates that using MUBs as a warm-start heuristic (Adaptive MUB-XRot QAOA) significantly improves solution quality over standard QAOA on several benchmark problems, although this comes with high computational overhead.

**Why it may be interesting.** The theoretical connection between MUBs and Gaussian width provides a deep mathematical understanding of state preparation in quantum algorithms, which is highly relevant for designing robust quantum initialization protocols.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper investigates the optimal structure for a finite set of initial states (basis-union ensemble) used in Variational Quantum Algorithms (VQAs) to maximize coverage of the random-Hamiltonian landscape.

**Main result.** The complete set of Mutually Unbiased Bases (MUBs) is theoretically proven to maximize the isotropic random-Hamiltonian Gaussian width among all unions of $d+1$ orthonormal bases.

**Method.** The authors combine advanced mathematical tools, such as the centered-convex Gaussian correlation inequality, with extensive empirical benchmarking across several combinatorial optimization problems.

**Model / system.** The study focuses on Variational Quantum Algorithms (VQAs) applied to quantum optimization problems (e.g., MaxCut, MIS). The theoretical analysis uses the random Hamiltonian $H$ acting on a $d$-dimensional Hilbert space, while the empirical tests use specific problem Hamiltonians.

**Key observables.** Isotropic random-Hamiltonian Gaussian width ($W(\mathcal{S})$), Decoded Solution Ratio ($ho$), and the solved rate in QAOA benchmarks.

**Important parameters / regimes.** The dimension $d$ of the Hilbert space, the number of bases used ($d+1$), and the depth/size parameters ($p, n$) of the tested optimization problems.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The theoretical optimality is restricted to unions of $d+1$ orthonormal bases, and the empirical results are explicitly noted to incur substantial runtime overhead and do not claim quantum advantage.

**Figures summary.** Figures summarize the performance of adaptive MUB-XRot QAOA versus standard QAOA across various problem families, showing improvements in decoded solution ratio and solved rate. Other figures compare QRAO approximation ratios for different MUB search strategies.

**Paper structure.** The paper first establishes the theoretical optimality of MUBs using Gaussian correlation inequalities. It then analyzes the algorithmic implications, showing how MUB dependence collapses for diagonal costs. Finally, it presents extensive empirical benchmarks comparing adaptive MUB-XRot QAOA and QRAO against standard methods.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

We study mutually unbiased bases (MUBs) as structured finite initialization and adaptation families for variational quantum algorithms. The main theoretical result is that, in every dimension admitting a complete set of MUBs, the complete MUB ensemble maximizes isotropic Gaussian random-Hamiltonian width among all unions of d+1 orthonormal bases in C^d. Equivalently, within this basis-union class, it gives the smallest expected best-of-set minimum for random-Hamiltonian minimization. The proof represents each orthonormal basis as a regular-simplex Gaussian block and uses a centered-convex Gaussian correlation inequality to show that the independent-block case, realized by complete MUBs, is stochastically extremal. We also record a radial extension for Hamiltonians H=RG with R nonnegative and independent, and the unrestricted qubit case, where complete qubit MUBs are globally optimal among arbitrary six-state ensembles by a Bloch-sphere/octahedron mean-width argument.   We then separate this coverage theorem from variational training dynamics. For diagonal QUBO costs, the MUB-family dependence of a fully matched construction collapses; for the canonical b=0 label it reduces to ordinary X-mixer QAOA. The empirical method is therefore adaptive MUB-XRot warm-start QAOA rather than canonical matched-mixer MUB-QAOA. In a cross-problem benchmark over MaxCut, weighted MaxCut, MIS, weighted MIS, and knapsack, adaptive MUB-XRot is non-worse than standard QAOA in 80.0% of 1500 paired cases, with win/tie/loss 829/371/300 and mean decoded-ratio improvement +0.1616. A separate QRAO MaxCut study shows that bit-flip MUB-family search reaches mean relaxed ratio 0.921 and improves over the X-variational baseline by +0.0608. The evidence is quality-oriented and incurs substantial runtime overhead; no quantum-advantage claim is made.

</details>

### [Strong universality class in disordered systems](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.15441v1)

**Summary.** This paper studies phase transitions in disordered magnetic systems using the Edwards-Anderson Hamiltonian. By combining Monte Carlo simulations with advanced fractal analysis, the authors demonstrate that while standard critical exponents depend on disorder strength, certain geometric exponents remain invariant. This invariance defines a 'strong universality class' crucial for understanding complex disordered materials.

**Why it may be interesting.** The concept of strong universality, where geometric properties are robust against disorder while thermodynamic exponents change, provides a powerful framework for understanding critical phenomena in complex, disordered quantum materials.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper investigates how disorder affects the critical exponents and fractal dimensions of phase transitions in disordered magnetic systems, aiming to identify exponents invariant with disorder.

**Main result.** While critical exponents like $eta$ and $
u$ vary with the disorder strength ($\sigma$), a subgroup of geometric quantities ($\eta, d_f, d_R$) remains invariant, suggesting a 'strong universality class'.

**Method.** The study combines analytical derivations using fractional differential geometry with extensive Monte Carlo simulations to validate scaling laws and critical behavior.

**Model / system.** The system analyzed is a disordered magnetic system modeled using the Edwards-Anderson Hamiltonian on a lattice. The disorder is controlled by the standard deviation $\sigma$ of the couplings.

**Key observables.** Magnetization $m(T)$, correlation length $ho(T)$, critical exponents ($eta, 
u, \eta$), and fractal dimensions ($d_f, d_R$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Disorder standard deviation $\sigma$, temperature $T$, and lattice dimension $d$.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis relies on extending concepts from Euclidean space to fractal media using fractional derivatives, and the determination of exponents depends on the fitting window chosen.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the temperature dependence of the order parameter, correlation length, and Riesz fractal dimension for various disorder strengths, showing clear scaling behavior near $T_c$.

**Paper structure.** The work systematically analyzes the EA Hamiltonian, reviews scaling theories (Fisher's $\eta$), applies fractal analysis to modify correlation functions, and uses MC simulations to demonstrate the existence of disorder-invariant geometric universality.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Disordered systems are very rich laboratories for exploring complex systems. In particular, disordered magnetic systems have been extremely important in the last five decades for understanding a wide range of phenomena. In this work, we use the Edwards-Anderson Hamiltonian to obtain the thermodynamic properties of disordered magnetic systems. In this way, we conduct a systematic investigation of magnetization, correlation functions, order parameter, and fractal dimensions, in function of disorder. In this context, the autocorrelation function for order--parameter fluctuations, introduced by Fisher ( Journal of Mathematical Physics 5, 944322 (1964)), provides an important mathematical framework for understanding the second-order phase transition at equilibrium. However, his analysis is restricted to a Euclidean space of dimension $d$, and an exponent $η$ is introduced to correct the spatial behavior of the correlation function at $T=T_c$. In recent work, Lima et al ( Phys. Rev. E 110, L062107 (2024)) demonstrated that at $T_c$ a fractal analysis is necessary for a complete description of the correlation function. We use Monte Carlo simulations to validate analytical results and to show how disorder alters critical exponents , giving rise to different universality classes. On the other hand, there is a subgroup of critical exponents and fractal dimensions that are invariant with disorder. This subgroup heralds a strong universality class.

</details>

<a id="paper-2605.16249"></a>
### [The Collapse of Unentangled Stoquastic Merlin-Arthur Proof Systems](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.16249v1)

**Authors:** William Gay, Fernando Granha Jeronimo  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.16249v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `entanglement & information structure` **1/5**

<div class="figure-strip-hint">↔ Scroll figures horizontally</div>
<div class="paper-figures-scroll">
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.16249_figures/2605.16249_fig1.jpg" alt="Figure 1">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 1.</b> Figure 1: Complexity landscape around the result. The highlighted equality is proved here for inverse- polynomial promise gaps by the direct k-prover collapse in Theorem 5.1. The dashed arrow is the direct BKS sum-of-squares simulation for nonnegative forms [22], which gives an EXP upper bound in the exponentially large witness dimension and is superseded, for stoquastic verifiers, by the collapse. The containment StoqMA ⊆AM follows from the stoquastic largest-eigenvalue/approximate-set-size framework of Bravyi–DiVincenzo–Oliveira–Terhal together with the original stoquastic-verification framework [13, 14]; StoqMA ⊆PP follows from StoqMA ⊆QMA ⊆PP [35].</figcaption>
</figure>
</div>

**Summary.** This paper analyzes the computational power of multi-prover quantum verification protocols under the stoquastic constraint. It proves that the ability to use multiple provers ($k$) does not enhance the verification power beyond that of a single prover ($	ext{StoqMA}$). The key tool is a specialized de Finetti theorem, leading to the collapse $	ext{StoqMA}(k) = 	ext{StoqMA}$ for specific gap conditions.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work provides a deep structural result in quantum complexity theory, showing that for certain restricted quantum protocols (stoquastic), the ability to use multiple entangled provers offers no computational advantage over a single prover.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper investigates the role of interference in quantum computation complexity by proving that unentanglement does not increase the power of stoquastic Merlin-Arthur verification.

**Main result.** It establishes the collapse $	ext{StoqMA}(k) = 	ext{StoqMA}$ for polynomially bounded $k$ and inverse-polynomial promise gaps, implying $	ext{StoqMA}(k) \subseteq 	ext{AM} \cap 	ext{PP} \subseteq 	ext{PSPACE}$.

**Method.** The core method involves applying a positive, value-based de Finetti theorem for separately symmetric extensions to relate the product state value to the largest eigenvalue of a projected operator.

**Model / system.** The model is quantum computational complexity, specifically comparing $k$-prover stoquastic Merlin-Arthur verification ($	ext{StoqMA}(k)$) against one-witness verification ($	ext{StoqMA}$). The constraints involve stoquasticity (nonnegative, positive semidefinite contraction).

**Key observables.** Acceptance probability, Hellinger distance, Relative Entropy, and the non-negative product value $\omega^+(M)$.

**Important parameters / regimes.** The number of provers $k$, the promise gap $\Delta(n)$ (required to be $\ge 1/	ext{poly}(n)$), and the approximation error $\epsilon$.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The result is contingent on the test matrix $M$ being entrywise nonnegative and the promise gap being inverse-polynomial. The de Finetti theorem used is a one-sided, test-dependent value theorem.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 illustrates the complexity landscape, confirming the equality $	ext{StoqMA} = 	ext{StoqMA}(2) = 	ext{StoqQMA}(2) = 	ext{StoqMA}(k)$ under the specified gap conditions.

**Paper structure.** The paper builds from establishing the positive de Finetti theorem for separately symmetric extensions (Chunk 3), to realizing this spectral relaxation as an actual one-witness verifier (Chunk 2), and finally applying this construction to prove the collapse $	ext{StoqMA}(k) = 	ext{StoqMA}$ (Chunk 4).

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Entanglement and interference are among the most fundamental properties of quantum mechanics. In this work, we investigate the role and power of interference in the context of detecting entanglement. We do so from a computational complexity lens by proving that unentanglement gives no additional power to stoquastic Merlin-Arthur verification. For every polynomial number of provers $k=k(n)$, \[   \text{StoqMa}(k)=\text{StoqMa} . \] Conceptually, the proof separates the role of entanglement from the role of interference: once destructive interference is ruled out by stoquasticity, the product-state constraint can be absorbed into a polynomially larger one-witness stoquastic verification.   The main analytic ingredient is a positive, value-based de Finetti theorem for separately symmetric extensions. If $M$ is an entrywise nonnegative positive semidefinite contraction on $A_1\otimes\cdots\otimes A_k$, then the nonnegative product value of $M$ is approximated to additive error $ε$ by the largest eigenvalue of \[   Π_R^{<k}   (M_{A_{1,1}\cdots A_{k-1,1}A_k}\otimes I)   Π_R^{<k},   \qquad   R=O\!\left(\frac{k^2\sum_i\log\dim A_i}{ε^3}\right), \] where $Π_R^{<k}$ is the operator on $A_1^{\otimes R} \otimes \cdots \otimes A_{k-1}^{\otimes R} \otimes A_k$ projecting to the subspace $\mathrm{Sym}^R(A_1) \otimes \cdots \otimes \mathrm{Sym}^{R}(A_{k-1}) \otimes A_k$.   The spectral relaxation is then realized as an actual one-witness stoquastic verifier. After replacing the uniform permutation averages in the symmetric projectors by inverse-polynomially close dyadic inverse-invariant averages. Consequently, \[   \text{StoqMa}(k)=\text{StoqMa}\subseteq\text{AM}\cap\text{PP}\subseteq\text{PSPACE} . \] The positive de Finetti theorem is isolated as a standalone technique and may be useful in other nonnegative tensor-optimization and stoquastic-verification settings.

</details>

<a id="paper-2605.15667"></a>
### [A Finite-State Gibbs Construction from a Recognition Cost](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.15667v1)

**Authors:** Megan Simons, Jonathan Washburn  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** statistical mechanics · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.15667v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks

<div class="figure-strip-hint">↔ Scroll figures horizontally</div>
<div class="paper-figures-scroll">
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.15667_figures/2605.15667_fig1.jpg" alt="Figure 1">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 1.</b> Figure 1. Quadratic lower bound on J. The RCL cost J(x) = 1</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.15667_figures/2605.15667_fig2.jpg" alt="Figure 2">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 2.</b> Figure 2. Gibbs weights pk as a function of the target mean cost ⟨X⟩for the three- state example with costs X = (0, 0.5, 1), plotted over the positive-temperature range Emc ∈(0, 1</figcaption>
</figure>
</div>

**Summary.** This paper constructs a finite-state Gibbs ensemble theory by deriving the necessary cost function from a specific composition law governing observable ratios. It shows that this approach successfully reproduces the canonical ensemble structure, providing a unified mathematical bridge between ratio data, information geometry, and statistical mechanics.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides a powerful, mathematically rigorous alternative to standard maximum-entropy methods by grounding the statistical mechanics in the algebraic structure of observed ratios, which is highly relevant for analyzing non-equilibrium or constrained physical systems.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper develops an alternative framework for deriving the finite-state Gibbs distribution by starting from a ratio-cost construction, bypassing the traditional reliance on explicit Hamiltonian definitions.

**Main result.** It establishes that the Recognition Composition Law (RCL), when calibrated by unit log-curvature, yields the cost function $J(x) = rac{1}{2}(x+x^{-1}) - 1$, which successfully connects ratio data to the canonical ensemble via entropy maximization.

**Method.** The methodology involves analyzing a multiplicative-reciprocal functional equation (the composition law) in log-coordinates, solving it using d'Alembert's equation, and then applying multinomial counting and convex duality to recover the Gibbs weights.

**Model / system.** The model is a finite-state system where the input data consists of dimensionless ratios $\{r_\omega\}$ (e.g., equilibrium constants or transition rate ratios). The framework is general but restricted to these finite, strictly positive outcome spaces.

**Key observables.** Gibbs weights ($p_\omega$), Recognition Free Energy ($F_R$), and the $J$-cost divergence ($D_J(q\|p)$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** The mean-cost constraint $\langle X angle_{	ext{target}}$ and the resulting inverse temperature $eta$ (or $T_R = 1/eta$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The framework is conditional on axioms (A1)–(A3), the adoption of the RCL, and the restriction to finite outcome spaces with strictly positive weights.

**Figures summary.** Figures compare the derived RCL cost $J(x)$ against its quadratic surrogate $(\log x)^2/2$, showing the divergence for large ratios. Other figures illustrate the convergence of Gibbs weights to the uniform measure as the mean cost constraint approaches a specific limit.

**Paper structure.** The paper moves from establishing the mathematical structure (the composition law and the resulting cost $J(x)$) to unifying the derivation chain $\{r_\omega\} 	o J 	o X 	o (	ext{Gibbs, FR, DKL})$, and finally applying this framework to test cases, including irrational cost structures.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

On a finite outcome space, the canonical Gibbs distribution is usually obtained by maximizing Shannon entropy at fixed mean of an externally supplied energy functional. This paper studies the finite-state consequences of a ratio-cost construction instead: after adopting the normalized d'Alembert degree-two closure called the Recognition Composition Law (RCL), with unit log-curvature calibration at the reference ratio, the continuous nontrivial positive branch is $J(x)=\tfrac12(x+x^{-1})-1=\cosh(\log x)-1$. Given the induced cost vector $X_ω=J(r_ω)$, multinomial counting and convex duality recover the finite-state Gibbs weights and the identity $F_{\mathrm{R}}(q)-F_{\mathrm{R}}(p)=T_{\mathrm{R}}\,D_{\mathrm{KL}}(q\Vert p)$; the entropy-maximization steps are classical once the cost is fixed. New technical content includes a non-asymptotic Stirling bound and soft-shell constrained-type theorems for real-valued costs. A three-state example compares the Gibbs law to squared-log, affinity-as-energy, and Tsallis alternatives at the same cost vector and mean-cost constraint, with sample-size power calculations at fixed RCL ground truth. The framework is conditional on axioms (A1)--(A3) and restricted to finite outcome spaces with strictly positive weights; it does not derive the composition law from a more primitive principle.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

### [Boundary anomalous dimensions from BCFT: $φ^{3}$ theories with a boundary and higher-derivative generalizations](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.16119v1)

**Summary.** This theoretical paper calculates the boundary anomalous dimensions and correlation function coefficients for $\phi^3$ theories in the presence of a boundary, extending the analysis to higher-derivative generalizations. It employs advanced CFT techniques, notably combining conformal multiplet recombination with boundary crossing symmetry, to determine critical exponents for models relevant to statistical physics, such as the Potts model.

**Why it may be interesting.** The rigorous application of CFT techniques like boundary crossing symmetry to determine critical exponents in interacting quantum field theories provides deep insights into universality classes relevant to critical phenomena in condensed matter systems.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper determines the boundary anomalous dimensions and operator expansion coefficients for $\phi^3$ theories with a boundary, and generalizes these results to higher-derivative theories.

**Main result.** Specific formulas are derived for the anomalous dimensions ($\hat{\gamma}_\Phi$) and one-point function coefficients for both the single-field (Yang-Lee) and multi-field (Potts) cases, including generalizations to higher-derivative interactions.

**Method.** The analysis combines the conformal multiplet recombination method with the boundary crossing symmetry applied to the bulk two-point correlator, often utilizing an $\epsilon$-expansion.

**Model / system.** The primary models are $\phi^3$ deformations of free boundary conformal field theories (BCFT). Specific physical realizations include the Yang-Lee model (related to branched polymers) and the $(N+1)$-state Potts model (related to percolation).

**Key observables.** Boundary anomalous dimensions ($\hat{\gamma}_\Phi$), boundary operator expansion coefficients ($\mu_\Phi$), and bulk one-point function coefficients ($a_O$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** The analysis is performed in the $\epsilon$ expansion, and results depend on the coupling constants and boundary condition parameters ($\sigma_0, \sigma_1$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The calculations rely on the $\epsilon 	o 0$ limit and the assumption that certain contributions (like the bulk identity contribution) do not receive corrections at low orders in the expansion parameter $\xi$.

**Figures summary.** The notes reference tables summarizing main results for various $\phi^3$ CFTs and provide complex mathematical equations detailing the derived parameters and correlation functions.

**Paper structure.** The paper systematically analyzes the problem by first establishing the general framework using multiplet recombination and boundary crossing symmetry. It then applies this framework to specific models, such as the Yang-Lee and Potts models, before generalizing the results to higher-derivative theories ($\phi \Box^k \phi$) and $\phi^{2n+1}$ theories.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

We consider the bulk $φ^3$ deformation of the free boundary conformal field theory in the $ε$ expansion. We determine the leading corrections to the scaling dimensions of boundary fundamental operators and some boundary operator expansion coefficients. Our procedure combines the conformal multiplet recombination with the boundary crossing symmetry. The results cover both the single field case and the multi-field case with $S_{N+1}$ global symmetry, which are associated with the Yang-Lee model and the $(N+1)$-state Potts model respectively. These semi-infinite models describe branched polymers, percolation, and spanning forest at a surface. We generalize these results to some higher derivative theories. In addition, we study the $φ^{2n+1}$ theories with $n>1$, but only obtain some boundary operator expansion coefficients.

</details>

### [Charge-sensitive vibrational modes in BEDT-TTF salts: Signatures of charge ordering and site charge](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.16012v1)

**Summary.** This paper analyzes how charge ordering in BEDT-TTF organic conductors manifests in the vibrational spectra, specifically using C=C stretching modes. It establishes quantitative relationships between frequency shifts and charge imbalance, but critically warns that structural disorder limits the technique to measuring charge *ordering* rather than absolute site charge.

**Why it may be interesting.** The work bridges solid-state electronic structure (charge ordering) with measurable spectroscopic fingerprints (vibrational modes), providing an experimental handle on strongly correlated phenomena in molecular solids.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study aims to understand the origin of various ground states (Mott, charge ordered, superconducting) in BEDT-TTF organic conductors by determining the precise charge distribution on the molecular sites in the insulating state.

**Main result.** While charge-frequency relations provide clear signatures for charge ordering ($\sim 141 	ext{ cm}^{-1}/	ext{e}$ for $
u_{27}(b_{1u})$), the large frequency spread ($\Deltaho \approx (\pm 0.045)e$) indicates that these relations are limited to estimating charge-ordering rather than determining absolute site charge.

**Method.** The research combines experimental vibrational spectroscopy (IR and Raman) to measure C=C stretching modes with theoretical analysis to establish linear relationships between frequency shifts and charge imbalance ($\deltaho$).

**Model / system.** The system under investigation is BEDT-TTF-based organic conductors, particularly those in the nominally $	ext{BEDT-TTF}^{+0.5}$ state, which exhibit complex ground states governed by electron-electron interactions.

**Key observables.** C=C stretching vibrational modes ($
u_{27}(b_{1u})$ and $
u_2(a_g)$), frequency shifts ($\delta
u$), and the resulting charge imbalance ($\deltaho$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** The charge imbalance ($\deltaho$) is the key parameter, with characteristic shifts of $141 	ext{ cm}^{-1}/	ext{e}$ and $98 	ext{ cm}^{-1}/	ext{e}$ observed for the respective modes.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The primary limitation is that structural variations cause a large uncertainty in the measured frequency spread, hindering the determination of absolute site charge values.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the dependence of $	ext{C=C}$ stretching modes on ionicity and show histograms of measured frequencies for $	ext{BEDT-TTF}^{+0.5}$ salts, highlighting the observed spectral spread.

**Paper structure.** The paper surveys the relevant vibrational modes, establishes empirical linear relationships between frequency shifts and charge, analyzes data from various charge-ordered salts, and concludes by defining the scope and limitations of using these spectroscopic techniques.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

BEDT-TTF-based organic conductors host a number of ground states, tuned by electron repulsion from Mott and charge ordered insulators to superconductors. Knowing charge distribution on the molecular sites in the insulating state of these materials is a key to understanding the origin of these ground states. We survey and discuss the C=C stretching modes in BEDT-TTF based molecular conductors. These molecular vibrations are extremely crucial in characterization of charge-ordered insulators, and are recently linked to superconductivity in some compounds. Focusing on the known examples of BEDT-TTF$^{+0.5}$ salts, we analyse the reliability of the C=C stretching modes for the determination of charge ordering and absolute site charge. Considering the charge-ordered states, a prominent shift in frequency of 141 cm$^{-1}$ per elementary charge $e$ for $ν_{27}(b_{1u})$ and 98 cm$^{-1}$$e$ for $ν_2$($a_g$) can be clearly realised, however, the distribution resulting from different compounds span over 20 cm$^{-1}$. For nominal BEDT-TTF$^{+0.5}$ compounds, the distribution of the resonance also extends around 20 cm$^{-1}$, yielding an unexpected large uncertainty of $Δρ~\approx~(~\pm~0.045)e$, which is presumably due to the influence of small differences in the structure. This highlights the limitations of charge-frequency relations to detect small deviations in absolute charge values on molecular lattice sites, and emphasises on the use of the relations to estimate charge-ordering, rather than absolute site charge.

</details>

<a id="paper-2605.15348"></a>
### [Clifford-deformed zero-rate LDPC codes with 50% biased noise thresholds](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.15348v1)

**Authors:** Jagannath Das, Sayandip Dhara, Pedro Medina, Arthur Pesah, Arpit Dua  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.15348v1>

**Summary.** This work establishes the theoretical conditions under which Clifford-deformed zero-rate LDPC codes can achieve a 50% code-capacity threshold when subjected to pure dephasing noise. By analyzing the scaling of logical operators, the authors unify the understanding of several known high-threshold codes. The analysis extends to finite-bias and circuit-level noise, providing practical guidelines for hardware implementation.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides a powerful, general theoretical framework linking code structure (LDPC, Clifford deformation) directly to a specific, physically relevant noise threshold (50% for dephasing), which is crucial for designing fault-tolerant quantum memories.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper aims to derive general conditions for zero-rate quantum LDPC codes to achieve a 50% code-capacity threshold under i.i.d. pure dephasing noise.

**Main result.** The 50% threshold is provably achievable for LDPC codes if the number of pure-Z logical operators grows subexponentially or if a non-overlapping basis of logical operators exists.

**Method.** The authors use analytical bounds, including a union-bound criterion and a sharper bound based on the Basis of Logical Operators (BLO), alongside numerical simulations for finite-bias and circuit-level noise.

**Model / system.** The study focuses on Clifford-deformed variants of zero-rate LDPC codes, which are subjected to anisotropic noise dominated by pure dephasing (Z errors).

**Key observables.** Code-capacity threshold (approaching 50%), logical failure probability decay, and residual bias in circuit-level noise.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Pure dephasing noise probability ($p_Z$), code rate (zero-rate limit), and the scaling of logical operators vs. distance.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis relies on the existence of specific structures within the logical operator space (e.g., non-overlapping BLO) and often assumes the infinite-bias limit for analytical proofs.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate phase diagrams showing regions of 50% thresholds for random deformations, and performance plots comparing thresholds for different structured deformations (Linear, TI, XY).

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses from establishing the general theoretical conditions for the 50% threshold using bounds, to applying these conditions to concrete examples like tile codes, and finally extending the analysis to finite-bias and circuit-level noise simulations.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Applying single-qubit Clifford unitaries to a Pauli stabilizer code produces a Clifford-deformed variant whose stabilizers remain Pauli operators, but with locally rotated Pauli axes. Such deformations provide a simple way to tailor a fixed code to anisotropic noise, and have enabled unusually high thresholds under strongly biased dephasing. In this work, we discuss zero-rate quantum low-density parity-check (LDPC) codes, for which there exist Clifford-deformed variants where the number of biased logical operators scales slower than the distance, or there exists a basis of logical operators whose overlap satisfies certain scaling conditions; in this case, the code-capacity threshold for the Clifford-deformed variant under i.i.d. pure dephasing noise approaches 50%. This property provably explains previously known code examples with 50% biased noise thresholds, such as XY surface code, XZZX surface code, color code, as well as some 3D Clifford-deformed codes. As a concrete new example, we study Clifford deformations of the tile codes of Ref. [1]. Similar to the phase diagram of 50% thresholds for random Clifford deformations of the surface code in Ref. [2], we find a similar phase diagram for the tile codes. We also construct several translationally invariant deformations of the tile code with 50% thresholds, and present numerical evidence for improved performance at finite bias and under circuit-level noise. In the circuit-level setting, performance is governed by the residual bias after a full syndrome-extraction cycle, linking our simulations to phenomenological models commonly used to study Clifford-deformed codes. We estimate this residual bias for different qubit platforms by modeling microscopic implementations of tile-code syndrome extraction.

</details>

### [Coherent States of Non-Null Torus Knots](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.15420v1)

**Authors:** Gabriel Canadas da Silva, Ion Vasile Vancea  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.15420v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks

<div class="figure-strip-hint">↔ Scroll figures horizontally</div>
<div class="paper-figures-scroll">
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.15420_figures/2605.15420_page2.jpg" alt="Figure 1">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 1.</b> Low-resolution page preview, page 2</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.15420_figures/2605.15420_page3.jpg" alt="Figure 2">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 2.</b> Low-resolution page preview, page 3</figcaption>

<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.15420_figures/2605.15420_page4.jpg" alt="Figure 3">

<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.15420_figures/2605.15420_page5.jpg" alt="Figure 4">

<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.15420_figures/2605.15420_page6.jpg" alt="Figure 5">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 5.</b> Low-resolution page preview, page 6</figcaption>
</figure>
</div>

**Summary.** This paper develops a quantum description for electromagnetic fields that possess the topology of non-null torus knots. By constructing specialized coherent states, the authors calculate the expectation values of observables like energy and helicity. The results successfully demonstrate a quantum-classical correspondence, showing that these quantum expectation values are determined entirely by the integer parameters defining the underlying classical knot structure.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work bridges topological field theory (knots) with quantum optics by showing how topological invariants dictate the expectation values of fundamental quantum observables like energy and helicity in a vacuum state.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper constructs a quantum description, using coherent states, for classical non-null torus knot solutions of Maxwell's equations in vacuum.

**Main result.** The expectation values of quantum field observables (energy, helicity, Poynting vector) in the constructed coherent state exactly reproduce the values predicted by the classical knotted electromagnetic fields, confirming quantum-classical correspondence.

**Method.** The authors use the general framework of coherent states, defined via displacement operators, to calculate expectation values of field operators in the knotted state.

**Model / system.** The system is the quantized electromagnetic field in vacuum, specifically modeling fields exhibiting non-null torus knot topologies, parameterized by integers $(n, m, l, s)$.

**Key observables.** Field expectation values ($\langle \hat{\mathbf{E}} angle, \langle \hat{\mathbf{B}} angle$), energy density, Poynting vector, helicity ($\langle \hat{\Lambda} angle$), and photon number ($\langle \hat{N} angle$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** The integer parameters $(n, m, l, s)$ characterizing the classical knot structure, and the characteristic length scale $L_0$.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis is performed in the Coulomb gauge ($
abla \cdot \mathbf{A} = 0$) and relies on the general relation between classical fields and coherent state amplitudes.

**Paper structure.** The paper proceeds by defining the coherent state using displacement operators derived from the classical field solutions, calculating the expectation values of key observables like energy and helicity in terms of the knot parameters, and confirming the quantum-classical correspondence.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

We construct coherent states for the quantized electromagnetic field that correspond to the classical non-null torus knot solutions of Maxwell's equations in vacuum. We derive the displacement operators from the general relation between classical fields and coherent state amplitudes and verify the defining properties of coherent states through direct computation. We determine the observables of the model: field expectation values, energy density, Poynting vector, helicity, photon number, quadrature uncertainties, and correlation functions, and calculate their expectation values in the knotted coherent states in terms of the integer parameters $(n,m,l,s)$ of the classical solutions. As an example, we particularize the construction in the case of the Hopfion coherent state. These results establish the quantum-classical correspondence for this type of vacuum topological electromagnetic systems.

</details>

**Summary.** This theoretical work rigorously compares the algebraic structures of the Ising minimal model and the $SU(2)_2$ Chern-Simons theory. By analyzing tensor product decompositions and fusion rules, the authors resolve apparent mathematical contradictions, confirming that the two descriptions yield consistent physical observables for Ising anyons.

**Why it may be interesting.** The deep connection between topological quantum field theories (Chern-Simons) and exactly solvable models (CFTs) is fundamental to understanding topological quantum computation, which is a key area of theoretical physics.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper resolves apparent algebraic contradictions between the fusion/braiding rules of the Ising minimal model and the representation theory of the $SU(2)_2$ Chern-Simons theory.

**Main result.** The tensor product decomposition rules for the quantum algebra $U_q(\mathfrak{sl}_2)$ at roots of unity successfully reproduce the known fusion and braiding rules of the Ising anyons, confirming the equivalence at the level of observables.

**Method.** The authors compare results derived from two formalisms: Operator Product Expansions (OPEs) for the CFT side and the representation theory of the quantum group $U_q(\mathfrak{sl}_2)$ for the Chern-Simons side.

**Model / system.** The study concerns the mathematical equivalence between the Ising minimal model $\mathcal{M}(4,3)$ (a 2D CFT with $c=1/2$) and the $SU(2)_2$ Chern-Simons theory, which describes Ising anyons.

**Key observables.** Wilson loop expectation values $\langle W_K^R angle$, fusion matrices ($F$), and braiding matrices ($R$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** The deformation parameter $q$ is set to a root of unity, specifically $q = \exp(\pi i/4)$.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The comparison is restricted to low-degree tensor products, and the resolution of contradictions relies on the fact that representations with zero quantum dimension do not contribute to observable quantities.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the Ising fusion rules, the structure of the 52 knot in different closures, and the transition matrices ($F^{\otimes\otimes\otimes}$) governing tensor product decompositions.

**Paper structure.** The paper proceeds by establishing the algebraic structures for both theories, calculating specific observables (like Wilson loops), and systematically comparing the tensor product decompositions ($\otimes^{\otimes\otimes\otimes}$) to show consistency.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

The present work is motivated by the statement that the Ising minimal model $\mathcal{M}(4,3)$ is equivalent, at the level of observables, to the $SU(2)_2$ Chern--Simons theory. At first glance, however, these two theories appear to differ substantially. For instance, the number of irreducible highest-weight representations does not match the number of Ising anyons. For tensor products of low degree, these discrepancies are examined in this work. While representation structure differs, it does not affect the observables underlying topological quantum computation algorithms.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2605.16248"></a>
### [Local Softmax and Global Weights in Non-Boolean Event Structures](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.16248v1)

**Authors:** Karl Svozil  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.16248v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks

<div class="figure-strip-hint">↔ Scroll figures horizontally</div>
<div class="paper-figures-scroll">
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.16248_figures/2605.16248_fig1.jpg" alt="Figure 1">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 1.</b> FIG. 1. Pentagon logic in the notation of Sec. VII. The cyclic atoms a1, . . . , a5 are intertwining atoms, each shared by two adjacent contexts. The atoms x1, . . . , x5 are context- specific. Each colored broken line represents one context Ci = {ai, ai+1, xi}, with indices understood modulo 5.</figcaption>
</figure>
</div>

**Summary.** This theoretical paper analyzes probability assignments on complex, overlapping logical structures called non-Boolean event structures. It shows that when local normalization rules, such as Softmax, are forced to be globally consistent across shared contexts, the rule merely parameterizes the set of all mathematically admissible weights. The key finding is that the interesting physics—the existence of weights beyond classical or quantum bounds—is determined by the structure itself, not the specific normalizing function used.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides a rigorous mathematical framework for understanding when local probabilistic assumptions (like those used in machine learning or cognitive models) are compatible with global physical constraints, separating the mathematical machinery (Softmax) from the underlying physical constraints (the polytope $W(L)$).

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper investigates the mathematical relationship between local normalization rules (like Softmax) applied in overlapping contexts and the requirement for a single, globally consistent probability weight in non-Boolean event structures.

**Main result.** Imposing global consistency (single-valuedness) forces the generalized softmax rule to merely parameterize the set of admissible weights, demonstrating that the softmax function itself does not introduce new physics beyond the structural constraints of the event structure.

**Method.** The authors use abstract algebraic and geometric methods to analyze the polytope of admissible weights, comparing local normalization schemes against global consistency requirements.

**Model / system.** The model is a non-Boolean event structure $L=(A, M)$, where $A$ is a set of atoms and $M$ is a family of overlapping contexts. The analysis focuses on characterizing the set of admissible probability weights $W(L)$ within this structure.

**Key observables.** Admissible weights $p \in W(L)$, the classical polytope $C(L)$, the quantum polytope $Q(L)$, and exotic weights that violate these bounds.

**Important parameters / regimes.** The link function $g$ used in the generalized softmax, and the structure of the context overlaps, particularly odd cycles (e.g., $n=5$ pentagon).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The core assumption is the requirement of single-valuedness or 'no-disturbance' across shared atoms, which restricts the general softmax framework.

**Figures summary.** The figures illustrate the geometry of probability assignments on odd cycles (like the pentagon), showing how the admissible half-weight violates both classical and quantum bounds.

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses by first defining the problem in terms of local vs. global normalization, then establishing the equivalence between single-valued softmax and parametrization of $W(L)$, and finally using odd cycles to demonstrate the existence of exotic, non-classical weights.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Softmax and related normalized response functions are widely used in choice theory, machine learning, and cognitive science. In non-Boolean event structures with overlapping contexts, however, local normalization does not automatically yield a global probability weight. We show that imposing single-valuedness on shared atoms -- equivalently, no-disturbance or consistent connectedness -- collapses generalized softmax rules to coordinate parametrizations of the strictly positive part of the admissible-weight polytope. Any strictly positive admissible weight can be represented in this way, while boundary weights arise as limits. Exotic weights that exceed classical or quantum bounds are therefore properties of the event structure and the chosen weight, not of the normalizing link. The resulting hierarchy separates local normalization, cross-context gluing, Cauchy--Gleason linearity, and physical or cognitive realizability.

</details>

**Summary.** This work analyzes how conductance fluctuations behave in disordered quantum conductors by examining their multifractal and ergodic properties. It finds a clear transition from non-ergodic to ergodic dynamics as disorder increases. Crucially, it distinguishes the physical origin of multifractality, showing it stems from temporal correlations in the weak disorder limit and from non-Gaussian probability distributions in the strong disorder limit.

**Why it may be interesting.** The findings provide a detailed understanding of how fundamental concepts like ergodicity and multifractality manifest in quantum transport, offering insights into the role of rare events and non-Gaussian statistics in disordered quantum systems.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study investigates the multifractal and ergodic properties of conductance fluctuations in disordered mesoscopic systems.

**Main result.** The system undergoes a transition from non-ergodic to ergodic behavior as disorder increases, with multifractality arising from temporal correlations in the non-ergodic regime and non-Gaussian PDF effects in the ergodic regime.

**Method.** The analysis employs Multifractal Detrended Fluctuation Analysis (MF-DFA) on a fictitious time series of conductance, comparing results with shuffled time series to distinguish correlation sources.

**Model / system.** The model is a two-dimensional (2D) tight-binding lattice subjected to Anderson disorder via on-site potentials. Transport is analyzed through the conductance, calculated using the Landauer-Büttiker formalism.

**Key observables.** Conductance correlation function, generalized Hurst exponent h(q), singularity spectrum f(alpha), fictitious correlation time $\epsilon$, and non-Gaussian indicator $\eta$.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Disorder strength ($U$), which controls the transition between non-ergodic and ergodic regimes.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis treats conductance as a function of a control parameter (like Fermi energy) to generate a time series for fluctuation analysis.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the transition from non-ergodic to ergodic behavior via correlation function decay, and show how multifractal scaling parameters ($\Delta\alpha$, $h(q)$) change with disorder strength $U$ for both original and shuffled time series.

**Paper structure.** The paper systematically analyzes the conductance fluctuations by first establishing the physical model and observables, then applying multifractal analysis to characterize the scaling properties in different disorder regimes, culminating in a discussion of the physical origin of the observed multifractality.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Understanding the stochastic properties of conductance fluctuations in disordered mesoscopic systems is fundamental to quantum transport. In this work, we investigate the multifractal and ergodic properties of the fictitious time series of conductance in two-dimensional tight-binding models under varying Anderson disorder. Using standard multifractal analysis, we show that conductance fluctuations exhibit a transition from non-ergodic to ergodic behavior as the disorder strength increases, as evidenced by the decay of the conductance correlation function. Remarkably, multifractality persists in both regimes; however, it becomes insensitive to shuffling in the strong-disorder (ergodic) regime, suggesting that distributional effects dominate temporal organization. On the contrary, in the weakly disordered (non-ergodic) regime, long-range correlations play a significant role. These findings are robust against changes in lead geometry (asymmetric vs. symmetric). Our results provide new insights into the interplay between ergodicity, multifractality, and rare events in disordered quantum transport.

</details>

**Summary.** This work reports the first observation of a distinct, flat-bottom U-shaped energy gap in high-Tc nickelate thin films using STM/S. By analyzing the gap's temperature and magnetic field dependence, the authors argue that the material exhibits unconventional, strong-coupling superconductivity with a large gap, suggesting potential for high critical temperatures at ambient pressure.

**Why it may be interesting.** The observation of a large, unconventional superconducting gap ($\Delta$) and the strong-coupling ratio ($\gg$ BCS limit) in a nickelate system provides crucial experimental data for theories attempting to explain high-Tc superconductivity beyond the cuprate paradigm.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study aims to understand the underlying mechanism of high-Tc superconductivity in nickelate superconductors and characterize the nature of the superconducting energy gap.

**Main result.** The researchers reported the first observation of an energy-symmetric, flat-bottom U-shaped gap in the nickelate film, whose temperature and magnetic field dependence is consistent with a superconducting gap function.

**Method.** The study employed ultra-low temperature Scanning Tunneling Microscopy/Spectroscopy (STM/S) and electrical transport measurements on the thin films.

**Model / system.** The physical system is (La,Pr)3Ni2O7 thin films grown on a SrLaAlO4 substrate. The analysis probes the superconducting gap structure, which is compared against theoretical expectations for strong-coupling superconductivity.

**Key observables.** Energy-symmetric, flat-bottom U-shaped energy gap; zero residual density of states at the Fermi level; gap reduction under a c-axis magnetic field; U-shaped to V-shaped gap evolution with temperature.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Onset $T_c$ above 40 K; Gap size $\Delta \sim 41.6 	ext{ meV}$; Strong-coupling ratio $2\Delta/k_B T_c \approx 20$; Magnetic field $B = 14 	ext{ T}$.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The observed gap structure is assumed to be superconducting, ruling out alternative origins like Coulomb blockade, and the analysis relies on careful deconvolution of thermal broadening effects.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the U-shaped gap structure, its suppression by a perpendicular magnetic field, and the temperature evolution from U-shaped to V-shaped spectra.

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses by first establishing the superconducting transport properties, then using STM/S to observe the characteristic U-shaped gap at low temperatures, followed by analyzing the gap's evolution with temperature and magnetic field, and finally comparing the results to theoretical models of strong-coupling superconductivity.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

The discovery of high transition temperature (high-Tc) superconductivity in Ruddlesden-Popper (R-P) bilayer nickelates under high pressure has stimulated extensive work to understand the underlying mechanism and search for superconductors with higher Tc. The recent realization of superconductivity in R-P bilayer nickelate thin films with onset Tc above 40 K at ambient-pressure enables the use of a wide array of powerful experimental tools to investigate the unconventional high-Tc superconductivity in bilayer nickelates. Here, using ultra-low temperature scanning tunneling microscopy/spectroscopy (STM/S) and electrical transport study, we report the first successful observation of an energy-symmetric, flat-bottom U-shaped gap with zero residual density of states around the Fermi level in the high-Tc nickelate (La,Pr)3Ni2O7 thin film grown on SrLaAlO4 substrate. Before and after STM/S studies, transport measurements on the same sample reveal consistent superconducting behaviors showing zero resistance, with an onset Tc above 40 K and zero resistance Tc above 20 K. The tunneling spectra exhibit highly unconventional temperature evolution, characterized by a rapid filling of the U-shaped energy gap to a V-shaped gap as the temperature increases. Furthermore, the U-shaped energy gap is reduced under a c-axis magnetic field of 14 T. The energy-symmetric U-shaped gap, taken together with its dependence on magnetic field and temperature, is consistent with the behavior of a superconducting gap, suggesting a nodeless gap function at ultra-low temperatures. Our findings shed new lights on the nature of high-Tc superconductivity and provide an encouraging and thought-provoking hint for a local superconductivity with Tc above liquid nitrogen boiling temperature in nickelate superconductors at ambient or zero pressure.

</details>
